Introduction
The Ô¨Årst year graduate program in mathematics at the University of Chicago
consists of three three-quarter courses, in analysis, algebra, and topology. The Ô¨Årst
two quarters of the topology sequence focus on manifold theory and diÔ¨Äerential
geometry, including diÔ¨Äerential forms and, usually, a glimpse of de Rham cohomol-
ogy. The third quarter focuses on algebraic topology. I have been teaching the
third quarter oÔ¨Äand on since around 1970. Before that, the topologists, including
me, thought that it would be impossible to squeeze a serious introduction to al-
gebraic topology into a one quarter course, but we were overruled by the analysts
and algebraists, who felt that it was unacceptable for graduate students to obtain
their PhDs without having some contact with algebraic topology.
This raises a conundrum. A large number of students at Chicago go into topol-
ogy, algebraic and geometric. The introductory course should lay the foundations
for their later work, but it should also be viable as an introduction to the subject
suitable for those going into other branches of mathematics. These notes reÔ¨Çect
my eÔ¨Äorts to organize the foundations of algebraic topology in a way that caters
to both pedagogical goals. There are evident defects from both points of view. A
treatment more closely attuned to the needs of algebraic geometers and analysts
would include ÀáCech cohomology on the one hand and de Rham cohomology and
perhaps Morse homology on the other. A treatment more closely attuned to the
needs of algebraic topologists would include spectral sequences and an array of
calculations with them. In the end, the overriding pedagogical goal has been the
introduction of basic ideas and methods of thought.
Our understanding of the foundations of algebraic topology has undergone sub-
tle but serious changes since I began teaching this course. These changes reÔ¨Çect
in part an enormous internal development of algebraic topology over this period,
one which is largely unknown to most other mathematicians, even those working in
such closely related Ô¨Åelds as geometric topology and algebraic geometry. Moreover,
this development is poorly reÔ¨Çected in the textbooks that have appeared over this
period.
Let me give a small but technically important example. The study of gen-
eralized homology and cohomology theories pervades modern algebraic topology.
These theories satisfy the excision axiom. One constructs most such theories ho-
motopically, by constructing representing objects called spectra, and one must then
prove that excision holds. There is a way to do this in general that is no more dif-
Ô¨Åcult than the standard veriÔ¨Åcation for singular homology and cohomology. I Ô¨Ånd
this proof far more conceptual and illuminating than the standard one even when
specialized to singular homology and cohomology. (It is based on the approxima-
tion of excisive triads by weakly equivalent CW triads.) This should by now be a
1

2
INTRODUCTION
standard approach. However, to the best of my knowledge, there exists no rigorous
exposition of this approach in the literature, at any level.
More centrally, there now exist axiomatic treatments of large swaths of homo-
topy theory based on Quillen‚Äôs theory of closed model categories. While I do not
think that a Ô¨Årst course should introduce such abstractions, I do think that the ex-
position should give emphasis to those features that the axiomatic approach shows
to be fundamental. For example, this is one of the reasons, although by no means
the only one, that I have dealt with coÔ¨Åbrations, Ô¨Åbrations, and weak equivalences
much more thoroughly than is usual in an introductory course.
Some parts of the theory are dealt with quite classically. The theory of fun-
damental groups and covering spaces is one of the few parts of algebraic topology
that has probably reached deÔ¨Ånitive form, and it is well treated in many sources.
Nevertheless, this material is far too important to all branches of mathematics to
be omitted from a Ô¨Årst course. For variety, I have made more use of the funda-
mental groupoid than in standard treatments,1 and my use of it has some novel
features. For conceptual interest, I have emphasized diÔ¨Äerent categorical ways of
modeling the topological situation algebraically, and I have taken the opportunity
to introduce some ideas that are central to equivariant algebraic topology.
Poincar¬¥e duality is also too fundamental to omit. There are more elegant ways
to treat this topic than the classical one given here, but I have preferred to give the
theory in a quick and standard fashion that reaches the desired conclusions in an
economical way. Thus here I have not presented the truly modern approach that
applies to generalized homology and cohomology theories.2
The reader is warned that this book is not designed as a textbook, although
it could be used as one in exceptionally strong graduate programs. Even then, it
would be impossible to cover all of the material in detail in a quarter, or even in a
year. There are sections that should be omitted on a Ô¨Årst reading and others that
are intended to whet the student‚Äôs appetite for further developments. In practice,
when teaching, my lectures are regularly interrupted by (purposeful) digressions,
most often directly prompted by the questions of students. These introduce more
advanced topics that are not part of the formal introductory course: cohomology
operations, characteristic classes, K-theory, cobordism, etc., are often Ô¨Årst intro-
duced earlier in the lectures than a linear development of the subject would dictate.
These digressions have been expanded and written up here as sketches without
complete proofs, in a logically coherent order, in the last four chapters.
These
are topics that I feel must be introduced in some fashion in any serious graduate
level introduction to algebraic topology.
A defect of nearly all existing texts is
that they do not go far enough into the subject to give a feel for really substantial
applications: the reader sees spheres and projective spaces, maybe lens spaces, and
applications accessible with knowledge of the homology and cohomology of such
spaces. That is not enough to give a real feeling for the subject. I am aware that
this treatment suÔ¨Äers the same defect, at least before its sketchy last chapters.
Most chapters end with a set of problems. Most of these ask for computa-
tions and applications based on the material in the text, some extend the theory
and introduce further concepts, some ask the reader to furnish or complete proofs
1But see R. Brown‚Äôs book cited in ¬ß2 of the suggestions for further reading.
2That approach derives Poincar¬¥e duality as a consequence of Spanier-Whitehead and Atiyah
duality, via the Thom isomorphism for oriented vector bundles.

INTRODUCTION
3
omitted in the text, and some are essay questions which implicitly ask the reader
to seek answers in other sources. Problems marked ‚àóare more diÔ¨Écult or more
peripheral to the main ideas. Most of these problems are included in the weekly
problem sets that are an integral part of the course at Chicago. In fact, doing the
problems is the heart of the course. (There are no exams and no grades; students
are strongly encouraged to work together, and more work is assigned than a student
can reasonably be expected to complete working alone.) The reader is urged to try
most of the problems: this is the way to learn the material. The lectures focus on
the ideas; their assimilation requires more calculational examples and applications
than are included in the text.
I have ended with a brief and idiosyncratic guide to the literature for the reader
interested in going further in algebraic topology.
These notes have evolved over many years, and I claim no originality for most
of the material. In particular, many of the problems, especially in the more classical
chapters, are the same as, or are variants of, problems that appear in other texts.
Perhaps this is unavoidable: interesting problems that are doable at an early stage
of the development are few and far between. I am especially aware of my debts to
earlier texts by Massey, Greenberg and Harper, Dold, and Gray.
I am very grateful to John Greenlees for his careful reading and suggestions,
especially of the last three chapters. I am also grateful to Igor Kriz for his sugges-
tions and for trying out the book at the University of Michigan. By far my greatest
debt, a cumulative one, is to several generations of students, far too numerous to
name. They have caught countless infelicities and outright blunders, and they have
contributed quite a few of the details. You know who you are. Thank you.


CHAPTER 1
The fundamental group and some of its
applications
We introduce algebraic topology with a quick treatment of standard mate-
rial about the fundamental groups of spaces, embedded in a geodesic proof of the
Brouwer Ô¨Åxed point theorem and the fundamental theorem of algebra.
1. What is algebraic topology?
A topological space X is a set in which there is a notion of nearness of points.
Precisely, there is given a collection of ‚Äúopen‚Äù subsets of X which is closed under
Ô¨Ånite intersections and arbitrary unions. It suÔ¨Éces to think of metric spaces. In that
case, the open sets are the arbitrary unions of Ô¨Ånite intersections of neighborhoods
UŒµ(x) = {y|d(x, y) < Œµ}.
A function p : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is continuous if it takes nearby points to nearby points.
Precisely, p‚àí1(U) is open if U is open. If X and Y are metric spaces, this means
that, for any x ‚ààX and Œµ > 0, there exists Œ¥ > 0 such that p(UŒ¥(x)) ‚äÇUŒµ(p(x)).
Algebraic topology assigns discrete algebraic invariants to topological spaces
and continuous maps. More narrowly, one wants the algebra to be invariant with
respect to continuous deformations of the topology.
Typically, one associates a
group A(X) to a space X and a homomorphism A(p) : A(X) ‚àí‚ÜíA(Y ) to a map
p : X ‚àí‚ÜíY ; one usually writes A(p) = p‚àó.
A ‚Äúhomotopy‚Äù h : p ‚âÉq between maps p, q : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is a continuous map
h : X √ó I ‚àí‚ÜíY such that h(x, 0) = p(x) and h(x, 1) = q(x), where I is the unit
interval [0, 1]. We usually want p‚àó= q‚àóif p ‚âÉq, or some invariance property close
to this.
In oversimpliÔ¨Åed outline, the way homotopy theory works is roughly this.
(1) One deÔ¨Ånes some algebraic construction A and proves that it is suitably
homotopy invariant.
(2) One computes A on suitable spaces and maps.
(3) One takes the problem to be solved and deforms it to the point that step
2 can be used to solve it.
The further one goes in the subject, the more elaborate become the construc-
tions A and the more horrendous become the relevant calculational techniques.
This chapter will give a totally self-contained paradigmatic illustration of the basic
philosophy. Our construction A will be the ‚Äúfundamental group.‚Äù We will calcu-
late A on the circle S1 and on some maps from S1 to itself. We will then use the
computation to prove the ‚ÄúBrouwer Ô¨Åxed point theorem‚Äù and the ‚Äúfundamental
theorem of algebra.‚Äù

3. Dependence on the basepoint
For a path a : x ‚Üíy, deÔ¨Åne Œ≥[a] : œÄ1(X, x) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(X, y) by Œ≥[a][f] = [a¬∑f ¬∑a‚àí1].
It is easy to check that Œ≥[a] depends only on the equivalence class of a and is a
homomorphism of groups. For a path b : y ‚Üíz, we see that Œ≥[b ¬∑ a] = Œ≥[b] ‚ó¶Œ≥[a]. It
follows that Œ≥[a] is an isomorphism with inverse Œ≥[a‚àí1]. For a path b : y ‚Üíx, we
have Œ≥[b ¬∑ a][f] = [b ¬∑ a][f][(b ¬∑ a)‚àí1]. If the group œÄ1(X, x) happens to be Abelian,
which may or may not be the case, then this is just [f]. By taking b = (a‚Ä≤)‚àí1 for
another path a‚Ä≤ : x ‚Üíy, we see that, when œÄ1(X, x) is Abelian, Œ≥[a] is independent
of the choice of the path class [a]. Thus, in this case, we have a canonical way to
identify œÄ1(X, x) with œÄ1(X, y).
4. Homotopy invariance
For a map p : X ‚àí‚ÜíY , deÔ¨Åne p‚àó: œÄ1(X, x) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(Y, p(x)) by p‚àó[f] =
[p ‚ó¶f], where p ‚ó¶f is the composite of p with the loop f : I ‚àí‚ÜíX.

p‚àóis a homomorphism.
The identity map id : X ‚àí‚ÜíX induces the identity
homomorphism. For a map q : Y ‚àí‚ÜíZ, q‚àó‚ó¶p‚àó= (q ‚ó¶p)‚àó.
Now suppose given two maps p, q : X ‚àí‚ÜíY and a homotopy h : p ‚âÉq. We

CHAPTER 2
Categorical language and the van Kampen
theorem
We introduce categorical language and ideas and use them to prove the van
Kampen theorem. This method of computing fundamental groups illustrates the
general principle that calculations in algebraic topology usually work by piecing
together a few pivotal examples by means of general constructions or procedures.
1. Categories
Algebraic topology concerns mappings from topology to algebra.
Category
theory gives us a language to express this. We just record the basic terminology,
without being overly pedantic about it.
A category C consists of a collection of objects, a set C (A, B) of morphisms
(also called maps) between any two objects, an identity morphism idA ‚ààC (A, A)
for each object A (usually abbreviated id), and a composition law
‚ó¶: C (B, C) √ó C (A, B) ‚àí‚ÜíC (A, C)
for each triple of objects A, B, C. Composition must be associative, and identity
morphisms must behave as their names dictate:
h ‚ó¶(g ‚ó¶f) = (h ‚ó¶g) ‚ó¶f,
id ‚ó¶f = f,
and
f ‚ó¶id = f
whenever the speciÔ¨Åed composites are deÔ¨Åned. A category is ‚Äúsmall‚Äù if it has a set
of objects.
We have the category S of sets and functions, the category U of topological
spaces and continuous functions, the category G of groups and homomorphisms,
the category A b of Abelian groups and homomorphisms, and so on.
2. Functors
A functor F : C ‚àí‚ÜíD is a map of categories. It assigns an object F(A) of
D to each object A of C and a morphism F(f) : F(A) ‚àí‚ÜíF(B) of D to each
morphism f : A ‚àí‚ÜíB of C in such a way that
F(idA) = idF (A)
and
F(g ‚ó¶f) = F(g) ‚ó¶F(f).
More precisely, this is a covariant functor. A contravariant functor F reverses the
direction of arrows, so that F sends f : A ‚àí‚ÜíB to F(f) : F(B) ‚àí‚ÜíF(A) and
satisÔ¨Åes F(g ‚ó¶f) = F(f) ‚ó¶F(g).
A category C has an opposite category C op
with the same objects and with C op(A, B) = C (B, A). A contravariant functor
F : C ‚àí‚ÜíD is just a covariant functor C op ‚àí‚ÜíD.
For example, we have forgetful functors from spaces to sets and from Abelian
groups to sets, and we have the free Abelian group functor from sets to Abelian
groups.

functors. It consists of a morphism Œ±A : F(A) ‚àí‚ÜíG(A) for each object A of C
such that the following diagram commutes for each morphism f : A ‚àí‚ÜíB of C :
F(A)

Intuitively, the maps Œ±A are deÔ¨Åned in the same way for every A.
For example, if F : S ‚àí‚ÜíA b is the functor that sends a set to the free
Abelian group that it generates and U : A b ‚àí‚ÜíS is the forgetful functor that
sends an Abelian group to its underlying set, then we have a natural inclusion of
sets S ‚àí‚ÜíUF(S). The functors F and U are left adjoint and right adjoint to each
other, in the sense that we have a natural isomorphism
A b(F(S), A) ‚àº= S (S, U(A))
for a set S and an Abelian group A. This just expresses the ‚Äúuniversal property‚Äù
of free objects: a map of sets S ‚àí‚ÜíU(A) extends uniquely to a homomorphism of
groups F(S) ‚àí‚ÜíA. Although we won‚Äôt bother with a formal deÔ¨Ånition, the notion
of an adjoint pair of functors will play an important role later on.
Two categories C and D are equivalent if there are functors F : C ‚àí‚ÜíD and
G : D ‚àí‚ÜíC and natural isomorphisms FG ‚àí‚ÜíId and GF ‚àí‚ÜíId, where the Id
are the respective identity functors.
4. Homotopy categories and homotopy equivalences
Let T be the category of spaces X with a chosen basepoint x ‚ààX; its mor-
phisms are continuous maps X ‚àí‚ÜíY that carry the basepoint of X to the basepoint
of Y . The fundamental group speciÔ¨Åes a functor T ‚àí‚ÜíG , where G is the category
of groups and homomorphisms.
When we have a (suitable) relation of homotopy between maps in a category
C , we deÔ¨Åne the homotopy category hC to be the category with the same objects
as C but with morphisms the homotopy classes of maps. We have the homotopy
category hU of unbased spaces. On T , we require homotopies to map basepoint to
basepoint at all times t, and we obtain the homotopy category hT of based spaces.
The fundamental group is a homotopy invariant functor on T , in the sense that it
factors through a functor hT ‚àí‚ÜíG .
A homotopy equivalence in U is an isomorphism in hU . Less mysteriously, a
map f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is a homotopy equivalence if there is a map g : Y ‚àí‚ÜíX such that
both g ‚ó¶f ‚âÉid and f ‚ó¶g ‚âÉid. Working in T , we obtain the analogous notion of
a based homotopy equivalence. Functors carry isomorphisms to isomorphisms, so
we see that a based homotopy equivalence induces an isomorphism of fundamental
groups. The same is true, less obviously, for unbased homotopy equivalences.
Proposition. If f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is a homotopy equivalence, then
f‚àó: œÄ1(X, x) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(Y, f(x))
is an isomorphism for all x ‚ààX.

A space X is said to be contractible if it is homotopy equivalent to a point.
Corollary. The fundamental group of a contractible space is zero.
5. The fundamental groupoid
While algebraic topologists often concentrate on connected spaces with chosen
basepoints, it is valuable to have a way of studying fundamental groups that does
not require such choices. For this purpose, we deÔ¨Åne the ‚Äúfundamental groupoid‚Äù
Œ†(X) of a space X to be the category whose objects are the points of X and whose
morphisms x ‚àí‚Üíy are the equivalence classes of paths from x to y. Thus the set
of endomorphisms of the object x is exactly the fundamental group œÄ1(X, x).
The term ‚Äúgroupoid‚Äù is used for a category all morphisms of which are isomor-
phisms. The idea is that a group may be viewed as a groupoid with a single object.
Taking morphisms to be functors, we obtain the category G P of groupoids. Then
we may view Œ† as a functor U ‚àí‚ÜíG P.
There is a useful notion of a skeleton skC of a category C . This is a ‚Äúfull‚Äù
subcategory with one object from each isomorphism class of objects of C , ‚Äúfull‚Äù
meaning that the morphisms between two objects of skC are all of the morphisms
between these objects in C . The inclusion functor J : skC ‚àí‚ÜíC is an equivalence
of categories.
An inverse functor F : C ‚àí‚ÜískC is obtained by letting F(A)
be the unique object in skC that is isomorphic to A, choosing an isomorphism
Œ±A : A ‚àí‚ÜíF(A), and deÔ¨Åning F(f) = Œ±B ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶Œ±‚àí1
A
: F(A) ‚àí‚ÜíF(B) for a
morphism f : A ‚àí‚ÜíB in C . We choose Œ± to be the identity morphism if A is in
skC , and then FJ = Id; the Œ±A specify a natural isomorphism Œ± : Id ‚àí‚ÜíJF.
A category C is said to be connected if any two of its objects can be connected
by a sequence of morphisms. For example, a sequence A ‚Üê‚àíB ‚àí‚ÜíC connects
A to C, although there need be no morphism A ‚àí‚ÜíC. However, a groupoid C
is connected if and only if any two of its objects are isomorphic. The group of
endomorphisms of any object C is then a skeleton of C . Therefore the previous
paragraph specializes to give the following relationship between the fundamental
group and the fundamental groupoid of a path connected space X.
Proposition. Let X be a path connected space. For each point x ‚ààX, the
inclusion œÄ1(X, x) ‚àí‚ÜíŒ†(X) is an equivalence of categories.
Proof. We are regarding œÄ1(X, x) as a category with a single object x, and it
is a skeleton of Œ†(X).

If D is a set regarded as a discrete category (only identity morphisms), then
colimits and limits indexed on D are coproducts and products indexed on the set
D. Coproducts are disjoint unions in S or U , wedges (or one-point unions) in T ,
free products in G , and direct sums in A b. Products are Cartesian products in all
of these categories; more precisely, they are Cartesian products of underlying sets,
with additional structure. If D is the category displayed schematically as

then the limits indexed on D are called pullbacks or equalizers, respectively.
A given category may or may not have all colimits, and it may have some but
not others. A category is said to be cocomplete if it has all colimits, complete if it
has all limits. The categories S , U , T , G , and A b are complete and cocomplete.
If a category has coproducts and coequalizers, then it is cocomplete, and similarly
for completeness. The proof is a worthwhile exercise.
7. The van Kampen theorem
The following is a modern dress treatment of the van Kampen theorem. I should
admit that, in lecture, it may make more sense not to introduce the fundamental
groupoid and to go directly to the fundamental group statement. The direct proof
is shorter, but not as conceptual.
However, as far as I know, the deduction of
the fundamental group version of the van Kampen theorem from the fundamental
groupoid version does not appear in the literature in full generality. The proof well
illustrates how to manipulate colimits formally. We have used the van Kampen
theorem as an excuse to introduce some basic categorical language, and we shall
use that language heavily in our treatment of covering spaces in the next chapter.
Theorem (van Kampen). Let O = {U} be a cover of a space X by path
connected open subsets such that the intersection of Ô¨Ånitely many subsets in O is
again in O. Regard O as a category whose morphisms are the inclusions of subsets
and observe that the functor Œ†, restricted to the spaces and maps in O, gives a
diagram
Œ†|O : O ‚àí‚ÜíG P
of groupoids. The groupoid Œ†(X) is the colimit of this diagram. In symbols,
Œ†(X) ‚àº= colimU‚ààO Œ†(U).
Proof. We must verify the universal property. For a groupoid C and a map
Œ∑ : Œ†|O ‚àí‚ÜíC of O-shaped diagrams of groupoids, we must construct a map
ÀúŒ∑ : Œ†(X) ‚àí‚ÜíC of groupoids that restricts to Œ∑U on Œ†(U) for each U ‚ààO. On
objects, that is on points of X, we must deÔ¨Åne ÀúŒ∑(x) = Œ∑U(x) for x ‚ààU. This is
independent of the choice of U since O is closed under Ô¨Ånite intersections. If a path
f : x ‚Üíy lies entirely in a particular U, then we must deÔ¨Åne ÀúŒ∑[f] = Œ∑([f]). Again,
since O is closed under Ô¨Ånite intersections, this speciÔ¨Åcation is independent of the
choice of U if f lies entirely in more than one U. Any path f is the composite of
Ô¨Ånitely many paths fi, each of which does lie in a single U, and we must deÔ¨Åne ÀúŒ∑[f]
to be the composite of the ÀúŒ∑[fi]. Clearly this speciÔ¨Åcation will give the required
unique map ÀúŒ∑, provided that ÀúŒ∑ so speciÔ¨Åed is in fact well deÔ¨Åned. Thus suppose
that f is equivalent to g. The equivalence is given by a homotopy h : f ‚âÉg through
paths x ‚Üíy. We may subdivide the square I √ó I into subsquares, each of which
is mapped into one of the U. We may choose the subdivision so that the resulting
subdivision of I √ó{0} reÔ¨Ånes the subdivision used to decompose f as the composite
of paths fi, and similarly for g and the resulting subdivision of I √ó {1}. We see
that the relation [f] = [g] in Œ†(X) is a consequence of a Ô¨Ånite number of relations,
each of which holds in one of the Œ†(U). Therefore ÀúŒ∑([f]) = ÀúŒ∑([g]). This veriÔ¨Åes the
universal property and proves the theorem.
‚ñ°
The fundamental group version of the van Kampen theorem ‚Äúfollows formally.‚Äù
That is, it is an essentially categorical consequence of the version just proved.
Arguments like this are sometimes called proof by categorical nonsense.

Theorem (van Kampen). Let X be path connected and choose a basepoint
x ‚ààX.
Let O be a cover of X by path connected open subsets such that the
intersection of Ô¨Ånitely many subsets in O is again in O and x is in each U ‚ààO.

that the functor œÄ1(‚àí, x), restricted to the spaces and maps in O, gives a diagram

of groups. The group œÄ1(X, x) is the colimit of this diagram. In symbols,
œÄ1(X, x) ‚àº= colimU‚ààO œÄ1(U, x).
We proceed in two steps.
Lemma. The van Kampen theorem holds when the cover O is Ô¨Ånite.
Proof. This step is based on the nonsense above about skeleta of categories.
We must verify the universal property, this time in the category of groups. For a
group G and a map Œ∑ : œÄ1|O ‚àí‚ÜíG of O-shaped diagrams of groups, we must show
that there is a unique homomorphism ÀúŒ∑ : œÄ1(X, x) ‚àí‚ÜíG that restricts to Œ∑U on
œÄ1(U, x). Remember that we think of a group as a groupoid with a single object
and with the elements of the group as the morphisms. The inclusion of categories
J : œÄ1(X, x) ‚àí‚ÜíŒ†(X) is an equivalence. An inverse equivalence F : Œ†(X) ‚àí‚Üí

8. EXAMPLES OF THE VAN KAMPEN THEOREM
19
In fact, by the usual subdivision argument, any loop I ‚àí‚ÜíX and any equivalence
h : I √ó I ‚àí‚ÜíX between loops has image in some US .
This implies directly
that œÄ1(X, x), together with the homomorphisms œÄ1(US , x) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(X, x), has the
universal property that characterizes the claimed colimit. We claim next that
colimU‚ààO œÄ1(U, x) ‚àº= colimS ‚ààF œÄ1(US , x),
and this will complete the proof. Substituting in the colimit on the right, we have
colimS ‚ààF œÄ1(US , x) ‚àº= colimS ‚ààF colimU‚ààS œÄ1(U, x).
By a comparison of universal properties, this iterated colimit is isomorphic to the
single colimit
colim(U,S )‚àà(O,F) œÄ1(U, x).
Here the indexing category (O, F) has objects the pairs (U, S ) with U ‚ààS ;
there is a morphism (U, S ) ‚àí‚Üí(V, T ) whenever both U ‚äÇV and US ‚äÇUT .
A moment‚Äôs reÔ¨Çection on the relevant universal properties should convince the
reader of the claimed identiÔ¨Åcation of colimits: the system on the right diÔ¨Äers
from the system on the left only in that the homomorphisms œÄ1(U, x) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(V, x)
occur many times in the system on the right, each appearance making the same
contribution to the colimit. If we assume known a priori that colimits of groups
exist, we can formalize this as follows. We have a functor O ‚àí‚ÜíF that sends U to
the singleton set {U} and thus a functor O ‚àí‚Üí(O, F) that sends U to (U, {U}).
The functor œÄ1(‚àí, x) : O ‚àí‚ÜíG factors through (O, F), hence we have an induced
map of colimits
colimU‚ààO œÄ1(U, x) ‚àí‚Üícolim(U,S )‚àà(O,F) œÄ1(U, x).
Projection to the Ô¨Årst coordinate gives a functor (O, F) ‚àí‚ÜíO. Its composite with
œÄ1(‚àí, x) : O ‚àí‚ÜíG deÔ¨Ånes the colimit on the right, hence we have an induced map
of colimits
colim(U,S )‚àà(O,F) œÄ1(U, x) ‚àí‚ÜícolimU‚ààO œÄ1(U, x).
These maps are inverse isomorphisms.
‚ñ°
8. Examples of the van Kampen theorem
So far, we have only computed the fundamental groups of the circle and of
contractible spaces. The van Kampen theorem lets us extend these calculations.
We now drop notation for the basepoint, writing œÄ1(X) instead of œÄ1(X, x).
Proposition. Let X be the wedge of a set of path connected based spaces Xi,
each of which contains a contractible neighborhood Vi of its basepoint. Then œÄ1(X)
is the coproduct (= free product) of the groups œÄ1(Xi).
Proof. Let Ui be the union of Xi and the Vj for j Ã∏= i. We apply the van
Kampen theorem with O taken to be the Ui and their Ô¨Ånite intersections. Since
any intersection of two or more of the Ui is contractible, the intersections make no
contribution to the colimit and the conclusion follows.
‚ñ°
Corollary. The fundamental group of a wedge of circles is a free group with
one generator for each circle.

PROBLEMS
(1) Compute the fundamental group of the two-holed torus (the compact sur-
face of genus 2 obtained by sewing together two tori along the boundaries
of an open disk removed from each).
(2) The Klein bottle K is the quotient space of S1 √óI obtained by identifying
(z, 0) with (z‚àí1, 1) for z ‚ààS1. Compute œÄ1(K).
(3) ‚àóLet X = {(p, q)|p Ã∏= ‚àíq} ‚äÇSn √ó Sn. DeÔ¨Åne a map f : Sn ‚àí‚ÜíX by
f(p) = (p, p). Prove that f is a homotopy equivalence.
(4) Let C be a category that has all coproducts and coequalizers. Prove that
C is cocomplete (has all colimits). Deduce formally, by use of opposite
categories, that a category that has all products and equalizers is com-
plete.

CHAPTER 3
Covering spaces
We run through the theory of covering spaces and their relationship to fun-
damental groups and fundamental groupoids. This is standard material, some of
the oldest in algebraic topology. However, I know of no published source for the
use that we shall make of the orbit category O(œÄ1(B, b)) in the classiÔ¨Åcation of
coverings of a space B. This point of view gives us the opportunity to introduce
some ideas that are central to equivariant algebraic topology, the study of spaces
with group actions. In any case, this material is far too important to all branches
of mathematics to omit.
1. The deÔ¨Ånition of covering spaces
While the reader is free to think about locally contractible spaces, weaker con-
ditions are appropriate for the full generality of the theory of covering spaces. A
space X is said to be locally path connected if for any x ‚ààX and any neighbor-
hood U of x, there is a smaller neighborhood V of x each of whose points can be
connected to x by a path in U. This is equivalent to the seemingly more stringent
requirement that the topology of X have a basis consisting of path connected open
sets. In fact, if X is locally path connected and U is an open neighborhood of a
point x, then the set
V = {y | y can be connected to x by a path in U}
is a path connected open neighborhood of x that is contained in U. Observe that
if X is connected and locally path connected, then it is path connected. Through-
out this chapter, we assume that all given spaces are connected and locally path
connected.
Definition. A map p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is a covering (or cover, or covering space) if
it is surjective and if each point b ‚ààB has an open neighborhood V such that each
component of p‚àí1(V ) is open in E and is mapped homeomorphically onto V by p.
We say that a path connected open subset V with this property is a fundamental
neighborhood of B. We call E the total space, B the base space, and Fb = p‚àí1(b)
a Ô¨Åber of the covering p.
Any homeomorphism is a cover. A product of covers is a cover. The projection
R ‚àí‚ÜíS1 is a cover. Each fn : S1 ‚àí‚ÜíS1 is a cover. The projection Sn ‚àí‚ÜíRP n
is a cover, where the real projective space RP n is obtained from Sn by identifying
antipodal points. If f : A ‚àí‚ÜíB is a map (where A is connected and locally path
connected) and D is a component of the pullback of f along p, then p : D ‚àí‚ÜíA is
a cover.

COVERING SPACES
2. The unique path lifting property
The following result is abstracted from what we saw in the case of the particular
cover R ‚àí‚ÜíS1. It describes the behavior of p with respect to path classes and
fundamental groups.
Theorem (Unique path lifting). Let p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB be a covering, let b ‚ààB, and
let e, e‚Ä≤ ‚ààFb.
(i) A path f : I ‚àí‚ÜíB with f(0) = b lifts uniquely to a path g : I ‚àí‚ÜíE such
that g(0) = e and p ‚ó¶g = f.
(ii) Equivalent paths f ‚âÉf ‚Ä≤ : I ‚àí‚ÜíB that start at b lift to equivalent paths
g ‚âÉg‚Ä≤ : I ‚àí‚ÜíE that start at e, hence g(1) = g‚Ä≤(1).
(iii) p‚àó: œÄ1(E, e) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(B, b) is a monomorphism.
(iv) p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e‚Ä≤)) is conjugate to p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e)).
(v) As e‚Ä≤ runs through Fb, the groups p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e‚Ä≤)) run through all conjugates
of p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e)) in œÄ1(B, b).
Proof. For (i), subdivide I into subintervals each of which maps to a fun-
damental neighborhood under f, and lift f to g inductively by use of the pre-
scribed homeomorphism property of fundamental neighborhoods.
For (ii), let
h : I √óI ‚àí‚ÜíB be a homotopy f ‚âÉf ‚Ä≤ through paths b ‚àí‚Üíb‚Ä≤. Subdivide the square
into subsquares each of which maps to a fundamental neighborhood under f. Pro-
ceeding inductively, we see that h lifts uniquely to a homotopy H : I √óI ‚àí‚ÜíE such
that H(0, 0) = e and p ‚ó¶H = h. By uniqueness, H is a homotopy g ‚âÉg‚Ä≤ through
paths e ‚àí‚Üíe‚Ä≤, where g(1) = e‚Ä≤ = g‚Ä≤(1). Parts (iii)‚Äì(v) are formal consequences of
(i) and (ii), as we shall see in the next section.
‚ñ°
Definition. A covering p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is regular if p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e)) is a normal
subgroup of œÄ1(B, b). It is universal if E is simply connected.
As we shall explain in ¬ß4, for a universal cover p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB, the elements of
Fb are in bijective correspondence with the elements of œÄ1(B, b). We illustrate the
force of this statement.
Example. For n ‚â•2, Sn is a universal cover of RP n. Therefore œÄ1(RP n) has
only two elements. There is a unique group with two elements, and this proves our
earlier claim that œÄ1(RP n) = Z2.
3. Coverings of groupoids
Much of the theory of covering spaces can be recast conceptually in terms of
fundamental groupoids. This point of view separates the essentials of the topol-
ogy from the formalities and gives a convenient language in which to describe the
algebraic classiÔ¨Åcation of coverings.
Definition. (i) Let C be a category and x be an object of C . The category
x\C of objects under x has objects the maps f : x ‚àí‚Üíy in C ; for objects f : x ‚àí‚Üíy
and g : x ‚àí‚Üíz, the morphisms Œ≥ : f ‚àí‚Üíg in x\C are the morphisms Œ≥ : y ‚àí‚Üíz
in C such that Œ≥ ‚ó¶f = g : x ‚àí‚Üíz. Composition and identity maps are given by
composition and identity maps in C . When C is a groupoid, Œ≥ = g ‚ó¶f ‚àí1, and the
objects of x\C therefore determine the category.

3. COVERINGS OF GROUPOIDS
23
(ii) Let C be a small groupoid. DeÔ¨Åne the star of x, denoted St(x) or StC (x),
to be the set of objects of x\C , that is, the set of morphisms of C with source x.
Write C (x, x) = œÄ(C , x) for the group of automorphisms of the object x.
(iii) Let E and B be small connected groupoids. A covering p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is a
functor that is surjective on objects and restricts to a bijection
p : St(e) ‚àí‚ÜíSt(p(e))
for each object e of E . For an object b of B, let Fb denote the set of objects of E
such that p(e) = b. Then p‚àí1(St(b)) is the disjoint union over e ‚ààFb of St(e).
Parts (i) and (ii) of the unique path lifting theorem can be restated as follows.
Proposition. If p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is a covering of spaces, then the induced functor
Œ†(p) : Œ†(E) ‚àí‚ÜíŒ†(B) is a covering of groupoids.
Parts (iii), (iv), and (v) of the unique path lifting theorem are categorical
consequences that apply to any covering of groupoids, where they read as follows.
Proposition. Let p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB be a covering of groupoids, let b be an object
of B, and let e and e‚Ä≤ be objects of Fb.
(i) p : œÄ(E , e) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ(B, b) is a monomorphism.
(ii) p(œÄ(E , e‚Ä≤)) is conjugate to p(œÄ(E , e)).
(iii) As e‚Ä≤ runs through Fb, the groups p(œÄ(E, e‚Ä≤)) run through all conjugates
of p(œÄ(E , e)) in œÄ(B, b).
Proof. For (i), if g, g‚Ä≤ ‚ààœÄ(E , e) and p(g) = p(g‚Ä≤), then g = g‚Ä≤ by the injectivity
of p on St(e). For (ii), there is a map g : e ‚àí‚Üíe‚Ä≤ since E is connected. Conjugation
by g gives a homomorphism œÄ(E , e) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ(E , e‚Ä≤) that maps under p to conjugation
of œÄ(B, b) by its element p(g). For (iii), the surjectivity of p on St(e) gives that
any f ‚ààœÄ(B, b) is of the form p(g) for some g ‚ààSt(e). If e‚Ä≤ is the target of g, then
p(œÄ(E , e‚Ä≤)) is the conjugate of p(œÄ(E , e)) by f.
‚ñ°
The Ô¨Åbers Fb of a covering of groupoids are related by translation functions.
Definition. Let p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB be a covering of groupoids. DeÔ¨Åne the Ô¨Åber
translation functor T = T(p) : B ‚àí‚ÜíS as follows. For an object b of B, T(b) = Fb.
For a morphism f : b ‚àí‚Üíb‚Ä≤ of B, T(f) : Fb ‚àí‚ÜíFb‚Ä≤ is speciÔ¨Åed by T(f)(e) = e‚Ä≤,
where e‚Ä≤ is the target of the unique g in St(e) such that p(g) = f.
It is an exercise from the deÔ¨Ånition of a covering of a groupoid to verify that T
is a well deÔ¨Åned functor. For a covering space p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB and a path f : b ‚àí‚Üíb‚Ä≤,
T(f) : Fb ‚àí‚ÜíFb‚Ä≤ is given by T(f)(e) = g(1) where g is the path in E that starts
at e and covers f.
Proposition. Any two Ô¨Åbers Fb and Fb‚Ä≤ of a covering of groupoids have the
same cardinality. Therefore any two Ô¨Åbers of a covering of spaces have the same
cardinality.
Proof. For f : b ‚àí‚Üíb‚Ä≤, T(f) : Fb ‚àí‚ÜíFb‚Ä≤ is a bijection with inverse T(f ‚àí1).

COVERING SPACES
4. Group actions and orbit categories
The classiÔ¨Åcation of coverings is best expressed in categorical language that
involves actions of groups and groupoids on sets.
A (left) action of a group G on a set S is a function G √ó S ‚àí‚ÜíS such that
es = s (where e is the identity element) and (g‚Ä≤g)s = g‚Ä≤(gs) for all s ‚ààS. The
isotropy group Gs of a point s is the subgroup {g|gs = s} of G. An action is free if
gs = s implies g = e, that is, if Gs = e for every s ‚ààS.
The orbit generated by a point s is {gs|g ‚ààG}. An action is transitive if for
every pair s, s‚Ä≤ of elements of S, there is an element g of G such that gs = s‚Ä≤.
Equivalently, S consists of a single orbit. If H is a subgroup of G, the set G/H
of cosets gH is a transitive G-set. When G acts transitively on a set S, we obtain
an isomorphism of G-sets between S and the G-set G/Gs for any Ô¨Åxed s ‚ààS by
sending gs to the coset gGs.
The following lemma describes the group of automorphisms of a transitive
G-set S. For a subgroup H of G, let NH denote the normalizer of H in G and deÔ¨Åne
WH = NH/H. Such quotient groups WH are sometimes called Weyl groups.
Lemma. Let G act transitively on a set S, choose s ‚ààS, and let H = Gs.
Then WH is isomorphic to the group AutG(S) of automorphisms of the G-set S.
Proof. For n ‚ààNH with image ¬Øn ‚ààWH, deÔ¨Åne an automorphism œÜ(¬Øn) of
S by œÜ(¬Øn)(gs) = gns. For an automorphism œÜ of S, we have œÜ(s) = ns for some
n ‚ààG. For h ‚ààH, hns = œÜ(hs) = œÜ(s) = ns, hence n‚àí1hn ‚ààGs = H and
n ‚ààNH. Clearly œÜ = œÜ(¬Øn), and it is easy to check that this bijection between WH
and AutG(S) is an isomorphism of groups.
‚ñ°
We shall also need to consider G-maps between diÔ¨Äerent G-sets G/H.
Lemma. A G-map Œ± : G/H ‚àí‚ÜíG/K has the form Œ±(gH) = gŒ≥K, where the
element Œ≥ ‚ààG satisÔ¨Åes Œ≥‚àí1hŒ≥ ‚ààK for all h ‚ààH.
Proof. If Œ±(eH) = Œ≥K, then the relation
Œ≥K = Œ±(eH) = Œ±(hH) = hŒ±(eH) = hŒ≥K
implies that Œ≥‚àí1hŒ≥ ‚ààK for h ‚ààH.
‚ñ°
Definition. The category O(G) of canonical orbits has objects the G-sets
G/H and morphisms the G-maps of G-sets.
The previous lemmas give some feeling for the structure of O(G) and lead to
the following alternative description.
Lemma. The category O(G) is isomorphic to the category G whose objects are
the subgroups of G and whose morphisms are the distinct subconjugacy relations
Œ≥‚àí1HŒ≥ ‚äÇK for Œ≥ ‚ààG.
If we regard G as a category with a single object, then a (left) action of G on a
set S is the same thing as a covariant functor G ‚àí‚ÜíS . (A right action is the same
thing as a contravariant functor.) If B is a small groupoid, it is therefore natural
to think of a covariant functor T : B ‚àí‚ÜíS as a generalization of a group action.
For each object b of B, T restricts to an action of œÄ(B, b) on T(b). We say that
the functor T is transitive if this group action is transitive for each object b. If B
is connected, this holds for all objects b if it holds for any one object b.

5. THE CLASSIFICATION OF COVERINGS OF GROUPOIDS
25
For example, for a covering of groupoids p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB, the Ô¨Åber translation
functor T restricts to give an action of œÄ(B, b) on the set Fb. For e ‚ààFb, the
isotropy group of e is precisely p(œÄ(E , e)). That is, T(f)(e) = e if and only if the
lift of f to an element of St(e) is an automorphism of e. Moreover, the action
is transitive since there is an isomorphism in E connecting any two points of Fb.
Therefore, as a œÄ(B, b)-set,
Fb ‚àº= œÄ(B, b)/p(œÄ(E , e)).
Definition. A covering p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB of groupoids is regular if p(œÄ(E , e)) is a
normal subgroup of œÄ(B, b). It is universal if p(œÄ(E , e)) = {e}. Clearly a covering
space is regular or universal if and only if its associated covering of fundamental
groupoids is regular or universal.
A covering of groupoids is universal if and only if œÄ(B, b) acts freely on Fb, and
then Fb is isomorphic to œÄ(B, b) as a œÄ(B, b)-set. Specializing to covering spaces,
this sharpens our earlier claim that the elements of Fb and œÄ1(B, b) are in bijective
correspondence.

6. The construction of coverings of groupoids
We have given an algebraic classiÔ¨Åcation of all possible covers of B: there is
at most one isomorphism class of covers corresponding to each conjugacy class of
subgroups of œÄ(B, b). We show that all of these possibilities are actually realized.
Since this algebraic result is not needed in the proof of its topological analogue, we
shall not give complete details.
Theorem. Choose a base object b of B and let G = œÄ(B, b). There is a functor

Proof. The idea is that, up to bijection, StE (G/H)(e) must be the same set for
each H, but the nature of its points can diÔ¨Äer with H. At one extreme, E (G/G) =
B, p = id, e = b, and the set of morphisms from b to any other object b‚Ä≤ is a copy
of œÄ(B, b). At the other extreme, E (G/e) is a universal cover of B and there is
just one morphism from e to any other object e‚Ä≤. In general, the set of objects of
E (G/H) is deÔ¨Åned to be StB(b)/H, the coset of the identity morphism being e.
Here G and hence its subgroup H act from the right on StB(b) by composition in

The following remarks place the orbit category O(œÄ(B, b)) in perspective by
relating it to several other equivalent categories.
Remark. Consider the category S B of functors T : B ‚àí‚ÜíS and natural
transformations. Let G = œÄ(B, b). Regarding G as a category with one object b,
it is a skeleton of B, hence the inclusion G ‚äÇB is an equivalence of categories.
Therefore, restriction of functors T to G-sets T(b) gives an equivalence of categories
from S B to the category of G-sets. This restricts to an equivalence between the
respective subcategories of transitive objects. We have chosen to focus on transitive
objects since we prefer to insist that coverings be connected.
The inclusion of
the orbit category O(G) in the category of transitive G-sets is an equivalence of
categories because O(G) is a full subcategory that contains a skeleton. We could
shrink O(G) to a skeleton by choosing one H in each conjugacy class of subgroups
of G, but the resulting equivalent subcategory is a less natural mathematical object.
7. The classiÔ¨Åcation of coverings of spaces
In this section and the next, we shall classify covering spaces and their maps by
arguments precisely parallel to those for covering groupoids in the previous sections.
In fact, applied to the associated coverings of fundamental groupoids, some of the
algebraic results directly imply their topological analogues.
We begin with the
following result, which deserves to be called the fundamental theorem of covering
space theory and has many other applications. It asserts that the fundamental
group gives the only ‚Äúobstruction‚Äù to solving a certain lifting problem. Recall our
standing assumption that all given spaces are connected and locally path connected.
Theorem. Let p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB be a covering and let f : X ‚àí‚ÜíB be a continuous
map.
Choose x ‚ààX, let b = f(x), and choose e ‚ààFb.
There exists a map

assume that im(f‚àó) ‚äÇim(p‚àó). Applied to the covering Œ†(p) : Œ†(E) ‚àí‚ÜíŒ†(B), the
analogue for groupoids gives a functor Œ†(X) ‚àí‚ÜíŒ†(E) that restricts on objects to
the unique map g : X ‚àí‚ÜíE of sets such that g(x) = e and p‚ó¶g = f. We need only
check that g is continuous, and this holds because p is a local homeomorphism. In
detail, if y ‚ààX and g(y) ‚ààU, where U is an open subset of E, then there is a
smaller open neighborhood U ‚Ä≤ of g(y) that p maps homeomorphically onto an open

then g(W) ‚äÇU ‚Ä≤ by inspection of the deÔ¨Ånition of g.

8. The construction of coverings of spaces
We have now given an algebraic classiÔ¨Åcation of all possible covers of B: there
is at most one isomorphism class of covers corresponding to each conjugacy class
of subgroups of œÄ1(B, b). We show here that all of these possibilities are actually
realized. We shall Ô¨Årst construct universal covers and then show that the existence
of universal covers implies the existence of all other possible covers. Again, while
it suÔ¨Éces to think in terms of locally contractible spaces, appropriate generality
demands a weaker hypothesis. We say that a space B is semi-locally simply con-
nected if every point b ‚ààB has a neighborhood U such that œÄ1(U, b) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(B, b)
is the trivial homomorphism.
Theorem. If B is connected, locally path connected, and semi-locally simply
connected, then B has a universal cover.
Proof. Fix a basepoint b ‚ààB. We turn the properties of paths that must
hold in a universal cover into a construction. DeÔ¨Åne E to be the set of equivalence
classes of paths f in B that start at b and deÔ¨Åne p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB by p[f] = f(1).
Of course, the equivalence relation is homotopy through paths from b to a given
endpoint, so that p is well deÔ¨Åned. Thus, as a set, E is just StŒ†(B)(b), exactly
as in the construction of the universal cover of Œ†(B). The topology of B has a
basis consisting of path connected open subsets U such that œÄ1(U, u) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ1(B, u)
is trivial for all u ‚ààU. Since every loop in U is equivalent in B to the trivial loop,
any two paths u ‚àí‚Üíu‚Ä≤ in such a U are equivalent in B. We shall topologize E so
that p is a cover with these U as fundamental neighborhoods. For a path f in B
that starts at b and ends in U, deÔ¨Åne a subset U[f] of E by
U[f] = {[g] | [g] = [c ¬∑ f] for some c : I ‚àí‚ÜíU}.
The set of all such U[f] is a basis for a topology on E since if U[f] and U ‚Ä≤[f ‚Ä≤] are
two such sets and [g] is in their intersection, then
W[g] ‚äÇU[f] ‚à©U ‚Ä≤[f ‚Ä≤]
for any open set W of B such that p[g] ‚ààW ‚äÇU ‚à©U ‚Ä≤. For u ‚ààU, there is a unique
[g] in each U[f] such that p[g] = u. Thus p maps U[f] homeomorphically onto U
and, if we choose a basepoint u in U, then p‚àí1(U) is the disjoint union of those
U[f] such that f ends at u. It only remains to show that E is connected, locally
path connected, and simply connected, and the second of these is clear. Give E
the basepoint e = [cb]. For [f] ‚ààE, deÔ¨Åne a path Àúf : I ‚àí‚ÜíE by Àúf(s) = [fs], where
fs(t) = f(st); Àúf is continuous since each Àúf ‚àí1(U[g]) is open by the deÔ¨Ånition of U[g]
and the continuity of f. Since Àúf starts at e and ends at [f], E is path connected.
Since fs(1) = f(s), p ‚ó¶Àúf = f. Thus, by deÔ¨Ånition,
T[f](e) = [ Àúf(1)] = [f].
Restricting attention to loops f, we see that T[f](e) = e if and only if [f] = e as
an element of œÄ1(B, b). Thus the action of œÄ1(B, b) on Fb is free and the isotropy
group p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e)) is trivial.
‚ñ°
We shall construct general covers by passage to orbit spaces from the universal
cover, and we need some preliminaries.

Definition. A G-space X is a space X that is a G-set with continuous action
map G √ó X ‚àí‚ÜíX. DeÔ¨Åne the orbit space X/G to be the set of orbits {Gx|x ‚ààX}
with its topology as a quotient space of X.
The deÔ¨Ånition makes sense for general topological groups G.
However, our
interest here is in discrete groups G, for which the continuity condition just means
that action by each element of G is a homeomorphism. The functoriality on O(G) of
our construction of general covers will be immediate from the following observation.
Lemma. Let X be a G-space. Then passage to orbit spaces deÔ¨Ånes a functor
X/(‚àí) : O(G) ‚àí‚ÜíU .
Proof. The functor sends G/H to X/H and sends a map Œ± : G/H ‚àí‚ÜíG/K
to the map X/H ‚àí‚ÜíX/K that sends the coset Hx to the coset KŒ≥‚àí1x, where Œ±
is given by the subconjugacy relation Œ≥‚àí1HŒ≥ ‚äÇK.
‚ñ°
The starting point of the construction of general covers is the following descrip-
tion of regular covers and in particular of the universal cover.
Proposition. Let p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB be a cover such that Aut(E) acts transitively
on Fb. Then the cover p is regular and E/ Aut(E) is homeomorphic to B.
Proof. For any points e, e‚Ä≤ ‚ààFb, there exists g ‚ààAut(E) such that g(e) = e‚Ä≤
and thus p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e)) = p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e‚Ä≤)).
Therefore all conjugates of p‚àó(œÄ1(E, e))

CHAPTER 4
Graphs
We deÔ¨Åne graphs, describe their homotopy types, and use them to show that
a subgroup of a free group is free and that any group is the fundamental group of
some space.
1. The deÔ¨Ånition of graphs
We give the deÔ¨Ånition in a form that will later make it clear that a graph is
exactly a one-dimensional CW complex. Note that the zero-sphere S0 is a discrete
space with two points. We think of S0 as the boundary of I and so label the points
0 and 1.
Definition. A graph X is a space that is obtained from a (discrete) set X0
of points, called vertices, and a (discrete) set J of functions j : S0 ‚àí‚ÜíX0 as the
quotient space of the disjoint union X0‚®ø(J √óI) that is obtained by identifying (j, 0)
with j(0) and (j, 1) with j(1). The images of the intervals {j} √ó I are called edges.
A graph is Ô¨Ånite if it has only Ô¨Ånitely many vertices and edges or, equivalently, if
it is a compact space. A graph is locally Ô¨Ånite if each vertex is a boundary point
of only Ô¨Ånitely many edges or, equivalently, if it is a locally compact space. A
subgraph A of X is a graph A ‚äÇX with A0 ‚äÇX0. That is, A is the union of some
of the vertices and edges of X.
Observe that a graph is a locally contractible space: any neighborhood of any
point contains a contractible neighborhood of that point. Therefore a connected
graph has all possible covers.
2. Edge paths and trees
An oriented edge k : I ‚àí‚ÜíX in a graph X is the traversal of an edge in either
the forward or backward direction. An edge path is a Ô¨Ånite composite of oriented
edges kn with kn+1(0) = kn(1). Such a path is reduced if it is never the case that
kn+1 is kn with the opposite orientation. An edge path is closed if it starts and
ends at the same vertex (and is thus a loop).
Definition. A tree is a connected graph with no closed reduced edge paths.
A subspace A of a space X is a deformation retract if there is a homotopy
h : X √ó I ‚àí‚ÜíX such that h(x, 0) = x, h(a, t) = a, and h(x, 1) ‚ààA for all x ‚ààX,
a ‚ààA, and t ‚ààI. Such a homotopy is called a deformation of X onto A.
Lemma. Any vertex v0 of a tree T is a deformation retract of T.
Proof. This is true by induction on the number of edges when T is Ô¨Ånite since
we can prune the last branch. For the general case, observe that each vertex v lies
in some Ô¨Ånite connected subtree T(v) that also contains v0. Choose an edge path

36

a(v) : I ‚àí‚ÜíT(v) connecting v to v0. For an edge j from v to v‚Ä≤, T(v) ‚à™T(v‚Ä≤) ‚à™j
is a Ô¨Ånite connected subtree of T. On the square j √ó I, we deÔ¨Åne
h : j √ó I ‚àí‚ÜíT(v) ‚à™T(v‚Ä≤) ‚à™j

connectivity of T(v) ‚à™T(v‚Ä≤) ‚à™j. As j runs over the edges, these homotopies glue
together to specify a deformation h of T onto v0.
‚ñ°
A subtree of a graph X is maximal if it is contained in no strictly larger tree.
Lemma. If a tree T is a subgraph of a graph X, then T is contained in a
maximal tree. If X is connected, then a tree in X is maximal if and only if it
contains all vertices of X.
Proof. Since the union of an increasing family of trees in X is a tree, the
Ô¨Årst statement holds by Zorn‚Äôs lemma. If X is connected, then a tree containing
all vertices is maximal since addition of an edge would result in a subgraph that
contains a closed reduced edge path and, conversely, a tree T that does not contain
all vertices is not maximal since a vertex not in T can be connected to a vertex in
T by a reduced edge path consisting of edges not in T.
‚ñ°
3. The homotopy types of graphs
Graph theory is a branch of combinatorics. The homotopy theory of graphs is
essentially trivial, by the following result.
Theorem. Let X be a connected graph with maximal tree T. Then the quotient
space X/T is the wedge of one circle for each edge of X not in T, and the quotient
map q : X ‚àí‚ÜíX/T is a homotopy equivalence.
Proof. The Ô¨Årst clause is evident. The second is a direct consequence of a
later result (that will be left as an exercise): for a suitably nice inclusion, called a
‚ÄúcoÔ¨Åbration,‚Äù of a contractible space T in a space X, the quotient map X ‚àí‚ÜíX/T

4. Covers of graphs and Euler characteristics
DeÔ¨Åne the Euler characteristic œá(X) of a Ô¨Ånite graph X to be V ‚àíE, where V
is the number of vertices of X and E is the number of edges. By induction on the
number of edges, œá(T) = 1 for any Ô¨Ånite tree. The determination of the homotopy
types of graphs has the following immediate implication.
Corollary. If X is a connected graph, then œÄ1(X) is a free group with one
generator for each edge not in a given maximal tree. If X is Ô¨Ånite, then œÄ1(X) is
free on 1 ‚àíœá(X) generators; in particular, œá(X) ‚â§1, with equality if and only if
X is a tree.
Theorem. If B is a connected graph with vertex set B0 and p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is a

Proof. Regard an edge j of B as a path I ‚àí‚ÜíB and let k(e) : I ‚àí‚ÜíE be
the unique path such that p ‚ó¶k = j and k(e)(0) = e, where e ‚ààFj(0). We claim
that E is a graph with E0 as vertex set and the k(e) as edges. An easy path lifting
argument shows that each point of E ‚àíE0 is an interior point of exactly one edge,
hence we have a continuous bijection from the graph E0 ‚®ø(K √óI)/(‚àº) to E, where
K is the evident set of ‚Äúattaching maps‚Äù S0 ‚àí‚ÜíE0 for the speciÔ¨Åed edges. This
map is a homeomorphism since it is a local homeomorphism over B.

5. Applications to groups
The following purely algebraic result is most simply proved by topology.
Theorem. A subgroup H of a free group G is free. If G is free on k generators
and H has Ô¨Ånite index n in G, then H is free on 1 ‚àín + nk generators.
Proof. Realize G as œÄ1(B), where B is the wedge of one circle for each gen-
erator of G in a given free basis. Construct a covering p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB such that
p‚àó(œÄ1(E)) = H. Since E is a graph, H must be free. If G has k generators, then
œá(B) = 1 ‚àík. If [G : H] = n, then Fb has cardinality n and œá(E) = nœá(B).
Therefore 1 ‚àíœá(E) = 1 ‚àín + nk.
‚ñ°
We can extend the idea to realize any group as the fundamental group of some
connected space.
Theorem. For any group G, there is a connected space X such that œÄ1(X) is
isomorphic to G.

CHAPTER 5
Compactly generated spaces
We brieÔ¨Çy describe the category of spaces in which algebraic topologists cus-
tomarily work. The ordinary category of spaces allows pathology that obstructs
a clean development of the foundations. The homotopy and homology groups of
spaces are supported on compact subspaces, and it turns out that if one assumes
a separation property that is a little weaker than the HausdorÔ¨Äproperty, then one
can reÔ¨Åne the point-set topology of spaces to eliminate such pathology without
changing these invariants. We shall leave the proofs to the reader, but the wise
reader will simply take our word for it, at least on a Ô¨Årst reading: we do not want
to overemphasize this material, the importance of which can only become apparent
in retrospect.
1. The deÔ¨Ånition of compactly generated spaces
We shall understand compact spaces to be both compact and HausdorÔ¨Ä, fol-
lowing Bourbaki. A space X is said to be ‚Äúweak HausdorÔ¨Ä‚Äù if g(K) is closed in X
for every map g : K ‚àí‚ÜíX from a compact space K into X. When this holds, the
image g(K) is HausdorÔ¨Äand is therefore a compact subspace of X. This separa-
tion property lies between T1 (points are closed) and HausdorÔ¨Ä, but it is not much
weaker than the latter.
A subspace A of X is said to be ‚Äúcompactly closed‚Äù if g‚àí1(A) is closed in K for
any map g : K ‚àí‚ÜíX from a compact space K into X. When X is weak HausdorÔ¨Ä,
this holds if and only if the intersection of A with each compact subset of X is
closed. A space X is a ‚Äúk-space‚Äù if every compactly closed subspace is closed.
A space X is ‚Äúcompactly generated‚Äù if it is a weak HausdorÔ¨Äk-space. For
example, any locally compact space and any weak HausdorÔ¨Äspace that satisÔ¨Åes
the Ô¨Årst axiom of countability (every point has a countable neighborhood basis) is
compactly generated. We have expressed the deÔ¨Ånition in a form that should make
the following statement clear.
Lemma. If X is a compactly generated space and Y is any space, then a func-
tion f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is continuous if and only if its restriction to each compact subspace
K of X is continuous.
We can make a space X into a k-space by giving it a new topology in which a
space is closed if and only if it is compactly closed in the original topology. We call
the resulting space kX. Clearly the identity function kX ‚àí‚ÜíX is continuous. If
X is weak HausdorÔ¨Ä, then so is kX, hence kX is compactly generated. Moreover,
X and kX then have exactly the same compact subsets.
Write X √óc Y for the product of X and Y with its usual topology and write
X √ó Y = k(X √óc Y ). If X and Y are weak HausdorÔ¨Ä, then X √ó Y = kX √ó kY . If
X is locally compact and Y is compactly generated, then X √ó Y = X √óc Y .
39

40
COMPACTLY GENERATED SPACES
By deÔ¨Ånition, a space X is HausdorÔ¨Äif the diagonal subspace ‚àÜX = {(x, x)} is
closed in X √óc X. The weak HausdorÔ¨Äproperty admits a similar characterization.
Lemma. If X is a k-space, then X is weak HausdorÔ¨Äif and only if ‚àÜX is
closed in X √ó X.
2. The category of compactly generated spaces
One major source of point-set level pathology can be passage to quotient spaces.
Use of compactly generated topologies alleviates this.
Proposition. If X is compactly generated and œÄ : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is a quotient map,
then Y is compactly generated if and only if (œÄ √ó œÄ)‚àí1(‚àÜY ) is closed in X √ó X.
The interpretation is that a quotient space of a compactly generated space by a
‚Äúclosed equivalence relation‚Äù is compactly generated. We are particularly interested
in the following consequence.
Proposition. If X and Y are compactly generated spaces, A is a closed sub-
space of X, and f : A ‚àí‚ÜíY is any continuous map, then the pushout Y ‚à™f X is
compactly generated.
Another source of pathology is passage to colimits over sequences of maps
Xi ‚àí‚ÜíXi+1. When the given maps are inclusions, the colimit is the union of the
sets Xi with the ‚Äútopology of the union;‚Äù a set is closed if and only if its intersection
with each Xi is closed.
Proposition. If {Xi} is a sequence of compactly generated spaces and inclu-
sions Xi ‚àí‚ÜíXi+1 with closed images, then colim Xi is compactly generated.
We now adopt a more categorical point of view.
We redeÔ¨Åne U to be the
category of compactly generated spaces and continuous maps, and we redeÔ¨Åne T
to be its subcategory of based spaces and based maps.
Let wU be the category of weak HausdorÔ¨Äspaces. We have the functor k :
wU ‚àí‚ÜíU , and we have the forgetful functor j : U ‚àí‚ÜíwU , which embeds U as
a full subcategory of wU . Clearly
U (X, kY ) ‚àº= wU (jX, Y )
for X ‚ààU and Y ‚ààwU since the identity map kY ‚àí‚ÜíY is continuous and
continuity of maps deÔ¨Åned on compactly generated spaces is compactly determined.
Thus k is right adjoint to j.
We can construct colimits and limits of spaces by performing these construc-
tions on sets: they inherit topologies that give them the universal properties of
colimits and limits in the classical category of spaces. Limits of weak HausdorÔ¨Ä
spaces are weak HausdorÔ¨Ä, but limits of k-spaces need not be k-spaces. We con-
struct limits of compactly generated spaces by applying the functor k to their limits
as spaces. It is a categorical fact that functors which are right adjoints preserve
limits, so this does give categorical limits in U . This is how we deÔ¨Åned X √ó Y , for
example.
Point-set level colimits of weak HausdorÔ¨Äspaces need not be weak HausdorÔ¨Ä.
However, if a point-set level colimit of compactly generated spaces is weak Haus-
dorÔ¨Ä, then it is a k-space and therefore compactly generated. We shall only be
interested in colimits in those cases where this holds. The propositions above give
examples. In such cases, these constructions give categorical colimits in U .

From here on, we agree that all given spaces are to be compactly generated,
and we agree to redeÔ¨Åne any construction on spaces by applying the functor k to

For example, for spaces X and Y in U , we understand the function space

the Ô¨Ånite intersections of the subsets of the form {f|f(K) ‚äÇU} for some compact
subset K of X and open subset U of Y . This leads to the following adjointness
homeomorphism, which holds without restriction when we work in the category of
compactly generated spaces.
Proposition. For spaces X, Y , and Z in U , the canonical bijection
Z(X√óY ) ‚àº= (ZY )X
is a homeomorphism.
Observe in particular that a homotopy X √óI ‚àí‚ÜíY can equally well be viewed
as a map X ‚àí‚ÜíY I. These adjoint, or ‚Äúdual,‚Äù points of view will play an important
role in the next two chapters.

CHAPTER 6
CoÔ¨Åbrations
Exact sequences that feature in the study of homotopy, homology, and coho-
mology groups all can be derived homotopically from the theory of coÔ¨Åber and Ô¨Åber
sequences that we present in this and the following two chapters. Abstractions of
these ideas are at the heart of modern axiomatic treatments of homotopical algebra
and of the foundations of algebraic K-theory.
The theories of coÔ¨Åber and Ô¨Åber sequences illustrate an important, but informal,
duality theory, known as Eckmann-Hilton duality. It is based on the adjunction
between Cartesian products and function spaces. Our standing hypothesis that all
spaces in sight are compactly generated allows the theory to be developed without
further restrictions on the given spaces. We discuss ‚ÄúcoÔ¨Åbrations‚Äù here and the
‚Äúdual‚Äù notion of ‚ÄúÔ¨Åbrations‚Äù in the next chapter.

4. A criterion for a map to be a coÔ¨Åbration
We want a criterion that allows us to recognize coÔ¨Åbrations when we see them.
We shall often consider pairs (X, A) consisting of a space X and a subspace A.
CoÔ¨Åbration pairs will be those pairs that ‚Äúbehave homologically‚Äù just like the as-
sociated quotient spaces X/A.
Definition. A pair (X, A) is an NDR-pair (= neighborhood deformation re-
tract pair) if there is a map u : X ‚àí‚ÜíI such that u‚àí1(0) = A and a homotopy
h : X √ó I ‚àí‚ÜíX such that h0 = id, h(a, t) = a for a ‚ààA and t ‚ààI, and h(x, 1) ‚ààA
if u(x) < 1; (X, A) is a DR-pair if u(x) < 1 for all x ‚ààX, in which case A is a
deformation retract of X.
Lemma. If (h, u) and (j, v) represent (X, A) and (Y, B) as NDR-pairs, then
(k, w) represents the ‚Äúproduct pair‚Äù (X √óY, X √óB ‚à™A√óY ) as an NDR-pair, where
w(x, y) = min(u(x), v(y)) and
k(x, y, t) =
(
(h(x, t), j(y, tu(x)/v(y))) if v(y) ‚â•u(x)
(h(x, tv(y)/u(x)), j(y, t)) if u(x) ‚â•v(y).
If (X, A) or (Y, B) is a DR-pair, then so is (X √ó Y, X √ó B ‚à™A √ó Y ).
Proof. If v(y) = 0 and v(y) ‚â•u(x), then u(x) = 0 and both y ‚ààB and
x ‚ààA; therefore we can and must understand k(x, y, t) to be (x, y). It is easy to
check from this and the symmetric observation that k is a well deÔ¨Åned continuous
homotopy as desired.

Theorem. Let A be a closed subspace of X. Then the following are equivalent:
(i) (X, A) is an NDR-pair.
(ii) (X √ó I, X √ó {0} ‚à™A √ó I) is a DR-pair.
(iii) X √ó {0} ‚à™A √ó I is a retract of X √ó I.

Proof. The lemma gives that (i) implies (ii), (ii) trivially implies (iii), and
we have already seen that (iii) and (iv) are equivalent. Assume given a retraction

4. A criterion for a map to be a Ô¨Åbration
Again, we want a criterion that allows us to recognize Ô¨Åbrations when we see
them. Here the idea of duality fails, and we instead think of Ô¨Åbrations as general-
izations of coverings. When restricted to the spaces U in a well chosen open cover
O of the base space B, a covering is homeomorphic to the projection U √ó F ‚àí‚ÜíU,
where F is a Ô¨Åxed discrete set.
The obvious generalization of this is the notion of a bundle. A map p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB
is a bundle if, when restricted to the spaces U in a well chosen open cover O of B,
there are homeomorphisms œÜ : U √ó F ‚àí‚Üíp‚àí1(U) such that p ‚ó¶œÜ = œÄ1, where F
is a Ô¨Åxed topological space. We require of a ‚Äúwell chosen‚Äù open cover that it be
numerable. This means that there are continuous maps ŒªU : B ‚àí‚ÜíI such that
Œª‚àí1
U (0, 1] = U and that the cover is locally Ô¨Ånite, in the sense that each b ‚ààB
has a neighborhood that intersects only Ô¨Ånitely many U ‚ààO. Any open cover of
a paracompact space has a numerable reÔ¨Ånement. With this proviso on the open
covers allowed in the deÔ¨Ånition of a bundle, the following result shows in particular
that every bundle is a Ô¨Åbration.
Theorem. Let p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB be a map and let O be a numerable open cover of
B. Then p is a Ô¨Åbration if and only if p : p‚àí1(U) ‚àí‚ÜíU is a Ô¨Åbration for every
U ‚ààO.

assume that p|p‚àí1(U) is a Ô¨Åbration for each U ‚ààO. We shall construct a path lifting

such that Œª‚àí1

Just as the fundamental group œÄ1(B, b) of the base space of a covering acts on
the Ô¨Åber Fb, so the fundamental group œÄ1(B, b) of the base space of a Ô¨Åbration acts
‚Äúup to homotopy‚Äù on the Ô¨Åber, in a sense made precise by the following corollary.
For a space X, let œÄ0(X) denote the set of path components of X. The set of
homotopy equivalences of X is denoted Aut(X) and is topologized as a subspace of
the function space of maps X ‚àí‚ÜíX. The composite of homotopy equivalences is a
homotopy equivalence, and composition deÔ¨Ånes a continuous product on Aut(X).
With this product, Aut(X) is a ‚Äútopological monoid,‚Äù namely a space with a con-
tinuous and associative multiplication with a two-sided identity element, but it is
not a group. However, the path components of Aut(X) are the homotopy classes
of homotopy equivalences of X, and these do form a group under composition.
Corollary. Lifting of equivalence classes of loops speciÔ¨Åes a homomorphism
œÄ1(B, b) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ0(Aut(Fb)).

Based coÔ¨Åber and Ô¨Åber sequences
We use coÔ¨Åbrations and Ô¨Åbrations in the category T of based spaces to generate
two ‚Äúexact sequences of spaces‚Äù from a given map of based spaces. We shall write
‚àógenerically for the basepoints of based spaces. Much that we do for coÔ¨Åbrations
can be done equally well in the unbased context of the previous chapter. However,
the dual theory of Ô¨Åbration sequences only makes sense in the based context.
1. Based homotopy classes of maps
For based spaces X and Y , we let [X, Y ] denote the set of based homotopy
classes of based maps X ‚àí‚ÜíY . This set has a natural basepoint, namely the
homotopy class of the constant map from X to the basepoint of Y .
The appropriate analogue of the Cartesian product in the category of based
spaces is the ‚Äúsmash product‚Äù X ‚àßY deÔ¨Åned by
X ‚àßY = X √ó Y/X ‚à®Y.
Here X ‚à®Y is viewed as the subspace of X √ó Y consisting of those pairs (x, y) such
that either x is the basepoint of X or y is the basepoint of Y .
The appropriate based analogue of the function space is the subspace F(X, Y )
of Y X consisting of the based maps, with the constant based map as basepoint.
With these deÔ¨Ånitions, we have a natural homeomorphism of based spaces
F(X ‚àßY, Z) ‚àº= F(X, F(Y, Z))
for based spaces X and Y .
Recall that œÄ0(X) denotes the set of path components of X. When X is based,
so is this set, and we sometimes denote it by œÄ0(X, ‚àó). Observe that [X, Y ] may be
identiÔ¨Åed with œÄ0(F(X, Y )).
2. Cones, suspensions, paths, loops
Let X be a based space. We deÔ¨Åne the cone on X to be CX = X ‚àßI, where I
is given the basepoint 1. That is,
CX = X √ó I/({‚àó} √ó I ‚à™X √ó {1}).
We view S1 as I/‚àÇI, denote its basepoint by 1, and deÔ¨Åne the suspension of X to
be Œ£X = X ‚àßS1. That is,
Œ£X = X √ó S1/({‚àó} √ó S1 ‚à™X √ó {1}).
These are sometimes called the reduced cone and suspension, to distinguish them
from the unreduced constructions, in which the line {‚àó} √ó I through the basepoint
of X is not identiÔ¨Åed to a point. We shall make use of both constructions in our
work, but we shall not distinguish them notationally.

Dually, we deÔ¨Åne the path space of X to be PX = F(I, X), where I is given the
basepoint 0. Thus the points of PX are the paths in X that start at the basepoint.
We deÔ¨Åne the loop space of X to be ‚Ñ¶X = F(S1, X). Its points are the loops at
the basepoint.
We have the adjunction

3. Based coÔ¨Åbrations
The deÔ¨Ånition of a coÔ¨Åbration has an evident based variant, in which all given
and constructed maps in our test diagrams are required to be based. A based map
i : A ‚àí‚ÜíX that is a coÔ¨Åbration in the unbased sense is necessarily a coÔ¨Åbration in
the based sense since the basepoint of X must lie in A.
We say that X is ‚Äúnondegenerately based,‚Äù or ‚Äúwell pointed,‚Äù if the inclusion of
its basepoint is a coÔ¨Åbration in the unbased sense. If A and X are nondegenerately
based and i : A ‚àí‚ÜíX is a based coÔ¨Åbration, then i is necessarily an unbased
coÔ¨Åbration.
We refer to based coÔ¨Åbrations simply as coÔ¨Åbrations in the rest of this chapter.
Write Y+ for the union of a space Y and a disjoint basepoint and observe that
we can identify X ‚àßY+ with X √ó Y/{‚àó} √ó Y .
The space X ‚àßI+ is called the reduced cylinder on X, and a based homotopy
X √óI ‚àí‚ÜíY is the same thing as a based map X ‚àßI+ ‚àí‚ÜíY . We change notations
and write Mf for the based mapping cylinder Y ‚à™f (X ‚àßI+) of a based map f.
As in the unbased case, we conclude that a based map i : A ‚àí‚ÜíX is a
coÔ¨Åbration if and only if Mi is a retract of X ‚àßI+.

5. Based Ô¨Åbrations
Similarly, the deÔ¨Ånition of a Ô¨Åbration has an evident based variant, in which
all given and constructed maps in our test diagrams are required to be based.
A based Ô¨Åbration p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is necessarily a Ô¨Åbration in the unbased sense,
as we see by restricting to spaces of the form Y+ in test diagrams and noting that
Y+‚àßI+ ‚àº= (Y √óI)+. Less obviously, if p is a based map that is an unbased Ô¨Åbration,
then it satisÔ¨Åes the based CHP for test diagrams in which Y is nondegenerately
based.
We refer to based Ô¨Åbrations simply as Ô¨Åbrations in the rest of this chapter.
Observe that a based homotopy X ‚àßI+ ‚àí‚ÜíY is the same thing as a based map
X ‚àí‚ÜíF(I+, Y ). Here F(I+, Y ) is the same space as Y I, but given a basepoint
determined by the basepoint of Y . Therefore the based version of the mapping path
space Nf of a based map f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is the same space as the unbased version,
but given a basepoint determined by the given basepoints of X and Y . However,
because path spaces are always deÔ¨Åned with I having basepoint 0 rather than 1,
we Ô¨Ånd it convenient to redeÔ¨Åne Nf correspondingly, setting
Nf = {(x, œá)|œá(1) = f(x)} ‚äÇX √ó Y I.
As in the unbased case, we easily check that a based map p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is a
Ô¨Åbration if and only if there is a based path lifting function
s : Np ‚àí‚ÜíF(I+, E).

CHAPTER 9
Higher homotopy groups
The most basic invariants in algebraic topology are the homotopy groups. They
are very easy to deÔ¨Åne, but very hard to compute. We give the basic properties of
these groups here.
1. The deÔ¨Ånition of homotopy groups
For n ‚â•0 and a based space X, deÔ¨Åne
œÄn(X) = œÄn(X, ‚àó) = [Sn, X],
the set of homotopy classes of based maps Sn ‚àí‚ÜíX. This is a group if n ‚â•1 and
an Abelian group if n ‚â•2. When n = 0 and n = 1, this agrees with our previous
deÔ¨Ånitions. Observe that
œÄn(X) = œÄn‚àí1(‚Ñ¶X) = ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ = œÄ0(‚Ñ¶nX).
For ‚àó‚ààA ‚äÇX, the (homotopy) Ô¨Åber of the inclusion A ‚àí‚ÜíX may be identiÔ¨Åed
with the space P(X; ‚àó, A) of paths in X that begin at the basepoint and end in A.
For n ‚â•1, deÔ¨Åne
œÄn(X, A) = œÄn(X, A, ‚àó) = œÄn‚àí1P(X; ‚àó, A).
This is a group if n ‚â•2 and an Abelian group if n ‚â•3. Again,
œÄn(X, A) = œÄ0(‚Ñ¶n‚àí1P(X; ‚àó, A)).
These are called relative homotopy groups.

4. A few calculations
We observe some easily derived calculational facts about homotopy groups.
Lemma. If X is contractible, then œÄn(X) = 0 for all n ‚â•0.
Lemma. If X is discrete, then œÄn(X) = 0 for all n > 0.
Lemma. If p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is a covering, then p‚àó: œÄn(E) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄn(B) is an
isomorphism for all n ‚â•2.
Lemma. œÄ1(S1) = Z and œÄn(S1) = 0 if n Ã∏= 1.
Lemma. If i ‚â•2, then œÄ1(RP i) = Z2 and œÄn(RP i) ‚àº= œÄn(Si) for n Ã∏= 1.

4. A FEW CALCULATIONS
67
Lemma. For all spaces X and Y and all n,
œÄn(X √ó Y ) ‚àº= œÄn(X) √ó œÄn(Y ).
Lemma. If i < n, then œÄi(Sn) = 0.
Proof. For any based map f : Si ‚àí‚ÜíSn, we can apply smooth (or simplicial)
approximation to obtain a based homotopy from f to a map that misses a point
p, and we can then deform f to the trivial map by contracting Sn ‚àí{p} to the
basepoint.
‚ñ°
There are three standard bundles, called the Hopf bundles, that can be used
to obtain a bit more information about the homotopy groups of spheres. Recall
that CP 1 is the space of complex lines in C2. That is, CP 1 = (C √ó C ‚àí{0})/(‚àº),
where (z1, z2) ‚àº(Œªz1, Œªz2) for complex numbers Œª, z1, and z2. Write [z1, z2] for
the equivalence class of (z1, z2).
We obtain a homeomorphism CP 1 ‚àí‚ÜíS2 by
identifying S2 with the one-point compactiÔ¨Åcation of C and mapping [z1, z2] to
z2/z1 if z1 Ã∏= 0 and to the point at ‚àûif z1 = 0. The Hopf map Œ∑ : S3 ‚àí‚ÜíS2
is speciÔ¨Åed by Œ∑(z1, z2) = [z1, z2], where S3 is identiÔ¨Åed with the unit sphere in
the complex plane C2. It is a worthwhile exercise to check that Œ∑ is a bundle with
Ô¨Åber S1. By use of the quaternions and Cayley numbers, we obtain analogous Hopf
maps ŒΩ : S7 ‚àí‚ÜíS4 and œÉ : S15 ‚àí‚ÜíS8. Then ŒΩ is a bundle with Ô¨Åber S3 and œÉ
is a bundle with Ô¨Åber S7. Since we have complete information on the homotopy
groups of S1, the long exact sequence of homotopy groups associated to Œ∑ has the
following direct consequence.
Lemma. œÄ2(S2) ‚àº= Z and œÄn(S3) ‚àº= œÄn(S2) for n ‚â•3.
We shall later prove the following more substantial result.
Theorem. For all n ‚â•1, œÄn(Sn) ‚àº= Z.
It is left as an exercise to show that the long exact sequence associated to ŒΩ
implies that œÄ7(S4) contains an element of inÔ¨Ånite order, and œÉ can be used similarly
to show the same for œÄ15(S8).
In fact, the homotopy groups œÄq(Sn) for q > n > 1 are all Ô¨Ånite except for
œÄ4n‚àí1(S2n), which is the direct sum of Z and a Ô¨Ånite group.
The diÔ¨Éculty of computing homotopy groups is well illustrated by the fact that
there is no non-contractible simply connected compact manifold (or Ô¨Ånite CW com-
plex) all of whose homotopy groups are known. We shall Ô¨Ånd many non-compact
spaces whose homotopy groups we can determine completely. Such computations
will rely on the following observation.
Lemma. If X is the colimit of a sequence of inclusions Xi ‚àí‚ÜíXi+1 of based
spaces, then the natural map
colimi œÄn(Xi) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄn(X)
is an isomorphism for each n.
Proof. This follows directly from the point-set topological fact that if K is a
compact space, then a map K ‚àí‚ÜíX has image in one of the Xi.

5. Change of basepoint
We shall use our results on change of Ô¨Åbers to generalize our results on change of
basepoint from the fundamental group to the higher absolute and relative homotopy
groups. In the absolute case, we have the identiÔ¨Åcation
œÄn(X, x) = [(Sn, ‚àó), (X, x)],
where we assume that n ‚â•1.
Since the inclusion of the basepoint in Sn is a
coÔ¨Åbration, evaluation at the basepoint gives a Ô¨Åbration p : XSn ‚àí‚ÜíX.
We

Corollary. A homotopy equivalence of spaces or of pairs of spaces induces
an isomorphism on all homotopy groups.
We shall soon show that the converse holds for a quite general class of spaces,
namely the class of CW complexes, but we Ô¨Årst need a few preliminaries.
6. n-Equivalences, weak equivalences, and a technical lemma
Definition. A map e : Y ‚àí‚ÜíZ is an n-equivalence if, for all y ‚ààY , the map
e‚àó: œÄq(Y, y) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄq(Z, e(y))
is an injection for q < n and a surjection for q ‚â§n; e is said to be a weak equivalence
if it is an n-equivalence for all n.
Thus any homotopy equivalence is a weak equivalence. The following technical
lemma will be at the heart of our study of CW complexes, but it will take some
getting used to. It gives a useful criterion for determining when a given map is an
n-equivalence.
It is convenient to take CX to be the unreduced cone X √ó I/X √ó {1} here. If
f, f ‚Ä≤ : (X, A) ‚àí‚Üí(Y, B) are maps of pairs such that f = f ‚Ä≤ on A, then we say that
f and f ‚Ä≤ are homotopic relative to A if there is a homotopy h : f ‚âÉf ‚Ä≤ such that
h is constant on A, in the sense that h(a, t) = f(a) for all a ‚ààA and t ‚ààI; we
write h : f ‚âÉf ‚Ä≤ rel A. Observe that œÄn+1(X, x) can be viewed as the set of relative
homotopy classes of maps (CSn, Sn) ‚àí‚Üí(X, x).

CHAPTER 10
CW complexes
We introduce a large class of spaces, called CW complexes, between which a
weak equivalence is necessarily a homotopy equivalence. Thus, for such spaces, the
homotopy groups are, in a sense, a complete set of invariants. Moreover, we shall
see that every space is weakly equivalent to a CW complex.
1. The deÔ¨Ånition and some examples of CW complexes
Let Dn+1 be the unit disk {x | |x| ‚â§1} ‚äÇRn+1 with boundary Sn.
Definition. (i) A CW complex X is a space X which is the union of an
expanding sequence of subspaces Xn such that, inductively, X0 is a discrete set of
points (called vertices) and Xn+1 is the pushout obtained from Xn by attaching
disks Dn+1 along ‚Äúattaching maps‚Äù j : Sn ‚àí‚ÜíXn. Thus Xn+1 is the quotient space
obtained from Xn ‚à™(Jn+1 √óDn+1) by identifying (j, x) with j(x) for x ‚ààSn, where
Jn+1 is the discrete set of such attaching maps j. Each resulting map Dn+1 ‚àí‚ÜíX
is called a ‚Äúcell.‚Äù The subspace Xn is called the n-skeleton of X.
(ii) More generally, given any space A, we deÔ¨Åne a relative CW complex (X, A)
in the same fashion, but with X0 replaced by the union of A and a (possibly empty)
discrete set of points; we write (X, A)n, or Xn when A is clear from the context,
for the relative n-skeleton, and we say that (X, A) has dimension ‚â§n if X = Xn.
(iii) A subcomplex A of a CW complex X is a subspace and a CW complex
such that the composite of each cell Dn ‚àí‚ÜíA of A and the inclusion of A in X is
a cell of X. That is, A is the union of some of the cells of X. The pair (X, A) can
then be viewed as a relative CW complex.
(iv) A map of pairs f : (X, A) ‚àí‚Üí(Y, B) between relative CW complexes is
said to be ‚Äúcellular‚Äù if f(Xn) ‚äÇY n for all n.
Of course, pushouts and unions are understood in the topological sense, with
the compactly generated topologies. A subspace of X is closed if and only if its
intersection with each Xn is closed.
Examples. (i) A graph is a one-dimensional CW complex.
(ii) Via a homeomorphism I √ó I ‚àº= D2, the standard presentations of the torus
T = S1 √ó S1, the projective plane RP 2, and the Klein bottle K as quotients of a
square display these spaces as CW complexes with one or two vertices, two edges,
and one 2-cell:

unit sphere in Cn. We leave the speciÔ¨Åcation of the required homeomorphism as
an exercise.
2. Some constructions on CW complexes
We need to know that various constructions on spaces preserve CW complexes.
We leave most of the proofs as exercises in the meaning of the deÔ¨Ånitions.
Lemma. If (X, A) is a relative CW complex, then the quotient space X/A is
a CW complex with a vertex corresponding to A and one n-cell for each relative
n-cell of (X, A).
Lemma. For CW complexes Xi with basepoints that are vertices, the wedge of
the Xi is a CW complex which contains each Xi as a subcomplex.
Lemma. If A is a subcomplex of a CW complex X, Y is a CW complex, and
f : A ‚àí‚ÜíY is a cellular map, then the pushout Y ‚à™f X is a CW complex that
contains Y as a subcomplex and has one cell for each cell of X that is not in A.
The quotient complex (Y ‚à™f X)/Y is isomorphic to X/A.
Lemma. The colimit of a sequence of inclusions of subcomplexes Xn ‚àí‚ÜíXn+1
in CW complexes is a CW complex that contains each of the Xi as a subcomplex.

Lemma. The product X √óY of CW complexes X and Y is a CW complex with
an n-cell for each pair consisting of a p-cell of X and q-cell of Y , where p + q = n.
