(Dn, Sn‚àí1) ‚àº= (Dp √ó Dq, Dp √ó Sq‚àí1 ‚à™Sp‚àí1 √ó Dq).

Lemma. For a CW complex X, X √ó I is a CW complex that contains X √ó ‚àÇI
as a subcomplex and, in addition, has one (n + 1)-cell for each n-cell of X.
A ‚Äúcellular homotopy‚Äù h : f ‚âÉf ‚Ä≤ between cellular maps X ‚àí‚ÜíY of CW

Theorem (Whitehead). An n-equivalence between CW complexes of dimension
less than n is a homotopy equivalence. A weak equivalence between CW complexes
is a homotopy equivalence.
Proof. Let e : Y ‚àí‚ÜíZ satisfy either hypothesis. Since e‚àó: [Z, Y ] ‚àí‚Üí[Z, Z]
is a bijection, there is a map f : Z ‚àí‚ÜíY such that e ‚ó¶f ‚âÉid. Then e ‚ó¶f ‚ó¶e ‚âÉe,
and, since e‚àó: [Y, Y ] ‚àí‚Üí[Y, Z] is also a bijection, this implies that f ‚ó¶e ‚âÉid.
‚ñ°
If X is a Ô¨Ånite CW complex, in the sense that it has Ô¨Ånitely many cells, and if
dim X > 1 and X is not contractible, then it is known that X has inÔ¨Ånitely many
non-zero homotopy groups. The Whitehead theorem is thus surprisingly strong: in
its Ô¨Årst statement, if low dimensional homotopy groups are mapped isomorphically,
then so are all higher homotopy groups.
4. The cellular approximation theorem
Cellular maps are under much better algebraic control than general maps, as
will become both clear and important later. Fortunately, any map between CW
complexes is homotopic to a cellular map. We need a lemma.
Definition. A space X is said to be n-connected if œÄq(X, x) = 0 for 0 ‚â§q ‚â§n
and all x. A pair (X, A) is said to be n-connected if œÄ0(A) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ0(X) is surjective
and œÄq(X, A, a) = 0 for 1 ‚â§q ‚â§n and all a. It is equivalent that the inclusion
A ‚àí‚ÜíX be an n-equivalence.
Lemma. A relative CW complex (X, A) with no m-cells for m ‚â§n is n-
connected. In particular, (X, Xn) is n-connected for any CW complex X.
Proof. Consider f : (Iq, ‚àÇIq, Jq) ‚àí‚Üí(X, A, a), where q ‚â§n. Since the image
of f is compact, we may assume that (X, A) has Ô¨Ånitely many cells. By induction
on the number of cells, we may assume that X = A‚à™j Dr, where r > n. By smooth
(or simplicial) approximation, there is a map f ‚Ä≤ : Iq ‚àí‚ÜíX such that f ‚Ä≤ = f on
‚àÇIq, f ‚Ä≤ ‚âÉf rel ‚àÇIq and f ‚Ä≤ misses a point p in the interior of Dr. Clearly we can
deform X ‚àí{p} onto A and so deform f ‚Ä≤ to a map into A.
‚ñ°
Theorem (Cellular approximation). Any map f : (X, A) ‚àí‚Üí(Y, B) between
relative CW complexes is homotopic relative to A to a cellular map.
Proof. We proceed by induction over skeleta. To start the induction, note
that any point of Y is connected by a path to a point in Y 0 and apply this to the
images of points of X0‚àíA to obtain a homotopy of f|X0 to a map into Y 0. Assume
given gn : Xn ‚àí‚ÜíY n and hn : Xn √ó I ‚àí‚ÜíY such that hn : f|Xn ‚âÉŒπn ‚ó¶gn, where
Œπn : Y n ‚àí‚ÜíY is the inclusion. For an attaching map j : Sn ‚àí‚ÜíXn of a cell

7. Approximation of excisive triads by CW triads
We will need another, and considerably more subtle, relative approximation
theorem. A triad (X; A, B) is a space X together with subspaces A and B. This
must not be confused with a triple (X, A, B), which would require B ‚äÇA ‚äÇX. A
triad (X; A, B) is said to be excisive if X is the union of the interiors of A and B.
Such triads play a fundamental role in homology and cohomology theory, and some
version of the arguments to follow must play a role in any treatment. We prefer
to use these arguments to prove a strong homotopical result, rather than its pale
homological reÔ¨Çection that is seen in standard treatments of the subject.
A CW triad (X; A, B) is a CW complex X with subcomplexes A and B such
that X = A ‚à™B.
Theorem. Let (X; A, B) be an excisive triad and let C = A ‚à©B. Then there
is a CW triad (ŒìX; ŒìA, ŒìB) and a map of triads
Œ≥ : (ŒìX; ŒìA, ŒìB) ‚àí‚Üí(X; A, B)
such that, with ŒìC = ŒìA ‚à©ŒìB, the maps
Œ≥ : ŒìC ‚àí‚ÜíC,
Œ≥ : ŒìA ‚àí‚ÜíA,
Œ≥ : ŒìB ‚àí‚ÜíB,
and
Œ≥ : ŒìX ‚àí‚ÜíX
are all weak equivalences. If (A, C) is n-connected, then (ŒìA, ŒìC) can be chosen
to have no q-cells for q ‚â§n, and similarly for (B, C).
Up to homotopy, CW
approximation of excisive triads is functorial in such a way that Œ≥ is natural.

A CW triad (X; A, B) is not excisive, since A and B are closed in X, but it
is equivalent to an excisive triad. To see this, we describe a simple but important
general construction. Suppose that maps i : C ‚àí‚ÜíA and j : C ‚àí‚ÜíB are given.

sending (c, t) to the image of c in the pushout.

homotopy equivalence. That is, there is a homotopy inverse map and a pair of

that are obtained by gluing B to Mi and to C, and q is induced by r.

a cellular map, and let Y ‚à™f X be the pushout of f and the inclusion A ‚àí‚ÜíX.
4. Show that Y ‚à™f X is a CW complex with Y as a subcomplex and X/A
as a quotient complex. Formulate and prove a formula relating the Euler

CHAPTER 11
The homotopy excision and suspension theorems
The fundamental obstruction to the calculation of homotopy groups is the
failure of excision: for an excisive triad (X; A, B), the inclusion (A, A ‚à©B) ‚àí‚Üí
(X, B) fails to induce an isomorphism of homotopy groups in general. It is this that
distinguishes homotopy groups from the far more computable homology groups.
However, we do have such an isomorphism in a range of dimensions. This implies
the Freudenthal suspension theorem, which gives that œÄn+q(Œ£nX) is independent
of n if q is small relative to n. We shall rely on the consequence œÄn(Sn) ‚àº= Z in our
construction of homology groups.
1. Statement of the homotopy excision theorem
We shall prove the following theorem later in this chapter, but we Ô¨Årst explain
its consequences.
Definition. A map f : (A, C) ‚àí‚Üí(X, B) of pairs is an n-equivalence, n ‚â•1,
if
(f‚àó)‚àí1(im(œÄ0(B) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ0(X))) = im(œÄ0(C) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ0(A))
(which holds automatically when A and X are path connected) and, for all choices
of basepoint in C,
f‚àó: œÄq(A, C) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄq(X, B)
is a bijection for q < n and a surjection for q = n.
Recall that a pair (A, C) is n-connected, n ‚â•0, if œÄ0(C) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ0(A) is surjective
and œÄq(A, C) = 0 for q ‚â§n.
Theorem (Homotopy excision). Let (X; A, B) be an excisive triad such that
C = A ‚à©B is non-empty. Assume that (A, C) is (m ‚àí1)-connected and (B, C) is
(n ‚àí1)-connected, where m ‚â•2 and n ‚â•1. Then the inclusion (A, C) ‚àí‚Üí(X, B)
is an (m + n ‚àí2)-equivalence.
This specializes to give a relationship between the homotopy groups of pairs
(X, A) and of quotients X/A and to prove the Freudenthal suspension theorem.
Theorem. Let f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY be an (n‚àí1)-equivalence between (n‚àí2)-connected
spaces, where n ‚â•2; thus œÄn‚àí1(f) is an epimorphism. Then the quotient map
œÄ : (Mf, X) ‚àí‚Üí(Cf, ‚àó) is a (2n ‚àí2)-equivalence. In particular, Cf is (n ‚àí1)-
connected. If X and Y are (n ‚àí1)-connected, then œÄ : (Mf, X) ‚àí‚Üí(Cf, ‚àó) is a
(2n ‚àí1)-equivalence.
Proof. We are writing Cf for the unreduced coÔ¨Åber Mf/X. We have the
excisive triad (Cf; A, B), where
A = Y ‚à™(X √ó [0, 2/3])
and
B = (X √ó [1/3, 1])/(X √ó {1}).
83

84
THE HOMOTOPY EXCISION AND SUSPENSION THEOREMS
Thus C ‚â°A ‚à©B = X √ó [1/3, 2/3]. It is easy to check that œÄ is homotopic to a
composite
(Mf, X)
‚âÉ
‚àí‚Üí(A, C) ‚àí‚Üí(Cf, B)
‚âÉ
‚àí‚Üí(Cf, ‚àó),
the Ô¨Årst and last arrows of which are homotopy equivalences of pairs. The hypoth-
esis on f and the long exact sequence of the pair (Mf, X) imply that (Mf, X)
and therefore also (A, C) are (n ‚àí1)-connected. In view of the connecting isomor-
phism ‚àÇ: œÄq+1(CX, X) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄq(X) and the evident homotopy equivalence of pairs
(B, C) ‚âÉ(CX, X), (B, C) is also (n ‚àí1)-connected, and it is n-connected if X is
(n ‚àí1)-connected. The homotopy excision theorem gives the conclusions.
‚ñ°
We shall later use the following bit of the result to prove the Hurewicz theorem
relating homotopy groups to homology groups.
Corollary. Let f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY be a based map between (n ‚àí1)-connected
nondegenerately based spaces, where n ‚â•2. Then Cf is (n ‚àí1)-connected and
œÄn(Mf, X) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄn(Cf, ‚àó)
is an isomorphism.

isomorphism
œÄn‚àí1(Ff) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄn(Cf).
Proof. Here in the based context, we are thinking of the reduced mapping
cylinder and coÔ¨Åber, but the maps to them from the unreduced constructions are
homotopy equivalences since our basepoints are nondegenerate. Thus the Ô¨Årst state-
ment is immediate from the theorem.
For the second, if j : X ‚àí‚ÜíMf is the
inclusion, then we have a map

3. Proof of the homotopy excision theorem
This is a deep result, and it is remarkable that a direct homotopical proof, in
principle an elementary one, is possible. Most standard texts, if they treat this
topic at all, give a far more sophisticated proof of a signiÔ¨Åcantly weaker result.
However, the reader may prefer to skip this argument on a Ô¨Årst reading. The idea
is clear enough. We are trying to show that a certain map of pairs induces an
isomorphism in a range of dimensions. We capture the relevant map as part of a
long exact sequence, and we prove that the third term in the long exact sequence
vanishes in the required range.
However, we start with an auxiliary long exact sequence that we shall also
need. Recall that a triple (X, A, B) consists of spaces B ‚äÇA ‚äÇX and must not be
confused with a triad.
Proposition. For a triple (X, A, B) and any basepoint in B, the following
sequence is exact:

inclusions.
Proof. The proof is a purely algebraic deduction from the long exact se-
quences of the various pairs in sight and is left as an exercise for the reader.

3. PROOF OF THE HOMOTOPY EXCISION THEOREM

Now we return to the homotopy excision theorem. Its conditions m ‚â•1 and
n ‚â•1 merely give that œÄ0(C) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ0(A) and œÄ0(C) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ0(B) are surjective,
and any extraneous components of A or B would not aÔ¨Äect the relevant homotopy
groups. The condition m ‚â•2 implies that (X, B) is 1-connected. By the long exact
sequence just given, the theorem is equivalent to the following one.
Theorem. Under the hypotheses of the homotopy excision theorem,
œÄq(X; A, B) = 0 for 2 ‚â§q ‚â§m + n ‚àí2
and all choices of basepoint ‚àó‚ààC.
In this form, the conclusion is symmetric in A and B and vacuous if m+n ‚â§3.
Thus our hypotheses m ‚â•2 and n ‚â•1 are the minimal ones under which our
strategy can apply.
In order to have some hope of tackling the problem in direct terms, we Ô¨Årst
reduce it to the case when A and B are each obtained from C by attaching a single
cell. We may approximate our given excisive triad by a weakly equivalent CW
triad. This does not change the triad homotopy groups. More precisely, by our
connectivity hypotheses, we may assume that X is a CW complex that is the union
of subcomplexes A and B with intersection C, where (A, C) has no relative q-cells
for q < m and (B, C) has no relative q-cells for q < n. Since any map Iq ‚àí‚ÜíX has
image contained in a Ô¨Ånite subcomplex, we may assume that X has Ô¨Ånitely many
cells. We may also assume that (A, C) and (B, C) each have at least one cell since
otherwise the result holds trivially.
We claim Ô¨Årst that, inductively, it suÔ¨Éces to prove the result when (A, C) has
exactly one cell. Indeed, suppose that C ‚äÇA‚Ä≤ ‚äÇA, where A is obtained from A‚Ä≤ by
attaching a single cell and (A‚Ä≤, C) has one less cell than (A, C). Let X‚Ä≤ = A‚Ä≤ ‚à™C B.

A little homological algebra
Let R be a commutative ring. The main example will be R = Z. We develop
some rudimentary homological algebra in the category of R-modules. We shall say
more later. For now, we give the minimum that will be needed to develop cellular
and singular homology theory.
1. Chain complexes
A chain complex over R is a sequence of maps of R-modules

over R is an analogous sequence

In practice, we usually require chain complexes to satisfy

such as this (and we never take the sum of elements in diÔ¨Äerent gradings).

4. Short and long exact sequences
A sequence M ‚Ä≤
f‚àí‚ÜíM
g‚àí‚ÜíM ‚Ä≤‚Ä≤ of modules is exact if im f = ker g. If M ‚Ä≤ = 0, this
means that g is a monomorphism; if M ‚Ä≤‚Ä≤ = 0, it means that f is an epimorphism.
A longer sequence is exact if it is exact at each position. A short exact sequence of
chain complexes is a sequence
0 ‚àí‚ÜíX‚Ä≤
f‚àí‚ÜíX
g‚àí‚ÜíX‚Ä≤‚Ä≤ ‚àí‚Üí0

Proposition. A short exact sequence of chain complexes naturally gives rise
to a long exact sequence of R-modules
¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚àí‚ÜíHq(X‚Ä≤)

Proof. Write [x] for the homology class of a cycle x. We deÔ¨Åne the ‚Äúconnecting

some x such that g(x) = x‚Ä≤‚Ä≤. There is such an x since g is an epimorphism, and
there is such an x‚Ä≤ since gd(x) = dg(x) = 0. It is a standard exercise in ‚Äúdiagram
chasing‚Äù to verify that ‚àÇis well deÔ¨Åned and the sequence is exact.
Naturality
means that a commutative diagram of short exact sequences of chain complexes
gives rise to a commutative diagram of long exact sequences of R-modules. The
essential point is the naturality of the connecting homomorphism, which is easily
checked.

CHAPTER 13
Axiomatic and cellular homology theory
Homology groups are the basic computable invariants of spaces. Unlike homo-
topy groups, these are stable invariants, the same for a space and its suspension,
and it is this that makes them computable. In this and the following two chapters,
we Ô¨Årst give both an axiomatic and a cellular description of homology, next revert
to an axiomatic development of the properties of homology, and then prove the
Hurewicz theorem and use it to prove the uniqueness of homology.
1. Axioms for homology
Fix an Abelian group œÄ and consider pairs of spaces (X, A). We shall see that
œÄ determines a ‚Äúhomology theory on pairs (X, A).‚Äù We say that a map (X, A) ‚àí‚Üí
(Y, B) of pairs is a weak equivalence if its maps A ‚àí‚ÜíB and X ‚àí‚ÜíY are weak
equivalences.
Theorem. For integers q, there exist functors Hq(X, A; œÄ) from the homotopy
category of pairs of spaces to the category of Abelian groups together with natural
transformations ‚àÇ: Hq(X, A; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHq‚àí1(A; œÄ), where Hq(X; œÄ) is deÔ¨Åned to be
Hq(X, ‚àÖ; œÄ). These functors and natural transformations satisfy and are character-
ized by the following axioms.
‚Ä¢ DIMENSION If X is a point, then H0(X; œÄ) = œÄ and Hq(X; œÄ) = 0 for
all other integers.
‚Ä¢ EXACTNESS The following sequence is exact, where the unlabeled arrows
are induced by the inclusions A ‚àí‚ÜíX and (X, ‚àÖ) ‚àí‚Üí(X, A):
¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚àí‚ÜíHq(A; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHq(X; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHq(X, A; œÄ)
‚àÇ‚àí‚ÜíHq‚àí1(A; œÄ) ‚àí‚Üí¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ .
‚Ä¢ EXCISION If (X; A, B) is an excisive triad, so that X is the union of the
interiors of A and B, then the inclusion (A, A ‚à©B) ‚àí‚Üí(X, B) induces
an isomorphism
H‚àó(A, A ‚à©B; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(X, B; œÄ).
‚Ä¢ ADDITIVITY If (X, A) is the disjoint union of a set of pairs (Xi, Ai),
then the inclusions (Xi, Ai) ‚àí‚Üí(X, A) induce an isomorphism
P
iH‚àó(Xi, Ai; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(X, A; œÄ).
‚Ä¢ WEAK EQUIVALENCE If f : (X, A) ‚àí‚Üí(Y, B) is a weak equivalence,
then
f‚àó: H‚àó(X, A; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(Y, B; œÄ)
is an isomorphism.

Here, by a standard abuse, we write f‚àóinstead of H‚àó(f) or Hq(f). Our approxi-
mation theorems for spaces, pairs, maps, homotopies, and excisive triads imply that
such a theory determines and is determined by an appropriate theory deÔ¨Åned on
CW pairs, as spelled out in the following CW version of the theorem.
Theorem. For integers q, there exist functors Hq(X, A; œÄ) from the homotopy
category of pairs of CW complexes to the category of Abelian groups together with
natural transformations ‚àÇ: Hq(X, A) ‚àí‚ÜíHq‚àí1(A; œÄ), where Hq(X; œÄ) is deÔ¨Åned to
be Hq(X, ‚àÖ; œÄ). These functors and natural transformations satisfy and are charac-
terized by the following axioms.
‚Ä¢ DIMENSION If X is a point, then H0(X; œÄ) = œÄ and Hq(X; œÄ) = 0 for
all other integers.
‚Ä¢ EXACTNESS The following sequence is exact, where the unlabeled arrows
are induced by the inclusions A ‚àí‚ÜíX and (X, ‚àÖ) ‚àí‚Üí(X, A):
¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚àí‚ÜíHq(A; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHq(X; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHq(X, A; œÄ)
‚àÇ‚àí‚ÜíHq‚àí1(A; œÄ) ‚àí‚Üí¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ .
‚Ä¢ EXCISION If X is the union of subcomplexes A and B, then the inclusion
(A, A ‚à©B) ‚àí‚Üí(X, B) induces an isomorphism
H‚àó(A, A ‚à©B; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(X, B; œÄ).
‚Ä¢ ADDITIVITY If (X, A) is the disjoint union of a set of pairs (Xi, Ai),
then the inclusions (Xi, Ai) ‚àí‚Üí(X, A) induce an isomorphism
P
iH‚àó(Xi, Ai; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(X, A; œÄ).
Such a theory determines and is determined by a theory as in the previous theorem.
Proof. We prove the last statement and return to the rest later. Since a CW
triad (which, we recall, was required to be the union of its given subcomplexes)
is homotopy equivalent to an excisive triad, it is immediate that the restriction to
CW pairs of a theory on pairs of spaces gives a theory on pairs of CW complexes.
Conversely, given a theory on CW pairs, we may deÔ¨Åne a theory on pairs of spaces
by turning the weak equivalence axiom into a deÔ¨Ånition. That is, we Ô¨Åx a CW
approximation functor Œì from the homotopy category of pairs of spaces to the
homotopy category of CW pairs and we deÔ¨Åne
H‚àó(X, A; œÄ) = H‚àó(ŒìX, ŒìA; œÄ).
Similarly, we deÔ¨Åne ‚àÇfor (X, A) to be ‚àÇfor (ŒìX, ŒìA). For a map f : (X, A) ‚àí‚Üí
(Y, B) of pairs, we deÔ¨Åne f‚àó= (Œìf)‚àó. It is clear from our earlier results that this
does give a well deÔ¨Åned homology theory on pairs of spaces.
‚ñ°
Clearly, up to canonical isomorphism, this construction of a homology theory
on pairs of spaces is independent of the choice of our CW approximation functor Œì.
The reader may have seen singular homology before. As we shall explain later, the
classical construction of singular homology amounts to a choice of a particularly
nice CW approximation functor, one that is actually functorial on the point-set
level, before passage to homotopy categories.

2. Cellular homology
We must still construct H‚àó(X, A; œÄ) on CW pairs. We shall give a seemingly
ad hoc construction, but we shall later see that precisely this construction is in
fact forced upon us by the axioms. We concentrate on the case œÄ = Z, and we
abbreviate notation by setting H‚àó(X, A) = H‚àó(X, A; Z).
Let X be a CW complex. We shall deÔ¨Åne the cellular chain complex C‚àó(X).
We let Cn(X) be the free Abelian group with one generator [j] for each n-cell j.

cones, coÔ¨Åbers, and suspensions in this section; that is, we do not choose basepoints
and so we do not collapse out lines through basepoints. (We shall discuss this dif-
ference more formally in the next chapter.) We still have the basic result that if
i : A ‚àí‚ÜíX is a coÔ¨Åbration, then collapsing the cone on A to a point gives a ho-
motopy equivalence œà : Ci ‚àí‚ÜíX/A. We shall use the notation œà‚àí1 for any chosen
homotopy inverse to such a homotopy equivalence. We again obtain œÄ : Ci ‚àí‚ÜíŒ£A
by collapsing the base X of the coÔ¨Åber to a point.

3. VeriÔ¨Åcation of the axioms
For a CW complex X with base vertex ‚àó, deÔ¨Åne ÀúC‚àó(X) = C‚àó(X)/C‚àó(‚àó) and
deÔ¨Åne ÀúH‚àó(X) = H‚àó( ÀúC‚àó(X)). This is the reduced homology of X. For a subcomplex
A of X, deÔ¨Åne

and deÔ¨Åne
H‚àó(X, A) = H‚àó(C‚àó(X, A)) ‚àº= ÀúH‚àó(X/A).
The long exact homology sequence of the exact sequence of chain complexes
0 ‚àí‚ÜíC‚àó(A) ‚àí‚ÜíC‚àó(X) ‚àí‚ÜíC‚àó(X, A) ‚àí‚Üí0
gives the connecting homorphisms ‚àÇ: Hq(X, A) ‚àí‚ÜíHq‚àí1(A) and the long exact
sequence called for in the exactness axiom.
If f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY is a cellular map, it induces maps Xn/Xn‚àí1 ‚àí‚ÜíY n/Y n‚àí1
that commute up to homotopy with the topological boundary maps and so induce
homomorphisms fn : Cn(X) ‚àí‚ÜíCn(Y ) that commute with the diÔ¨Äerentials. That
is, f‚àóis a chain map, and it induces a homomorphism H‚àó(X) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(Y ).
For any CW complex X, X √ó I is a CW complex whose cellular chains are
isomorphic to C‚àó(X) ‚äóC‚àó(I), as we shall verify in the next section. Here C‚àó(I) ‚àº=
I has basis elements [0] and [1] of degree zero and [I] of degree one such that
d([I]) = [0] ‚àí[1]. We have observed that, for chain complexes C and D, a chain
map C ‚äóI ‚àí‚ÜíD can be identiÔ¨Åed with a chain homotopy between its restrictions
to C ‚äóZ[0] and C ‚äóZ[1]. A cellular homotopy h : X √ó I ‚àí‚ÜíY induces just such
a chain map, hence cellularly homotopic maps induce the same homomorphism on
homology. The analogous conclusion for pairs follows by consideration of quotient
complexes.
The dimension and additivity axioms are obvious. If X is a point, then C‚àó(X) =
Z, concentrated in degree zero. The cellular chain complex of ‚®øXi is the direct sum
of the chain complexes C‚àó(Xi), and similarly for pairs. Excision is also obvious.
If X = A ‚à™B, then the inclusion A/A ‚à©B ‚àí‚ÜíX/B is an isomorphism of CW
complexes.
We have dealt so far with the case of integral homology. For more general
coeÔ¨Écient groups œÄ, we deÔ¨Åne
C‚àó(X, A; œÄ) = C‚àó(X, A) ‚äóœÄ
and proceed in exactly the same fashion to deÔ¨Åne homology groups and verify
the axioms. Observe that a homomorphism of groups œÄ ‚àí‚ÜíœÅ induces a natural
transformation
Hq(X, A; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHq(X, A; œÅ)
that commutes with the connecting homomorphisms.

algebraic procedure for the calculation of the homology of Cartesian products. We
explain the topological point here and return to the algebra later, where we discuss
the K¬®unneth theorem for the computation of the homology of tensor products of
chain complexes.

5. Some examples: T, K, and RP n
Cellular chains make some computations quite trivial. For example, since Sn
is a CW complex with one vertex and one n-cell, we see immediately that
ÀúHn(Sn; œÄ) ‚àº= œÄ
and
ÀúHq(Sn; œÄ) = 0
for q Ã∏= n.
A little less obviously, if we look back at the CW decompositions of the torus
T, the projective plane RP 2, and the Klein bottle K and if we let j denote the
unique 2-cell in each case, then we Ô¨Ånd the following descriptions of the cellular
chains and integral homologies by quick direct inspections. We agree to write Zn
for the cyclic group Z/nZ.
Examples. (i) The cell complex C‚àó(T) has one basis element [v] in degree
zero, two basis elements [e1] and [e2] in degree one, and one basis element [j] in
degree two. All basis elements are cycles, hence H‚àó(T; Z) = C‚àó(T).
(ii) The cell complex C‚àó(RP 2) has two basis elements [v1] and [v2] in degree
zero, two basis elements [e1] and [e2] in degree one, and one basis element [j] in
degree two. The diÔ¨Äerentials are given by
d([e1]) = [v1] ‚àí[v2],
d([e2]) = [v2] ‚àí[v1],
and d([j]) = 2[e1] + 2[e2].
Therefore H0(RP 2; Z) = Z with basis element the homology class of [v1] (or
[v2]), H1(RP 2; Z) = Z2 with non-zero element the homology class of e1 + e2, and
Hq(RP 2; Z) = 0 for q ‚â•2.
(iii) The cell complex C‚àó(K) has one basis element [v] in degree zero, two basis
elements [e1] and [e2] in degree one, and one basis element [j] in degree two. The
only non-zero diÔ¨Äerential is d([j]) = 2[e2].
Therefore H0(K; Z) = Z with basis
element the homology class of [v], H1(K; Z) = Z‚äïZ2 with Z generated by the class
of [e1] and Z2 generated by the class of [e2], and Hq(K; Z) = 0 for q ‚â•2.

However, these examples are misleading. While homology groups are far eas-
ier to compute than homotopy groups, direct chain level calculation is seldom the
method of choice. Rather, one uses chains as a tool for developing more sophisti-
cated algebraic techniques, notably spectral sequences. We give an illustration that
both shows that chain level calculations are sometimes practicable even when there
are many non-zero diÔ¨Äerentials to determine and indicates why one might not wish
to attempt such calculations for really complicated spaces.

RP n as the quotient of Sn obtained by identifying antipodal points, and we need
to know the degree of the antipodal map.

CHAPTER 14
Derivations of properties from the axioms
Returning to the axiomatic approach to homology, we assume given a theory
on pairs of spaces and make some deductions from the axioms. We abbreviate no-
tations by setting Eq(X, A) = Hq(X, A; œÄ). However, the arguments in this chapter
make no use whatever of the dimension axiom. A ‚Äúgeneralized homology theory‚Äù
E‚àóis deÔ¨Åned to be a system of functors Eq(X, A) and natural transformations
‚àÇ: Eq(X, A) ‚àí‚ÜíEq‚àí1(A) that satisfy all of our axioms except for the dimension
axiom. Similarly, we have the notion of a generalized homology theory on CW
pairs, and the results of the Ô¨Årst section of the previous chapter generalize directly
to give the following result.
Theorem. A homology theory E‚àóon pairs of spaces determines and is deter-
mined by its restriction to a homology theory E‚àóon pairs of CW complexes.
The study of such generalized homology theories pervades modern algebraic
topology, and we shall describe some examples later on. The brave reader may be
willing to think of E‚àóin such generality in this chapter. The timorous reader may
well prefer to think of E‚àó(X, A) concretely, following our proposal that E‚àó(X, A)
be taken as an alternative notation for H‚àó(X, A; œÄ).
1. Reduced homology; based versus unbased spaces
One of the themes of this chapter is the relationship between homology theories
on pairs of spaces and reduced homology theories on based spaces. The latter are
more convenient in most advanced work in algebraic topology. For a based space
X, we deÔ¨Åne the reduced homology of X to be
ÀúEq(X) = Eq(X, ‚àó).
Since the basepoint is a retract of X, there results a direct sum decomposition
E‚àó(X) ‚àº= ÀúE‚àó(X) ‚äïE‚àó(‚àó)
that is natural with respect to based maps. For ‚àó‚ààA ‚äÇX, the summand E‚àó(‚àó)
maps isomorphically under the map E‚àó(A) ‚àí‚ÜíE‚àó(X), and the exactness axiom
implies that there is a reduced long exact sequence
¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚àí‚ÜíÀúEq(A) ‚àí‚ÜíÀúEq(X) ‚àí‚ÜíEq(X, A)
‚àÇ‚àí‚ÜíÀúEq‚àí1(A) ‚àí‚Üí¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ .
We can obtain the unreduced homology groups as special cases of the reduced
ones. For an unbased space X, we deÔ¨Åne a based space X+ by adjoining a disjoint
basepoint to X. By the additivity axiom, we see immediately that
E‚àó(X) = ÀúE‚àó(X+).

Similarly, a map f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY of unbased spaces induces a map f+ : X+ ‚àí‚ÜíY+
of based spaces, and the map f‚àóon unreduced homology coincides with the map
(f+)‚àóon reduced homology.
We shall make considerable use of coÔ¨Åber sequences in this chapter. To be
consistent about this, we should always work with reduced cones and coÔ¨Åbers.
However, it is more convenient to make the convention that we work with unreduced
cones and coÔ¨Åbers when we apply unreduced homology theories, and we work with
reduced cones and coÔ¨Åbers when we apply reduced homology theories.
In fact,
the unreduced cone on a space Y coincides with the reduced cone on Y+: the line
through the disjoint basepoint is identiÔ¨Åed to the cone point when constructing
the reduced cone on Y+. Therefore the unreduced coÔ¨Åber of an unbased map f
coincides with the reduced coÔ¨Åber of the based map f+. Our convention really
means that we are always working with reduced coÔ¨Åbers, but when we are studying
unreduced homology theories we are implicitly applying the functor (‚àí)+ to put
ourselves in the based context before constructing cones and coÔ¨Åbers.
The observant reader will have noticed that the unreduced suspension of X is
not the reduced suspension on X+. Rather, under either interpretation of suspen-
sion, Œ£(X+) is homotopy equivalent to the wedge of Œ£(X) and a circle.

3. Suspension and the long exact sequence of pairs
We have a fundamentally important consequence of the results of the previous
section, which should be contrasted with what happened with homotopy groups.
Recall that a basepoint ‚àó‚ààX is nondegenerate if the inclusion {‚àó} ‚àí‚ÜíX is a
coÔ¨Åbration. This ensures that the inclusion of the line through the basepoint in the
unreduced suspension of X is a coÔ¨Åbration, so that the map from the unreduced
suspension to the suspension that collapses out the line through the basepoint is a
homotopy equivalence. We apply reduced homology here, so we use reduced cones
and suspensions.

4. Axioms for reduced homology
In the study of generalized homology theories, it is most convenient to restrict
attention to reduced homology theories deÔ¨Åned on nondegenerately based spaces.
The results of the previous sections imply that we can do so without loss of gen-

E‚àó, but we opt for bravery:

CHAPTER 15
The Hurewicz and uniqueness theorems
We now return to the context of ‚Äúordinary homology theories,‚Äù namely those
that satisfy the dimension axiom. We prove a fundamental relationship, called the
Hurewicz theorem, between homotopy groups and homology groups. We then use
it to prove the uniqueness of ordinary homology with coeÔ¨Écients in œÄ.
1. The Hurewicz theorem
Although the reader may prefer to think in terms of the cellular homology
theory already constructed, the proof of the Hurewicz theorem depends only on the
axioms. It is this fact that will allow us to use the result to prove the uniqueness
of homology theories in the next section. We take œÄ = Z and delete it from the
notation. The dimension axiom implicitly Ô¨Åxes a generator i0 of ÀúH0(S0), and we
choose generators in of ÀúHn(Sn) inductively by setting Œ£in = in+1.
Definition. For based spaces X, deÔ¨Åne the Hurewicz homomorphism
h : œÄn(X) ‚àí‚ÜíÀúHn(X)
by

Proof. For maps f, g : Sn ‚àí‚ÜíX, [f + g] is represented by the composite

where p is the pinch map and ‚ñΩis the codiagonal map; that is, ‚ñΩrestricts to the
identity on each wedge summand. Since p‚àó(in) = in + in and ‚ñΩinduces addition
on ÀúH‚àó(X), the conclusion follows.

Lemma. Let X be a wedge of n-spheres. Then
h : œÄn(X) ‚àí‚ÜíÀúHn(X)
is the Abelianization homomorphism if n = 1 and is an isomorphism if n > 1.
Proof. When X is a single sphere, h[id] = in and the conclusion is obvious.
In general, œÄn(X) is the free group if n = 1 or the free Abelian group if n ‚â•2
with generators given by the inclusions of the wedge summands. Since h maps
these generators to the canonical generators of the free Abelian group ÀúHn(X), the
conclusion follows.

2. The uniqueness of the homology of CW complexes
We assume given an ordinary homology theory on CW pairs and describe how

(1) Let œÄ be any group. Construct a connected CW complex K(œÄ, 1) such
that œÄ1(K(œÄ, 1)) = œÄ and œÄq(K(œÄ, 1)) = 0 for q Ã∏= 1.

n. (Hint: construct M(œÄ, n) as the coÔ¨Åber of a map between wedges of
spheres.) The spaces M(œÄ, n) are called Moore spaces.
(4) Let n ‚â•1 and let œÄ be an Abelian group. Construct a connected CW com-
plex K(œÄ, n) such that œÄn(X) = œÄ and œÄq(X) = 0 for q Ã∏= n. (Hint: start
with M(œÄ, n), using the Hurewicz theorem, and kill its higher homotopy
groups.) The spaces K(œÄ, n) are called Eilenberg-Mac Lane spaces.
(5) There are familiar spaces that give K(Z, 1), K(Z2, 1), and K(Z, 2). Name
them.

where K(œÄ, n) is the Eilenberg-Mac Lane space that you have constructed.

homotopy classes of based maps.

Singular homology theory
We explain, without giving full details, how the standard approach to singular
homology theory Ô¨Åts into our framework. We also introduce simplicial sets and
spaces and their geometric realization. These notions play a fundamental role in
modern algebraic topology.

3. PROOFS OF THE THEOREMS
125
Thus the singular chain complex of X is the cellular chain complex of a func-
torial CW approximation of X, and this shows that our original construction of
homology coincides with the classical construction in terms of singular chains. Each
approach has its mathematical and pedagogical advantages.

4. Simplicial objects in algebraic topology
A simplicial set K‚àóis a sequence of sets Kn, n ‚â•0, connected by face and
degeneracy operators di : Kn ‚àí‚ÜíKn‚àí1 and si : Kn ‚àí‚ÜíKn+1, 0 ‚â§i ‚â§n, that
satisfy the commutation relations that we displayed for the total singular complex
S‚àóX = {SnX} of a space X. Thus S‚àóis a functor from spaces to simplicial sets.
We may deÔ¨Åne the geometric realization |K‚àó| of general simplicial sets exactly
as we deÔ¨Åned the geometric realization ŒìX = |S‚àóX| of the total singular complex
of a topological space. In fact, the total singular complex and geometric realization
functors are adjoint. If S S is the category of simplicial sets and U the category
of spaces, then
U (|K‚àó|, X) ‚àº= S S (K‚àó, S‚àóX).

spaces is the composite

In fact, one can develop homotopy theory and homology in the category of

the chain complex C‚àó(K‚àó) deÔ¨Åned exactly as we deÔ¨Åned the singular chain complex,
using the alternating sum of the face maps, and there result homology groups
H‚àó(K‚àó; œÄ) = H‚àó(C‚àó(K‚àó) ‚äóœÄ).
Exactly as in the case of S‚àóX, |K‚àó| is a CW complex and C‚àó(K‚àó) is naturally iso-
morphic to the cellular chain complex C‚àó(|K‚àó|). Singular homology is the special
case obtained by taking K‚àó= S‚àóX for spaces X. The passage back and forth
between simplicial sets and topological spaces plays a major role in many applica-
tions.
The ideas generalize. One can deÔ¨Åne a simplicial object in any category C
as a sequence of objects Kn of C connected by face and degeneracy maps in C
that satisfy the commutation relations that we have displayed.
Thus we have
simplicial groups, simplicial Abelian groups, simplicial spaces, and so forth. We
can think of simplicial sets as discrete simplicial spaces, and we then see that
geometric realization generalizes directly to a functor | ‚àí| from the category S U
of simplicial spaces to the category U of spaces. This provides a very useful way
of constructing spaces with desirable properties.
We note one of the principal features of geometric realization.
DeÔ¨Åne the
product X‚àó√ó Y‚àóof simplicial spaces X‚àóand Y‚àóto be the simplicial space whose
space of n-simplexes is Xn √ó Yn, with faces and degeneracies di √ó di and si √ó si.
The projections induce maps of simplicial spaces from X‚àó√ó Y‚àóto X‚àóand Y‚àó. On
passage to geometric realization, these give the coordinates of a map
|X‚àó√ó Y‚àó| ‚àí‚Üí|X‚àó| √ó |Y‚àó|.
It turns out that this map is always a homeomorphism.
Now restrict attention to simplicial sets K‚àóand L‚àó. Then the homeomorphism
just speciÔ¨Åed is a map between CW complexes. However, it is not a cellular map;
rather, it takes the n-skeleton of |K‚àó√ó L‚àó| to the 2n-skeleton of |K‚àó| √ó |L‚àó|. It
is homotopic to a cellular map, no longer a homeomorphism, and there results a
chain homotopy equivalence
C‚àó(|K‚àó√ó L‚àó|) ‚àí‚ÜíC‚àó(|K‚àó|) ‚äóC‚àó(|L‚àó|).
In particular, for spaces X and Y , there is a natural chain homotopy equivalence
from the singular chain complex C‚àó(X √ó Y ) to the tensor product C‚àó(X) ‚äóC‚àó(Y ).
One can be explicit about this but, pedagogically, one technical advantage of ap-
proaching homology via CW complexes is that it leaves us free to work directly
with the natural cell structures on Cartesian products of CW complexes and to
postpone the introduction of chain homotopy equivalences such as these to a later
stage of the development.

CHAPTER 17
Some more homological algebra
The reader will by now appreciate that the calculation of homology groups,
although far simpler than the calculation of homotopy groups, can still be a diÔ¨Écult
task. In practice, one seldom uses chains explicitly; rather, one uses them to prove
algebraic theorems that simplify topological calculations. Indeed, if one focuses on
singular chains, then one eschews chain level computations in principle as well as
in practice.
We here recall some classical results in homological algebra that explain how
to calculate H‚àó(X; œÄ) from H‚àó(X) ‚â°H‚àó(X; Z) and how to calculate H‚àó(X √ó Y )
from H‚àó(X)‚äóH‚àó(Y ). We then say a little about cochain complexes in preparation
for the deÔ¨Ånition of cohomology groups.
We again work over a general commutative ring R, although the main example
will be R = Z. Tensor products are understood to be taken over R.
1. Universal coeÔ¨Écients in homology
Let X and Y be chain complexes over R. We think of H‚àó(X) ‚äóH‚àó(Y ) as a
graded R-module which, in degree n, is P

by Œ±([x] ‚äó[y]) = [x ‚äóy] for cycles x and y that represent homology classes [x] and
[y]. As a special case, for an R-module M we have
Œ± : H‚àó(X) ‚äóM ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(X ‚äóM).
We omit the proof of the following standard result, but we shall shortly give
the quite similar proof of a cohomological analogue. Recall that an R-module M is
said to be Ô¨Çat if the functor M ‚äóN is exact (that is, preserves exact sequences in
the variable N). We say that a graded R-module is Ô¨Çat if each of its terms is Ô¨Çat.
We assume that the reader has seen torsion products, which measure the failure
of tensor products to be exact functors. For a principal ideal domain (PID) R, the
only torsion product is the Ô¨Årst one, denoted TorR

5. Relations between ‚äóand Hom
We shall need some observations about cochain complexes and tensor products,
and we Ô¨Årst recall some general facts about the category of R-modules. For R-
modules L, M, and N, we have an adjunction
Hom(L ‚äóM, N) ‚àº= Hom(L, Hom(M, N)).
We also have a natural homomorphism
Hom(L, M) ‚äóN ‚àí‚ÜíHom(L, M ‚äóN),
and this is an isomorphism if either L or N is a Ô¨Ånitely generated projective R-
module. Again, we have a natural map
Hom(L, M) ‚äóHom(L‚Ä≤, M ‚Ä≤) ‚àí‚ÜíHom(L ‚äóL‚Ä≤, M ‚äóM ‚Ä≤),
which is an isomorphism if L and L‚Ä≤ are Ô¨Ånitely generated and projective or if L is
Ô¨Ånitely generated and projective and M = R.
We can replace L and L‚Ä≤ by chain complexes and obtain similar maps, inserting
signs where needed. For example, a chain homotopy X ‚äóI ‚àí‚ÜíX‚Ä≤ between chain
maps f, g : X ‚àí‚ÜíX‚Ä≤ induces a chain map
Hom(X‚Ä≤, M) ‚àí‚ÜíHom(X ‚äóI , M) ‚àº= Hom(I , Hom(X, M)) ‚àº= Hom(X, M) ‚äóI ‚àó,
where I ‚àó= Hom(I , R). It should be clear that this implies that our original
chain homotopy induces a homotopy of cochain maps
f ‚àó‚âÉg‚àó: Hom(X‚Ä≤, M) ‚àí‚ÜíHom(X, M).
If Y and Y ‚Ä≤ are cochain complexes, then we have the natural homomorphism
Œ± : H‚àó(Y ) ‚äóH‚àó(Y ‚Ä≤) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(Y ‚äóY ‚Ä≤)
given by Œ±([y]‚äó[y‚Ä≤]) = [y‚äóy‚Ä≤], exactly as for chain complexes. (In fact, by regrading,
we may view this as a special case of the map for chain complexes.) The K¬®unneth
theorem applies to this map. For its Ô¨Çatness hypothesis, it is useful to remember
that, for any Noetherian ring R, the dual Hom(F, R) of a free R-module is a Ô¨Çat
R-module.
As indicated above, if Y = Hom(X, M) and Y ‚Ä≤ = Hom(X‚Ä≤, M ‚Ä≤) for chain
complexes X and X‚Ä≤ and R-modules M and M ‚Ä≤, then we also have the map of

CHAPTER 18
Axiomatic and cellular cohomology theory
We give a treatment of cohomology that is precisely parallel to our treatment
of homology. The essential new feature is the cup product structure that makes
the cohomology of X with coeÔ¨Écients in a commutative ring R a commutative
graded R-algebra. This additional structure ties together the cohomology groups
in diÔ¨Äerent degrees and is fundamentally important to most of the applications.
1. Axioms for cohomology

all other integers.

2. Cellular and singular cohomology
We deÔ¨Åne the cellular cochains of a CW pair (X, A) with coeÔ¨Écients in an
Abelian group œÄ to be
C‚àó(X, A; œÄ) = Hom(C‚àó(X, A), œÄ).
We then deÔ¨Åne the cellular cohomology groups to be
H‚àó(X, A; œÄ) = H‚àó(C‚àó(X, A; œÄ)).
If M is a module over a commutative ring R, we have a natural identiÔ¨Åcation
C‚àó(X, A; M) ‚àº= HomR(C‚àó(X, A) ‚äóR, M)
which allows us to do homological algebra over R rather than over Z when conve-
nient. In particular, if R is a Ô¨Åeld, then
H‚àó(X, A; M) ‚àº= HomR(H‚àó(X, A; R), M).
In general, with R = Z, we have a natural and splittable short exact sequence
0 ‚àí‚ÜíExt1
Z(Hn‚àí1(X, A), œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHn(X, A; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHom(Hn(X, A), œÄ) ‚àí‚Üí0.
The veriÔ¨Åcation of the axioms listed in the previous section is immediate, as
in homology. The fact that cellularly homotopic maps induce the same map on
cohomology uses our observations relating homotopies of chain complexes with
homotopies of cochain complexes. For exactness, the fact that our chain complexes
are free over Z implies that we have a short exact sequence of cochain complexes
0 ‚àí‚ÜíC‚àó(X, A; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíC‚àó(X; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíC‚àó(A; œÄ) ‚àí‚Üí0.
The required natural long exact sequence follows. The rest is the same as in ho-
mology.
For general spaces X, we can use ŒìX = |S‚àóX| as a canonical CW approxi-

3. Cup products in cohomology

that knowledge of the cohomological behavior of a map f : RP m ‚àí‚ÜíRP n on coho-

We assume that m ‚â•1 and n ‚â•1 to avoid triviality.
Proposition. Let f : RP m ‚àí‚ÜíRP n be a map such that f‚àó: œÄ1(RP m) ‚àí‚Üí
œÄ1(RP n) is non-zero. Then m ‚â§n.
Proof. Since œÄ1(RP 1) = Z and œÄ1(RP m) = Z2 if m ‚â•2, the result is certainly

Thus assume that n > 1 and assume for a contradiction that

H1(RP n; Z) is non-zero. By our universal coeÔ¨Écient theorems, the same is true
for mod 2 homology and for mod 2 cohomology.
That is, if x is the non-zero
element of H1(RP n; Z2), then f ‚àó(x) is the non-zero element of H1(RP m; Z2). By
the naturality of cup products
(f ‚àó(x))m = f ‚àó(xm).
However, the left side is non-zero in Hm(RP m; Z2) and the right side is zero since
xm = 0 by our assumption that m > n. The contradiction establishes the conclu-
sion.

5. Obstruction theory
We give an outline of one of the most striking features of cohomology: the
cohomology groups of a space X with coeÔ¨Écients in the homotopy groups of a
space Y control the construction of homotopy classes of maps X ‚àí‚ÜíY . As a
matter of motivation, this helps explain why one is interested in general coeÔ¨Écient
groups.
It also explains why the letter œÄ is so often used to denote coeÔ¨Écient
groups.
Definition. Fix n ‚â•1. A connected space X is said to be n-simple if œÄ1(X)
is Abelian and acts trivially on the homotopy groups œÄq(X) for q ‚â§n; X is said to
be simple if it is n-simple for all n.
Let (X, A) be a relative CW complex with relative skeleta Xn and let Y be an
n-simple space. The assumption on Y has the eÔ¨Äect that we need not worry about
basepoints. Let f : Xn ‚àí‚ÜíY be a map. We ask when f can be extended to a map
Xn+1 ‚àí‚ÜíY that restricts to the given map on A.
If we compose the attaching maps Sn ‚ÜíX of cells of X \ A with f, we obtain
elements of œÄn(Y ). These elements specify a well deÔ¨Åned ‚Äúobstruction cocycle‚Äù
cf ‚ààCn+1(X, A; œÄn(Y )).
Clearly, by considering extensions cell by cell, f extends to Xn+1 if and only if
cf = 0.
This is not a computable criterion.
However, if we allow ourselves to
modify f a little, then we can reÔ¨Åne the criterion to a cohomological one that often
is computable. If f and f ‚Ä≤ are maps Xn ‚ÜíY and h is a homotopy rel A of the
restrictions of f and f ‚Ä≤ to Xn‚àí1, then f, f ‚Ä≤, and h together deÔ¨Åne a map
h(f, f ‚Ä≤) : (X √ó I)n ‚àí‚ÜíY.
Applying ch(f,f ‚Ä≤) to cells j √ó I, we obtain a ‚Äúdeformation cochain‚Äù
df,f ‚Ä≤,h ‚ààCn(X, A; œÄn(Y ))
such that Œ¥df,f ‚Ä≤,h = cf ‚àícf ‚Ä≤. Moreover, given f and d, there exists f ‚Ä≤ that coin-
cides with f on Xn‚àí1 and satisÔ¨Åes df,f ‚Ä≤ = d, where the constant homotopy h is
understood. This gives the following result.
Theorem. For f : Xn ‚àí‚ÜíY , the restriction of f to Xn‚àí1 extends to a map
Xn+1 ‚ÜíY if and only if [cf] = 0 in Hn+1(X, A; œÄn(Y )).
It is natural to ask further when such extensions are unique up to homotopy,
and a similar argument gives the answer.
Theorem. Given maps f, f ‚Ä≤ : Xn ‚ÜíY and a homotopy rel A of their restric-
tions to Xn‚àí1, there is an obstruction class in Hn(X, A; œÄn(Y )) that vanishes if
and only if the restriction of the given homotopy to Xn‚àí2 extends to a homotopy
f ‚âÉf ‚Ä≤ rel A.

5. OBSTRUCTION THEORY

(2) Show that there is an isomorphism
H‚àó(K(œÄ, 1); A) ‚àº= Ext‚àó
Z[œÄ](Z, A).
When A is a commutative ring, the Ext groups have algebraically deÔ¨Åned
products, constructed as follows. The evident isomorphism Z ‚àº= Z ‚äóZ is
covered by a map of free Z[œÄ]-resolutions P ‚àí‚ÜíP ‚äóP, where Z[œÄ] acts
diagonally on tensor products, Œ±(x ‚äóy) = Œ±x ‚äóŒ±y. This chain map is
unique up to chain homotopy. It induces a map of chain complexes

on pairs of spaces and give some deductions from the axioms. This may be viewed

CHAPTER 20
The Poincar¬¥e duality theorem
The crucial starting point for applications of algebraic topology to geometric
topology is the Poincar¬¥e duality theorem. It gives a tight algebraic constraint on
the homology and cohomology groups of compact manifolds.
1. Statement of the theorem
It is apparent that there is a kind of duality relating the construction of ho-
mology and cohomology. In its simplest form, this is reÔ¨Çected by the fact that
evaluation of cochains on chains gives a natural homomorphism
Cp(X; œÄ) ‚äóCp(X; œÅ) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ ‚äóœÅ.
This passes to homology and cohomology to give an evaluation pairing
Hp(X; œÄ) ‚äóHp(X; œÅ) ‚àí‚ÜíœÄ ‚äóœÅ.
Taking œÄ = œÅ to be a commutative ring R and using its product, there results a
pairing
Hp(X; R) ‚äóR Hp(X; R) ‚àí‚ÜíR.
It is usually written ‚ü®Œ±, x‚ü©for Œ± ‚ààHp(X; R) and x ‚ààHp(X; R). When R is a Ô¨Åeld
and the Hp(X; R) are Ô¨Ånite dimensional vector spaces, the adjoint of this pairing
is an isomorphism
Hp(X; R) ‚àº= HomR(Hp(X; R), R).
That is, the cohomology groups of X are the vector space duals of the homology
groups of X.
Now let M be a compact manifold of dimension n. We shall study manifolds
without boundary in this chapter, turning to manifolds with boundary in the next.
We do not assume that M is diÔ¨Äerentiable. It is known that M can be given the
structure of a Ô¨Ånite CW complex, and its homology and cohomology groups are
therefore Ô¨Ånitely generated. When M is diÔ¨Äerentiable, it is not hard to prove this
using Morse theory, but it is a deep theorem in the general topological case. We
shall not go into the proof but shall take the result as known.
We have the cup product
Hp(M; R) ‚äóHn‚àíp(M; R) ‚àí‚ÜíHn(M; R).
If R is a Ô¨Åeld and M is ‚ÄúR-orientable,‚Äù then there is an ‚ÄúR-fundamental class‚Äù
z ‚ààHn(M; R). The composite of the cup product and evaluation on z gives a cup
product pairing
Hp(M; R) ‚äóHn‚àíp(M; R) ‚àí‚ÜíR.
One version of the Poincar¬¥e duality theorem asserts that this pairing is nonsingular,
so that its adjoint is an isomorphism
Hp(M; R) ‚àº= HomR(Hn‚àíp(M; R), R) ‚àº= Hn‚àíp(M; R).
151

152
THE POINCAR¬¥E DUALITY THEOREM
In fact, Poincar¬¥e duality does not require the commutative ring R to be a Ô¨Åeld, and
it is useful to allow R-modules œÄ as coeÔ¨Écients in our homology and cohomology
groups. We shall gradually make sense of and prove the following theorem.
Theorem (Poincar¬¥e duality). Let M be a compact R-oriented n-manifold.
Then, for an R-module œÄ, there is an isomorphism
D : Hp(M; œÄ) ‚àí‚ÜíHn‚àíp(M; œÄ).
We shall deÔ¨Åne the notion of an R-orientation and an R-fundamental class of
a manifold in ¬ß3, and we shall later prove the following result.
Proposition. If M is a compact n-manifold, then an R-orientation of M
determines and is determined by an R-fundamental class z ‚ààHn(M; R).
The isomorphism D is given by the adjoint of the cup product pairing deter-
mined by z, but it is more convenient to describe it in terms of the ‚Äúcap product.‚Äù
For any space X and R-module œÄ, there is a cap product
‚à©: Hp(X; œÄ) ‚äóR Hn(X; R) ‚àí‚ÜíHn‚àíp(X; œÄ).
We shall deÔ¨Åne it in the next section. The isomorphism D is speciÔ¨Åed by
D(Œ±) = Œ± ‚à©z.
When œÄ = R, we shall prove that the cap product, cup product, and evaluation
pairing are related by the fundamental identity
‚ü®Œ± ‚à™Œ≤, x‚ü©= ‚ü®Œ≤, Œ± ‚à©x‚ü©.
Taking x = z, this shows that in this case D is adjoint to the cup product pairing
determined by z.
We explain a few consequences before beginning to Ô¨Åll in the details and proofs.

Hn(M) ‚àº= Z with generator Œ∂ dual to z, ‚ü®Œ∂, z‚ü©= 1. The relation between ‚à™and ‚à©

Corollary. Let Tp ‚äÇHp(M) be the torsion subgroup. The cup product pair-

3. Orientations and fundamental classes
Let M be an n-manifold, not necessarily compact; the extra generality will be
crucial to our proof of the Poincar¬¥e duality theorem. For x ‚ààM, we can choose
a coordinate chart U ‚àº= Rn with x ‚ààU. By excision, exactness, and homotopy
invariance, we have isomorphisms
Hi(M, M ‚àíx) ‚àº= Hi(U, U ‚àíx) ‚àº= ÀúHi‚àí1(U ‚àíx) ‚àº= ÀúHi‚àí1(Sn‚àí1).

156
THE POINCAR¬¥E DUALITY THEOREM
This holds with any coeÔ¨Écient group, but we agree to take coeÔ¨Écients in a given
commutative ring R. Thus Hi(M, M ‚àíx) = 0 if i Ã∏= n and Hn(M, M ‚àíx) ‚àº=
R.
We think of Hn(M, M ‚àíx) as a free R-module on one generator, but the
generator (which corresponds to a unit of the ring R) is unspeciÔ¨Åed. Intuitively, an
R-orientation of M is a consistent choice of generators.
Definition. An R-fundamental class of M at a subspace X is an element
z ‚ààHn(M, M ‚àíX) such that, for each x ‚ààX, the image of z under the map
Hn(M, M ‚àíX) ‚àí‚ÜíHn(M, M ‚àíx)
induced by the inclusion (M, M ‚àíX) ‚àí‚Üí(M, M ‚àíx) is a generator. If X = M,
we refer to z ‚ààHn(M) as a fundamental class of M. An R-orientation of M is an
open cover {Ui} and R-fundamental classes zi of M at Ui such that if Ui ‚à©Uj is
non-empty, then ui and uj map to the same element of Hn(M, M ‚àíUi ‚à©Uj).
We say that M is R-orientable if it admits an R-orientation. When R = Z,
we refer to orientations and orientability.
There are various equivalent ways of
formulating these notions. We leave it as an exercise for the reader to reconcile
the present deÔ¨Ånition of orientability with any other deÔ¨Ånition he or she may have
seen.
Clearly an R-fundamental class z determines an R-orientation: given any open
cover {Ui}, we take zi to be the image of z in Hn(M, M ‚àíUi). The converse holds
when M is compact. To show this, we need the following vanishing theorem, which
we shall prove in the next section.
Theorem (Vanishing). Let M be an n-manifold. For any coeÔ¨Écient group œÄ,
Hi(M; œÄ) = 0 if i > n, and ÀúHn(M; œÄ) = 0 if M is connected and is not compact.
We can use this together with Mayer-Vietoris sequences to construct R-fun-
damental classes at compact subspaces from R-orientations. To avoid trivialities,
we tacitly assume that n > 0. (The trivial case n = 0 forced the use of reduced
homology in the statement; where arguments use reduced homology below, it is
only to ensure that what we write is correct in dimension zero.)
Theorem. Let K be a compact subset of M. Then, for any coeÔ¨Écient group
œÄ, Hi(M, M ‚àíK; œÄ) = 0 if i > n, and an R-orientation of M determines an R-
fundamental class of M at K. In particular, if M is compact, then an R-orientation
of M determines an R-fundamental class of M.
Proof. First assume that K is contained in a coordinate chart U ‚àº= Rn. By
excision and exactness, we then have
Hi(M, M ‚àíK; œÄ) ‚àº= Hi(U, U ‚àíK; œÄ) ‚àº= ÀúHi‚àí1(U ‚àíK; œÄ).
Since U ‚àíK is open in U, the vanishing theorem implies that ÀúHi‚àí1(U ‚àíK; œÄ) = 0
for i > n. In fact, a lemma used in the proof of the vanishing theorem will prove
this directly. In this case, an R-fundamental class in Hn(M, M ‚àíU) maps to an
R-fundamental class in Hn(M, M ‚àíK). A general compact subset K of M can be
written as the union of Ô¨Ånitely many compact subsets, each of which is contained
in a coordinate chart. By induction, it suÔ¨Éces to prove the result for K ‚à™L under
the assumption that it holds for K, L, and K ‚à©L. With any coeÔ¨Écients, we have

4. The proof of the vanishing theorem
Let M be an n-manifold, n > 0. Take all homology groups with coeÔ¨Écients
in a given Abelian group œÄ in this section. We must prove the intuitively obvious
statement that Hi(M) = 0 for i > n and the much more subtle statement that
Hn(M) = 0 if M is connected and is not compact. The last statement is perhaps
the technical heart of our proof of the Poincar¬¥e duality theorem.
We begin with the general observation that homology is ‚Äúcompactly supported‚Äù
in the sense of the following result.
Lemma. For any space X and element x ‚ààHq(X), there is a compact subspace
K of X and an element k ‚ààHq(K) that maps to x.
Proof. Let Œ≥ : Y ‚àí‚ÜíX be a CW approximation of X and let x = Œ≥‚àó(y). If y
is represented by a cycle z ‚ààCq(Y ), then z, as a Ô¨Ånite linear combination of q-cells,
is an element of Cq(L) for some Ô¨Ånite subcomplex L of Y . Let K = Œ≥(L) and let
k be the image of the homology class represented by z. Then K is compact and k
maps to x.
‚ñ°
We need two lemmas about open subsets of Rn to prove the vanishing theorem,
the Ô¨Årst of which is just a special case.
Lemma. If U is open in Rn, then Hi(U) = 0 for i ‚â•n.
Proof. Let s ‚ààHi(U), i ‚â•n. There is a compact subspace K of U and an
element k ‚ààHi(K) that maps to s. We may decompose Rn as a CW complex
whose n-cells are small n-cubes in such a way that there is a Ô¨Ånite subcomplex L of
Rn with K ‚äÇL ‚äÇU. (To be precise, use a cubical grid with small enough mesh.)
For i > 0, the connecting homomorphisms ‚àÇare isomorphisms in the commutative
diagram
Hi+1(Rn, L)

/ Hi(U).
Since (Rn, L) has no relative q-cells for q > n, the groups on the left are zero for
i ‚â•n. Since s is in the image of Hi(L), s = 0.

Lemma. Let U be open in Rn. Suppose that t ‚ààHn(Rn, U) maps to zero in
Hn(Rn, Rn ‚àíx) for all x ‚ààRn ‚àíU. Then t = 0.
Proof. We prove the equivalent statement that if s ‚ààÀúHn‚àí1(U) maps to zero
in ÀúHn‚àí1(Rn ‚àíx) for all x ‚ààRn ‚àíU, then s = 0. Choose a compact subspace K
of U such that s is in the image of ÀúHn‚àí1(K). Then K is contained in an open

an element r ‚ààÀúHn‚àí1(V ). We claim that r maps to zero in ÀúHn‚àí1(U), so that s = 0.

5. The proof of the Poincar¬¥e duality theorem
Let M be an R-oriented n-manifold, not necessarily compact. Unless otherwise
speciÔ¨Åed, we take homology and cohomology with coeÔ¨Écients in a given R-module
œÄ in this section. Remember that homology is a covariant functor with compact
supports. Cohomology is a contravariant functor, and it does not have compact
supports. We would like to prove the Poincar¬¥e duality theorem by inductive com-
parisons of Mayer-Vietoris sequences, and the opposite variance of homology and
cohomology makes it unclear how to proceed. To get around this, we introduce a
variant of cohomology that does have compact supports and has enough covariant
functoriality to allow us to proceed by comparisons of Mayer-Vietoris sequences.
Consider the set K of compact subspaces K of M. This set is directed under
inclusion; to conform with our earlier discussion of colimits, we may view K as
a category whose objects are the compact subspaces K and whose maps are the
inclusions between them. We deÔ¨Åne
Hq
c (M) = colim Hq(M, M ‚àíK),
where the colimit is taken with respect to the homomorphisms
Hq(M, M ‚àíK) ‚àí‚ÜíHq(M, M ‚àíL)
induced by the inclusions (M, M ‚àíL) ‚äÇ(M, M ‚àíK) for K ‚äÇL. This is the
cohomology of M with compact supports. Intuitively, thinking in terms of singular
cohomology, its elements are represented by cocycles that vanish oÔ¨Äsome compact
subspace.

We can use homology with coeÔ¨Écients in a commutative ring R to construct

It depends on the units of R.

oriented. The evident ring homomorphism Z ‚àí‚ÜíR induces a natural homomor-

CHAPTER 21
The index of manifolds; manifolds with boundary
The Poincar¬¥e duality theorem imposes strong constraints on the Euler char-
acteristic of a manifold. It also leads to new invariants, most notably the index.
Moreover, there is a relative version of Poincar¬¥e duality in the context of manifolds
with boundary, and this leads to necessary algebraic conditions on the cohomology
of a manifold that must be satisÔ¨Åed if it is to be a boundary. In particular, the index
of a compact oriented 4n-manifold M is zero if M is a boundary. We shall later
outline the theory of cobordism, which leads to necessary and suÔ¨Écient algebraic
conditions for a manifold to be a boundary.
1. The Euler characteristic of compact manifolds
The Euler characteristic œá(X) of a space with Ô¨Ånitely generated homology is
deÔ¨Åned by
œá(X) = P
i(‚àí1)i rank Hi(X; Z).
The universal coeÔ¨Écient theorem implies that
œá(X) = P
i(‚àí1)i dim Hi(X; F)
for any Ô¨Åeld of coeÔ¨Écients F. Examination of the relevant short exact sequences
shows that
œá(X) = P
i(‚àí1)i rank Ci(X; Z)
for any decomposition of X as a Ô¨Ånite CW complex. The veriÔ¨Åcations of these
statements are immediate from earlier exercises.
Now consider a compact oriented n-manifold. Recall that we take it for granted
that M can be decomposed as a Ô¨Ånite CW complex, so that each Hi(M; Z) is Ô¨Ånitely
generated. By the universal coeÔ¨Écient theorem and Poincar¬¥e duality, we have
Hi(M; F) ‚àº= Hi(M; F) ‚àº= Hn‚àíi(M; F)
for any Ô¨Åeld F. We may take F = Z2, and so dispense with the requirement that
M be oriented. If n is odd, the summands of œá(M) cancel in pairs, and we obtain
the following conclusion.
Proposition. If M is a compact manifold of odd dimension, then œá(M) = 0.
If n = 2m and M is oriented, then
œá(M) = Pm‚àí1
i=0 (‚àí1)i2 dim Hi(M) + (‚àí1)m dim Hm(M)
for any Ô¨Åeld F of coeÔ¨Écients.
Let us take F = Q.
Of course, we can replace
homology by cohomology in the deÔ¨Ånition and formulas for œá(M). The middle
dimensional cohomology group Hm(M) plays a particularly important role. Recall
that we have the cup product pairing

Lemma. Let F be a Ô¨Åeld of characteristic Ã∏= 2, V be a Ô¨Ånite dimensional vector

Then V has a basis {x1, . . ., xr, y1, . . ., yr} such that œÜ(xi, yi) = 1 for 1 ‚â§i ‚â§r and
œÜ(z, w) = 0 for all other pairs of basis elements (z, w). Therefore the dimension of
V is even.
Proof. We proceed by induction on dim V , and we may assume that V Ã∏= 0.
Since œÜ(x, y) = ‚àíœÜ(y, x), œÜ(x, x) = 0 for all x ‚ààV . Choose x1 Ã∏= 0. Certainly there
exists y1 such that œÜ(x1, y1) = 1, and x1 and y1 are then linearly independent.
DeÔ¨Åne

(œÜ(x, x1), œÜ(x, y1)). Since œà(x1) = (0, 1) and œà(y1) = (‚àí1, 0), œà is an epimorphism.
Thus dim W = dim V ‚àí2. Since œÜ restricts to a nonsingular skew symmetric bilinear

2. The index of compact oriented manifolds
To study manifolds of dimension 4k, we consider an analogue for symmetric
bilinear forms of the previous algebraic lemma. Since we will need to take square
roots, we will work over R.
Lemma. Let V be a Ô¨Ånite dimensional real vector space and œÜ : V √ó V ‚àí‚ÜíR
be a nonsingular symmetric bilinear form. DeÔ¨Åne q(x) = œÜ(x, x). Then V has
a basis {x1, . . ., xr, y1, . . ., ys} such that œÜ(z, w) = 0 for all pairs (z, w) of distinct
basis elements, q(xi) = 1 for 1 ‚â§i ‚â§r and q(yj) = ‚àí1 for 1 ‚â§j ‚â§s. The number
r ‚àís is an invariant of œÜ, called the signature of œÜ.
Proof. We proceed by induction on dim V , and we may assume that V Ã∏= 0.
Clearly q(rx) = r2q(x). Since we can take square roots in R, we can choose x1 ‚ààV
such that q(x1) = ¬±1. DeÔ¨Åne œà : V ‚àí‚ÜíR by œà(x) = œÜ(x, x1) and let W = ker œà.
Since œà(x1) = ¬±1, œà is an epimorphism and dim W = dim V ‚àí1. Since œÜ restricts
to a nonsingular symmetric bilinear form on W, the existence of a basis as speciÔ¨Åed
follows directly from the induction hypothesis. Invariance means that the integer
r ‚àís is independent of the choice of basis on which q takes values ¬±1, and we leave
the veriÔ¨Åcation to the reader.

3. Manifolds with boundary
Let Hn = {(x1, . . ., xn)|xn ‚â•0} be the upper half-plane in Rn. Recall that an
n-manifold with boundary is a HausdorÔ¨Äspace M having a countable basis of open
sets such that every point of M has a neighborhood homeomorphic to an open
subset of Hn. A point x is an interior point if it has a neighborhood homeomorphic
to an open subset of Hn ‚àí‚àÇHn ‚àº= Rn; otherwise it is a boundary point. It is a fact
called ‚Äúinvariance of domain‚Äù that if U and V are homeomorphic subspaces of Rn
and U is open, then V is open. Therefore, a homeomorphism of an open subspace
of Hn onto an open subspace of Hn carries boundary points to boundary points.
We denote the boundary of an n-manifold M by ‚àÇM. Thus M is a manifold
without boundary if ‚àÇM is empty; M is said to be closed if, in addition, it is
compact. The space ‚àÇM is an (n ‚àí1)-manifold without boundary.
It is a fundamental question in topology to determine which closed manifolds
are boundaries. The question makes sense with varying kinds of extra structure. For
example, we can ask whether or not a smooth (= diÔ¨Äerentiable) closed manifold is
the boundary of a smooth manifold (with the induced smooth structure). Numerical
invariants in algebraic topology give criteria. One such criterion is given by the
following consequence of the Poincar¬¥e duality theorem. Remember that œá(M) = 0
if M is a closed manifold of odd dimension.

5. The index of manifolds that are boundaries
We shall prove the following theorem.
Theorem. If M is the boundary of a compact oriented (4k +1)-manifold, then
I(M) = 0.
We Ô¨Årst give an algebraic criterion for the vanishing of the signature of a form
and then show that the cup product form on the middle dimensional cohomology

Lemma. Let W be a n-dimensional subspace of a 2n-dimensional real vector
space V . Let œÜ : V √ó V ‚àí‚ÜíR be a nonsingular symmetric bilinear form such that
œÜ : W √ó W ‚àí‚ÜíR is identically zero. Then the signature of œÜ is zero.
Proof. Let r and s be as in the deÔ¨Ånition of the signature. Then r + s = 2n
and we must show that r = s. We prove that r ‚â•n. Applied to the form ‚àíœÜ, this
will also give that s ‚â•n, implying the conclusion. We proceed by induction on n.
Let {x1, . . ., xn, z1, . . ., zn} be a basis for V , where {x1, . . ., xn} is a basis for W.

and

at least n, neither can have dimension more than n and Œ∏ and œà must both be
epimorphisms. Choose y1 such that Œ∏(y1) = (1, 0, . . ., 0). Let q(x) = œÜ(x, x) and

2a + q(y1) for a ‚ààR.

PROBLEMS
Let M be a compact connected n-manifold with boundary ‚àÇM, where n ‚â•2.
(1) Prove: ‚àÇM is not a retract of M.
(2) Prove: if M is contractible, then ‚àÇM has the homology of a sphere.
(3) Assume that M is orientable. Let n = 2m + 1 and let K be the kernel of
the homomorphism Hm(‚àÇM) ‚àí‚ÜíHm(M) induced by the inclusion, where
homology is taken with coeÔ¨Écients in a Ô¨Åeld. Prove: dim Hm(‚àÇM) =
2 dim K.
Let n = 3 in the rest of the problems.
4. Prove: if M is orientable, ‚àÇM is empty, and H1(M; Z) = 0, then M has
the same homology groups as a 3-sphere.
5. Prove: if M is nonorientable and ‚àÇM is empty, then H1(M; Z) is inÔ¨Ånite.
(Hint for the last three problems: use the standard classiÔ¨Åcation of closed 2-
manifolds and think about Ô¨Årst homology groups.)
6. Prove: if M is orientable and H1(M; Z) = 0, then ‚àÇM is a disjoint union
of 2-spheres.
7. Prove: if M is orientable, ‚àÇM Ã∏= œÜ, and ‚àÇM contains no 2-spheres, then
H1(M; Z) is inÔ¨Ånite.

CHAPTER 22
Homology, cohomology, and K(œÄ, n)s
We have given an axiomatic deÔ¨Ånition of ordinary homology and cohomology,
and we have shown how to realize the axioms by means of either cellular or singu-
lar chain and cochain complexes. We here give a homotopical way of constructing
ordinary theories that makes no use of chains, whether cellular or singular. We also
show how to construct cup and cap products homotopically. This representation
of homology and cohomology in terms of Eilenberg-Mac Lane spaces is the starting
point of the modern approach to homology and cohomology theory, and we shall
indicate how theories that do not satisfy the dimension axiom can be represented.
We shall also describe Postnikov systems, which give a way to approximate general
(simple) spaces by weakly equivalent spaces built up out of Eilenberg-Mac Lane
spaces. This is conceptually dual to the way that CW complexes allow the ap-
proximation of spaces by weakly equivalent spaces built up out of spheres. Finally,
we present the important notion of cohomology operations and relate them to the
cohomology of Eilenberg-Mac Lane spaces.
1. K(œÄ, n)s and homology
Recall that a reduced homology theory on based CW complexes is a sequence
of functors ÀúEq from the homotopy category of based CW complexes to the category
of Abelian groups. Each ÀúEq must satisfy the exactness and additivity axioms, and
there must be a natural suspension isomorphism. Up to isomorphism, ordinary
reduced homology with coeÔ¨Écients in œÄ is characterized as the unique such theory
that satisÔ¨Åes the dimension axiom:
ÀúE0(S0) = œÄ and ÀúEq(S0) = 0 if q Ã∏= 0. We
proceed to construct such a theory homotopically.
For based spaces X and Y , we let [X, Y ] denote the set of based homotopy
classes of based maps X ‚àí‚ÜíY . Recall that we require Eilenberg-Mac Lane spaces
K(œÄ, n) to have the homotopy types of CW complexes and that, up to homotopy
equivalence, there is a unique such space for each n and œÄ. By a result of Milnor, if
X has the homotopy type of a CW complex, then so does ‚Ñ¶X. By the Whitehead
theorem, we therefore have a homotopy equivalence

This map is the adjoint of a map
œÉ : Œ£K(œÄ, n) ‚àí‚ÜíK(œÄ, n + 1).

use the suspension homomorphism on homotopy groups to obtain maps

2. K(œÄ, n)s and cohomology
The homotopical description of ordinary cohomology theories is both simpler
and more important to the applications than the homotopical description of ordi-
nary homology theories.
Theorem. For CW complexes X, Abelian groups œÄ, and integers n ‚â•0, there
are natural isomorphisms

3. Cup and cap products
Changing notations, let A and B be Abelian groups and X and Y be based

to obtain a cup product

4. Postnikov systems
We have implicitly studied the represented functors k(X) = [X, Y ] by decom-

cohomology [X, K(œÄ, n)] by means of cellular chains. There is an Eckmann-Hilton
dual way of studying [X, Y ] by decomposing Y into ‚Äúcocells.‚Äù We brieÔ¨Çy describe
this decomposition of spaces into their ‚ÄúPostnikov systems‚Äù here.
This decomposition answers a natural question: how close are the homotopy
groups of a CW complex X to being a complete set of invariants for its homotopy
type? Since Q

not weakly homotopy equivalent to it, some added information is needed. If X is
simple, it turns out that the homotopy groups together with an inductively deÔ¨Åned
sequence of cohomology classes give a complete set of invariants.

acts trivially on œÄn(X) for n ‚â•2. A Postnikov system for a simple based space X

5. Cohomology operations
Consider a ‚Äúrepresented functor‚Äù k(X) = [X, Z] and another contravariant
functor k‚Ä≤ from the homotopy category of based CW complexes to the category
of sets. The following simple observation actually applies to represented functors
on arbitrary categories. We shall use it to describe cohomology operations, but it
also applies to describe many other invariants in algebraic topology, such as the
characteristic classes of vector bundles.
Lemma (Yoneda). There is a canonical bijection between natural transforma-
tions Œ¶ : k ‚àí‚Üík‚Ä≤ and elements œÜ ‚ààk‚Ä≤(Z).
Proof. Given Œ¶, we deÔ¨Åne œÜ to be Œ¶(id), where id ‚ààk(Z) = [Z, Z] is the
identity map. Given œÜ, we deÔ¨Åne Œ¶ : k(X) ‚àí‚Üík‚Ä≤(X) by the formula Œ¶(f) = f ‚àó(œÜ).
Here f is a map X ‚àí‚ÜíZ, and it induces f ‚àó= k‚Ä≤(f) : k‚Ä≤(Z) ‚àí‚Üík‚Ä≤(X). It is simple
to check that these are inverse bijections.

CHAPTER 23
Characteristic classes of vector bundles
Some of the most remarkable applications of algebraic topology result from the
translation of problems in geometric topology into problems in homotopy theory.
The essential intermediary in many of these translations is the theory of vector
bundles. We here explain the classiÔ¨Åcation theorem for vector bundles and its rela-
tionship to the theory of characteristic classes. The reader is assumed to be familiar
with the tangent and normal bundles of smooth manifolds and to be reasonably
well acquainted with the deÔ¨Ånitions and elementary properties of vector bundles in
general.
1. The classiÔ¨Åcation of vector bundles
Let E be a (real) vector bundle over a base space B. Thus we are given a
projection p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB such that, for each b ‚ààB, the Ô¨Åber p‚àí1(b) is a copy of
Rn for some n. In the case of non-connected base spaces, the Ô¨Åbers over points in
diÔ¨Äerent components may have diÔ¨Äerent dimension. We say that p is an n-plane
bundle if all Ô¨Åbers have dimension n. For each U in some open cover of B, there
is a homeomorphism (a ‚Äúcoordinate chart‚Äù) œÜU : U √ó Rn ‚àí‚Üíp‚àí1(U) over U that
restricts to a linear isomorphism on each Ô¨Åber. We shall require our open covers
to be numerable, as can always be arranged when B is paracompact. For a second
vector bundle q : D ‚àí‚ÜíA, a map (g, f) : D ‚àí‚ÜíE of vector bundles is a pair of
maps f : A ‚àí‚ÜíB and g : D ‚àí‚ÜíE such that p ‚ó¶g = f ‚ó¶q and g : q‚àí1(a) ‚àí‚Üí
p‚àí1(f(a)) is linear for all a ‚ààA. This gives the category of vector bundles. A map
(g, f) of vector bundles is an isomorphism if and only if f is a homeomorphism
and g restricts to an isomorphism on each Ô¨Åber. We are mainly interested in the
subcategories of n-plane bundles and maps that are linear isomorphisms on Ô¨Åbers.
We say that two vector bundles over B are equivalent if they are isomorphic over B,
so that there is an isomorphism (g, id) between them. We let En(B) denote the set
of equivalence classes of n-plane bundles over B. (That this really is a well deÔ¨Åned
set will emerge shortly.) If f : A ‚àí‚ÜíB is a continuous map, then the pullback of
f and a vector bundle p : E ‚àí‚ÜíB is a vector bundle f ‚àóE over A. Moreover, a
bundle D over A is equivalent to f ‚àóE if and only if there is a map (g, f) : D ‚àí‚ÜíE
that is an isomorphism on Ô¨Åbers.
Thus we have a contravariant set-valued functor En(‚àí) on spaces. Vector bun-
dles should be thought of as rather rigid geometric objects, and the equivalence
relation between them preserves that rigidity. Nevertheless, equivalence classes of
n-plane bundles can be classiÔ¨Åed homotopically. This is a crucial starting point
for the translation of geometric problems to homotopical ones. In turn, the start-
ing point of the classiÔ¨Åcation theorem is the observation that the functor En(‚àí),
like homology and cohomology, is homotopy invariant in the sense that it factors

It too is a compact manifold.

Definition. Let k‚àóbe a cohomology theory, such as H‚àó(‚àí; œÄ) for an Abelian

back their external sum along the diagonal map of B.

3. Stiefel-Whitney classes of manifolds
It is convenient to consider H‚àó‚àó(X) = Q
i Hi(X) and to write its elements
as formal sums P xi, deg xi = i. In practice, we usually impose conditions that

a vector bundle Œæ to be P wi(Œæ); here the sum is clearly Ô¨Ånite. Note in particular

w(Œ∂ ‚äïŒæ) = w(Œ∂) ‚à™w(Œæ).

which shows how to calculate tangential Stiefel-Whitney classes in terms of nor-

the Whitney duality formula can lead to a contradiction if q is too small.

depends on its smooth structure, this is rather surprising.
4. Characteristic numbers of manifolds
Characteristic classes determine important numerical invariants of manifolds,
called their characteristic numbers.
Definition. Let M be a smooth closed R-oriented n-manifold with funda-

large. (These numbers are well deÔ¨Åned because any two embeddings of M in Rq
for large q are isotopic and have equivalent normal bundles.)

geometrically.
5. Thom spaces and the Thom isomorphism theorem
There are several ways to construct the Stiefel-Whitney classes.
The most
illuminating one depends on a simple, but fundamentally important, construction
on vector bundles, namely their ‚ÄúThom spaces.‚Äù This construction will also be at
the heart of the proof of Thom‚Äôs theorem in the last chapter.
Definition. Let Œæ : E ‚àí‚ÜíB be an n-plane bundle. Apply one-point com-

one-point compactiÔ¨Åcation and then identifying all of the points at ‚àûto a single
basepoint (denoted ‚àû). Observe that this construction is functorial with respect
to maps of vector bundles.
Remark. If we give the bundle Œæ a Euclidean metric and let D(E) and S(E)
denote its unit disk bundle and unit sphere bundle, then there is an evident homeo-
morphism between TŒæ and the quotient space D(E)/S(E). In turn, D(E)/S(E) is
homotopy equivalent to the coÔ¨Åber of the inclusion S(E) ‚àí‚ÜíD(E) and therefore
to the coÔ¨Åber of the projection S(E) ‚àí‚ÜíB.
If the bundle Œæ is trivial, so that E = B √ó Rn, then Sph(E) = B √ó Sn.
Quotienting out B amounts to the same thing as giving B a disjoint basepoint and
then forming the smash product B+ ‚àßSn. That is, in this case the Thom complex
is Œ£nB+. Therefore, for any cohomology theory k‚àó,

sequence is a device for computing the cohomology of the total space E of a Ô¨Åbration

deviation of H‚àó(E) from H‚àó(B)‚äóH‚àó(F). In the present situation, the existence of a

Just as in orientation theory for manifolds, the question of orientability depends
on the structure of the units of the ring R, and this leads to the following conclusion.
Proposition. Every vector bundle admits a unique Z2-orientation.
This can be proved along with the Thom isomorphism theorem by a Mayer-
Vietoris argument.
6. The construction of the Stiefel-Whitney classes
We indicate two constructions of the Stiefel-Whitney classes. Each has distinct
advantages over the other. First, taking the characteristic class point of view, we
deÔ¨Åne the Stiefel-Whitney classes in terms of the Steenrod operations by setting
wi(Œæ) = Œ¶‚àí1SqiŒ¶(1) = Œ¶‚àí1Sqi¬µ.
Naturality is obvious.
Axiom 1 is immediate from the relations Sq0 = id and
Sqi(x) = 0 if i > deg x. For axiom 2, we use the following observation.
Lemma. There is a homotopy equivalence j : RP ‚àû‚àí‚ÜíTŒ≥1.
Proof. TŒ≥1 is homeomorphic to D(Œ≥1)/S(Œ≥1). Here S(Œ≥1) is the inÔ¨Ånite sphere
S‚àû, which is the universal cover of RP ‚àûand is therefore contractible. The zero
section RP ‚àû‚àí‚ÜíD(Œ≥1) and the quotient map D(Œ≥1) ‚àí‚ÜíTŒ≥1 are homotopy equiv-
alences, and their composite is the required homotopy equivalence j.
‚ñ°
Since Sq1(x) = x2 if deg x = 1, the lemma implies that Sq1 is non-zero on the
Thom class of Œ≥1, verifying axiom 2. For axiom 3, we easily check that T(Œæ ‚äïŒµ) ‚àº=
Œ£T(Œæ) for any vector bundle Œæ and that the Thom class of Œæ ‚äïŒµ is the suspension
of the Thom class of Œæ. Thus axiom 3 follows from the stability of the Steenrod
operations. For axiom 4, we easily check that, for any vector bundles Œ∂ and Œæ,
T(Œ∂ √ó Œæ) ‚àº= TŒ∂ ‚àßTŒæ and the Thom class of Œ∂ √ó Œæ is the tensor product of the Thom
classes of Œ∂ and Œæ. Interpreting the Cartan formula for the Steenrod operations
externally in the cohomology of products and therefore of smash products, we
see that it implies axiom 4. That is, the properties that axiomatize the Steenrod
operations directly imply the properties that axiomatize the Stiefel-Whitney classes.
We next take the classifying space point of view. As we shall explain in ¬ß8,
passage from topological groups to their classifying spaces is a product-preserving
functor, at least up to homotopy. We may embed (Z2)n = O(1)n in O(n) as the
subgroup of diagonal matrices. The classifying space BO(1) is RP ‚àû, and we obtain
a map

as the subgroup of permutation matrices, and the diagonal subgroup O(1)n is closed
under conjugation by symmetric matrices. Application of the classifying space func-
tor to conjugation by permutation matrices induces the corresponding permutation
of the factors of BO(1)n, and it induces the identity map on BO(n). Indeed, up to
homotopy, inner conjugation by an element of G induces the identity map on BG
for any topological group G.

8. A glimpse at the general theory
We should place the theory of vector bundles in a more general context. We
have written BO(n), BU(n), and BSO(n) for certain ‚Äúclassifying spaces‚Äù in this
chapter, but we deÔ¨Åned a classifying space BG for any topological group G in
Chapter 16 ¬ß5. In fact, the spaces here are homotopy equivalent to the spaces of
the same name that we deÔ¨Åned there, and we here explain why.
Consider bundles Œæ : Y ‚àí‚ÜíB with Ô¨Åber G. For spaces U in a numerable open
cover O of B, there are homeomorphisms œÜ : U √óG ‚àí‚Üíp‚àí1(U) such that p‚ó¶œÜ = œÄ1.
We say that Y is a principal G-bundle if Y has a free right action by G, B is the
orbit space Y/G, Œæ is the quotient map, and the œÜ are maps of right G-spaces. We
say that Œæ : Y ‚àí‚ÜíB is a universal principal G-bundle if Y is a contractible space. In
particular, for any topological group G whose identity element is a nondegenerate
basepoint, such as any Lie group G, the map p : EG ‚àí‚ÜíBG constructed in
Chapter 16 ¬ß5 is a universal principal G-bundle. The classiÔ¨Åcation theorem below
implies that the base spaces of any two universal principal G-bundles are homotopy
equivalent, and it is usual to write BG for any space in this homotopy type. Observe
that the long exact sequence of homotopy groups of a universal principal G-bundle
gives isomorphisms œÄq(BG) ‚àº= œÄq‚àí1(G) for q ‚â•1.
We have implicitly constructed other examples of universal principal G-bundles
when G is O(n), U(n), or SO(n). To see this, consider Vn(Rq). Write Rq = Rn √ó

group O(q). Of course, O(q) acts on vectors in Rq and thus on n-frames. Consider
the Ô¨Åxed n-frame x0 = {e1, . . ., en}.
