PROBLEMS
(1) Verify that w(RP q) = 1 if and only if q = 2k ‚àí1 for some k.
(2) Prove that RP 2k cannot immerse in R2k+1‚àí2. (By the Whitney embedding
theorem, any smooth closed n-manifold immerses in R2n‚àí1, so this is a
best possible non-immersion result.)
(3) Prove that all tangential Stiefel-Whitney numbers of RP q are zero if and
only if q is odd.
(4) * Try to construct a smooth compact manifold whose boundary is RP 3.
(5) Prove that a smooth closed n-manifold M is R-orientable if and only its
tangent bundle is R-orientable.

CHAPTER 24
An introduction to K-theory
The Ô¨Årst generalized cohomology theory to be discovered was K-theory, and it
plays a vital role in the connection of algebraic topology to analysis and algebraic
geometry. The fact that it is a generalized cohomology theory is a consequence of
the Bott periodicity theorem, which is one of the most important and inÔ¨Çuential
theorems in all of topology. We give some basic information about K-theory and,
following Adams and Atiyah, we explain how the Adams operations in K-theory
allow a quick solution to the ‚ÄúHopf invariant one problem.‚Äù One implication is the
purely algebraic theorem that the only possible dimensions of a real (not necessarily
associative) division algebra are 1, 2, 4, and 8. We shall only discuss complex K-
theory, although there is a precisely analogous construction of real K-theory KO.
From the point of view of algebraic topology, real K-theory is a substantially more
powerful invariant, but complex K-theory is usually more relevant to applications
in other Ô¨Åelds.
1. The deÔ¨Ånition of K-theory
Except where otherwise noted, we work with complex vector bundles through-
out this chapter. Dimension will mean complex dimension and line bundles will
mean complex line bundles. We consider the set V ect(X) of equivalence classes
of vector bundles over a space X. We assume unless otherwise speciÔ¨Åed that X
is compact. We remind the reader that vector bundles can have diÔ¨Äerent dimen-
sion over diÔ¨Äerent components of X. The set V ect(X) forms an Abelian monoid
(= semi-group) under Whitney sum, and it forms a semi-ring with multiplication
given by the (internal) tensor product of vector bundles over X.
There is a standard construction, called the Grothendieck construction, of an
Abelian group G(M) associated to an Abelian monoid M: one takes the quotient of
the free Abelian group generated by the elements of M by the subgroup generated
by the set of elements of the form m + n ‚àím ‚äïn, where ‚äïis the sum in M.
The evident morphism of Abelian monoids i : M ‚àí‚ÜíG(M) is universal: for any
homomorphism of monoids f : M ‚àí‚ÜíG, where G is an Abelian group, there is a
unique homomorphism of groups Àúf : G(M) ‚àí‚ÜíG such that Àúf ‚ó¶i = f. If M is a
semi-ring, then its multiplication induces a multiplication on G(M) such that G(M)
is a ring, called the Grothendieck ring of M. If the semi-ring M is commutative,
then the ring G(M) is commutative.
Definition. The K-theory of X, denoted K(X), is the Grothendieck ring of
the semi-ring V ect(X). An element of K(X) is called a virtual bundle over X. We
write [Œæ] for the element of K(X) determined by a vector bundle Œæ.
Since Œµ is the identity element for the product in K(X), it is standard to write
q = [Œµq], where Œµq is the q-dimensional trivial bundle. For vector bundles over a
203

We have a homotopical interpretation of these deÔ¨Ånitions, and it is for this that

BU to be the colimit of the BU(n), with the topology of the union.
We say that bundles Œ∂ and Œæ are stably equivalent if, for a suÔ¨Éciently large q,
the bundles Œ∂ ‚äïŒµq‚àím and Œæ ‚äïŒµq‚àín are equivalent, where m = d(Œ∂) and n = d(Œæ).
Let E U(X) be the set of stable equivalence classes of vector bundles over X. If X

over the maps E Un(X) ‚àí‚ÜíE Un+1(X) obtained by sending a bundle Œæ to Œæ ‚äïŒµ.
Since a map from a compact space X into BU has image in one of the BU(n), and
similarly for homotopies, we see that in this case [X+, BU] ‚àº= colim[X+, BU(n)]
and therefore
E U(X) ‚àº= [X+, BU].
A deeper use of compactness gives the following basic fact.
Proposition. If Œæ : E ‚àí‚ÜíX is a vector bundle over X, then there is a bundle
Œ∑ over X such that Œæ ‚äïŒ∑ is equivalent to Œµq for some q.

map g : X √ó V ‚àí‚ÜíE speciÔ¨Åed by g(x, s) = s(x) is an epimorphism of bundles over
X. The resulting short exact sequence of vector bundles, like any other short exact
sequence of vector bundles, splits as a direct sum, and the conclusion follows.

1. THE DEFINITION OF K-THEORY
205
For nondegenerately based compact spaces X, there is a natural isomorphism
ÀúK(X) ‚àº= [X, BU √ó Z].
Proof. When X is connected, the Ô¨Årst isomorphism sends [Œæ]‚àíq to (f, n‚àíq),
where Œæ is an n-plane bundle with classifying map f : X ‚àí‚ÜíBU(n) ‚äÇBU. The
isomorphism for non-connected spaces follows since both functors send disjoint
unions to Cartesian products. The second isomorphism follows from the Ô¨Årst since
d : K(X) ‚àí‚ÜíZ can be identiÔ¨Åed with the map [X+, BU √ó Z] ‚àí‚Üí[S0, BU √ó Z]
induced by the coÔ¨Åbration S0 ‚àí‚ÜíX+, and the latter has kernel [X, BU √ó Z] since
X+/S0 = X.
‚ñ°
For general, non-compact, spaces X, it is best to deÔ¨Åne K-theory to mean
represented K-theory. Here we implicitly apply CW approximation, or else use the
deÔ¨Ånition in the following form.
Definition. For a space X of the homotopy type of a CW complex, deÔ¨Åne
K(X) = [X+, BU √ó Z].
For a nondegenerately based space of the homotopy type of a CW complex, deÔ¨Åne
ÀúK(X) = [X, BU √ó Z].
When X is compact, we know that K(X) is a ring. It is natural to expect this
to remain true for general X. That this is the case is a direct consequence of the
following result, which the reader should regard as an aside.
Proposition. The space BU √ó Z is a ring space up to homotopy. That is,
there are additive and multiplicative H-space structures on BU √ó Z such that the
associativity, commutativity, and distributivity diagrams required of a ring commute
up to homotopy.
Indications of proof. By passage to colimits over m and n, the maps pm,n :
BU(m) √ó BU(n) ‚àí‚ÜíBU(m + n) induce an ‚Äúaddition‚Äù ‚äï: BU √ó BU ‚àí‚ÜíBU. In
fact, we can deÔ¨Åne BU in terms of planes in any copy of C‚àû, and the explicit maps
pm,n of Chapter 23 ¬ß2 pass to colimits to give
G‚àû(C‚àû) √ó G‚àû(C‚àû) ‚àí‚ÜíG‚àû(C‚àû‚äïC‚àû);
use of an isomorphism C‚àû‚äïC‚àû‚àº= C‚àûgives the required map ‚äï, which is well
deÔ¨Åned, associative, and commutative up to homotopy; the zero-dimensional plane
provides a convenient basepoint 0 with which to check that we have a zero element
up to homotopy. Using ordinary addition on Z, we obtain the additive H-space
structure on BU √ó Z. Tensor products of universal bundles give rise to classifying
maps qm,n : BU(m) √ó BU(n) ‚àí‚ÜíBU(mn).
These do not pass to colimits so
readily, since one must take into account the bilinearity of the tensor product, for
example the relation (Œ≥m ‚äïŒµ)‚äóŒ≥n ‚àº= (Œ≥m ‚äóŒ≥n)‚äïŒ≥n, and we merely aÔ¨Érm that, by
fairly elaborate arguments, one can pass to colimits to obtain a product on BU √óZ.
It actually factors through the smash product with respect to the basepoint 0, since
that acts as zero for the tensor product, and it restricts to an H-space structure on
BO √ó {1} with basepoint (0, 1).
‚ñ°
The study of ring spaces such as this is a relatively new, and quite deep, part
of algebraic topology. However, the reader should feel reasonably comfortable with
the additive H-space structure on BU.

2. The Bott periodicity theorem
There are various ways to state, and various ways to prove, this basic result.
We describe several versions and implications. One starting point is the following
calculation. We have a canonical line bundle Œ≥2
1 over S2 ‚àº= CP 1; its points are pairs
(L, x), where L is a line in C2 and x is a point on that line. We let H = Hom(Œ≥2
1, Œµ)
denote its dual.
Theorem. K(S2) is generated as a ring by [H] subject to the single relation
([H] ‚àí1)2 = 0. Therefore, as Abelian groups, K(S2) is free on the basis {1, [H]}
and ÀúK(S2) is free on the basis {1 ‚àí[H]}.
Indication of proof. We think of S2 as the one-point compactiÔ¨Åcation of C
decomposed as the union of the unit disk D and the complement D‚Ä≤ of the interior
of D, so that D ‚à©D‚Ä≤ = S1.
Any n-plane bundle over S2 restricts to a trivial
bundle over D and D‚Ä≤, and these trivial bundles restrict to the same bundle over
S1. Conversely, an isomorphism f from the trivial bundle over S1 to itself gives a
way to glue together the trivial bundles over D and D‚Ä≤ to reconstruct a bundle over
S2. Say that two such ‚Äúclutching functions‚Äù f are equivalent if the bundles they
give rise to are equivalent. A careful analysis of the form of the possible clutching
functions f leads to a canonical example in each equivalence class and thus to the
required calculation.
‚ñ°
For any pair of spaces X and Y , we have a K¬®unneth-type ring homomorphism
Œ± : K(X) ‚äóK(Y ) ‚àí‚ÜíK(X √ó Y )
speciÔ¨Åed by Œ±(x ‚äóy) = œÄ‚àó

Theorem (Bott periodicity). For compact spaces X,
Œ± : K(X) ‚äóK(S2) ‚àí‚ÜíK(X √ó S2)
is an isomorphism.
Indication of proof. The restrictions to X √óD and X √óD‚Ä≤ of a bundle over
X√óS2 are equivalent to pullbacks of bundles over X, and their further restrictions to
S1 are equivalent. Conversely, bundles Œ∂ and Œæ over X together with an equivalence
f between the restrictions to X √ó S1 of the pullbacks of Œ∂ and Œæ to X √ó D and
X √ó D‚Ä≤ determine a bundle over X √ó S2. Again, a careful analysis, which is similar
to that in the special case when X = pt, of the equivalence classes of the possible
clutching data (Œ∂, f, Œæ) leads to the conclusion.
‚ñ°
The following useful observation applies to any representable functor, not just
K-theory.
Lemma. For nondegenerately based spaces X and Y , the projections of X √ó Y
on X and on Y and the quotient map X√óY ‚àí‚ÜíX‚àßY induce a natural isomorphism
ÀúK(X ‚àßY ) ‚äïÀúK(X) ‚äïÀúK(Y ) ‚àº= ÀúK(X √ó Y ),
and ÀúK(X ‚àßY ) is the kernel of the map ÀúK(X √ó Y ) ‚àí‚ÜíÀúK(X) ‚äïÀúK(Y ) induced by
the inclusions of X and Y in X √ó Y .
Proof. The inclusion X ‚à®Y ‚àí‚ÜíX √ó Y is a coÔ¨Åbration with quotient X ‚àßY ,
and X and Y are retracts of X √ó Y via the inclusions and projections.

3. The splitting principle and the Thom isomorphism
Returning to our bundle theoretic construction of K-theory, with X compact,
we describe brieÔ¨Çy some important generalizations of the Bott periodicity theorem.
The reader should recall the Thom isomorphism theorem in ordinary cohomology
from Chapter 23 ¬ß5.
We let Œæ : E ‚àí‚ÜíX be an n-plane bundle over X, Ô¨Åxed
throughout this section.
(We shall use the letters E and Œæ more or less inter-
changeably.) Results for general vector bundles over non-connected spaces X can
be deduced by applying the results to follow to one component of X at a time.
Definition. Let E0 be the zero section of E. DeÔ¨Åne the projective bundle
œÄ : P(E) ‚àí‚ÜíX by letting the non-zero complex numbers act on E ‚àíE0 by scalar
multiplication on Ô¨Åbers and taking the orbit space under this action. Equivalently,
the Ô¨Åber œÄ‚àí1(x) ‚äÇP(E) is the complex projective space of lines through the origin
in the Ô¨Åber Œæ‚àí1(x) ‚äÇE. DeÔ¨Åne the canonical line bundle L(E) over P(E) to be the
subbundle of the pullback œÄ‚àóE of Œæ along œÄ whose points are the pairs consisting
of a line in a Ô¨Åber of E and a point on that line. Let Q(E) be the quotient bundle
œÄ‚àóE/L(E) and let H(E) denote the dual of L(E).
Observe that P(Œµ2) = X√óCP 1 is the trivial bundle over X with Ô¨Åber CP 1 ‚àº= S2.
The Ô¨Årst version of Bott periodicity generalizes, with essentially the same proof by
analysis of clutching data, to the following version. Regard K(P(E)) as a K(X)-
algebra via œÄ‚àó: K(X) ‚àí‚ÜíK(P(E)).
Theorem (Bott periodicity). Let L be a line bundle over X and let H =
H(L ‚äïŒµ). Then the K(X)-algebra K(P(L ‚äïŒµ)) is generated by the single element
[H] subject to the single relation ([H] ‚àí1)([L][H] ‚àí1) = 0.
There is a further generalization to arbitrary bundles E. To place it in context,
we shall Ô¨Årst explain a cohomological analogue that expresses a diÔ¨Äerent approach

Theorem (Splitting principle). There is a compact space F(E) and a map
p : F(E) ‚àí‚ÜíX such that p‚àóE is a sum of line bundles over F(E) and both

monomorphisms on cohomology and K-theory.

Sketch proof. Suppose Ô¨Årst that E is the sum of n line bundles. Using the
fact that if E is an n-plane bundle and L is a line bundle, then P(E) is canonically

Projective bundles are closely related to Thom spaces. The inclusion of vector
bundles Œæ ‚äÇŒæ ‚äïŒµ induces an inclusion of projective bundles P(E) ‚äÇP(E ‚äïŒµ).
We give E a Hermitian metric and regard the Thom space TŒæ as the quotient
D(E)/S(E) of the unit disk bundle by the unit sphere bundle. The total space of Œµ
is X √óC and we write 1x = (x, 1). DeÔ¨Åne a map Œ∑ : D(E) ‚àí‚ÜíP(E ‚äïŒµ) by sending

maps D(E) ‚àíS(E) homeomorphically onto P(E ‚äïŒµ) ‚àíP(E) and maps S(E) onto
P(E) by the evident Hopf map. Therefore Œ∑ induces a homeomorphism
T(Œæ) ‚àº= D(E)/S(E) ‚àº= P(E ‚äïŒµ)/P(E).
Just as in ordinary cohomology, the Thom diagonal gives rise to a product
K(X) ‚äóÀúK(TŒæ) ‚àí‚ÜíÀúK(TŒæ).

5. The Adams operations
There are natural operations in K-theory, called the Adams operations, that
are somewhat analogous to the Steenrod operations in mod 2 cohomology. In fact,
the analogy can be given content by establishing a precise relationship between the
Adams and Steenrod operations, but we will not go into that here.
Theorem. For each non-zero integer k, there is a natural homomorphism of
rings œàk : K(X) ‚àí‚ÜíK(X). These operations satisfy the following properties.

6. The Hopf invariant one problem and its applications
We give one of the most beautiful and impressive illustrations of the philosophy
described in the Ô¨Årst chapter. We deÔ¨Åne a numerical invariant, called the ‚ÄúHopf
invariant,‚Äù of maps f : S2n‚àí1 ‚àí‚ÜíSn and show that it can only rarely take the
value one. We then indicate several problems whose solution can be reduced to
the question of when such maps f take the value one. Adams‚Äô original solution to
the Hopf invariant one problem used secondary cohomology operations in ordinary
cohomology and was a critical starting point of modern algebraic topology. The
later realization that a problem that required secondary operations in ordinary
cohomology could be solved much more simply using primary operations in K-
theory had a profound impact on the further development of the subject.
Take cohomology with integer coeÔ¨Écients unless otherwise speciÔ¨Åed.
Definition. Let X be the coÔ¨Åber of a based map f : S2n‚àí1 ‚àí‚ÜíSn, where
n ‚â•2. Then X is a CW complex with a single vertex, a single n-cell i, and a single
2n-cell j. The diÔ¨Äerential in the cellular chain complex of X is zero for obvious
dimensional reasons, hence ÀúH‚àó(X) is free Abelian on generators x = [i] and y = [j].
DeÔ¨Åne an integer h(f), the Hopf invariant of f, by x2 = h(f)y. We usually regard
h(f) as deÔ¨Åned only up to sign (thus ignoring problems of orientations of cells).
Note that h(f) depends only on the homotopy class of f.
If n is odd, then 2x2 = 0 and thus x2 = 0. We assume from now on that n
is even. Although not essential to the main point of this section, we record the
following basic properties of the Hopf invariant.
Proposition. The Hopf invariant enjoys the following properties.
(1) If g : S2n‚àí1 ‚àí‚ÜíS2n‚àí1 has degree d, then h(f ‚ó¶g) = dh(f).
(2) If e : Sn ‚àí‚ÜíSn has degree d, then h(e ‚ó¶f) = d2h(f).
(3) The Hopf invariant deÔ¨Ånes a homomorphism œÄ2n‚àí1(Sn) ‚àí‚ÜíZ.
(4) There is a map f : S2n‚àí1 ‚àí‚ÜíSn such that h(f) = 2.
Proof. We leave the Ô¨Årst three statements to the reader. For property 4, let
œÄ : Dn ‚àí‚ÜíDn/Sn‚àí1 ‚àº= Sn be the quotient map and deÔ¨Åne
f : S2n‚àí1 ‚àº= (Dn √ó Sn‚àí1) ‚à™(Sn‚àí1 √ó Dn) ‚àí‚ÜíSn
by f(x, y) = œÄ(x) and f(y, x) = œÄ(x) for x ‚ààDn and y ‚ààSn‚àí1. We leave it to the
reader to verify that h(f) = 2.

Theorem. If Sn is parallelizable, then n = 0, 1, 3, or 7.

so that its tangent bundle œÑ is trivial. We will show that Sn is an H-space. DeÔ¨Åne

CHAPTER 25
An introduction to cobordism
Cobordism theories were introduced shortly after K-theory, and their use per-
vades modern algebraic topology. We shall describe the cobordism of smooth closed
manifolds, but this is in fact a particularly elementary example. Other examples
include smooth closed manifolds with extra structure on their stable normal bun-
dles: orientation, complex structure, Spin structure, or symplectic structure for
example. All of these except the symplectic case have been computed completely.
The complex case is particularly important since complex cobordism and theories
constructed from it have been of central importance in algebraic topology for the
last few decades, quite apart from their geometric origins in the classiÔ¨Åcation of
manifolds. The area is pervaded by insights from algebraic topology that are quite
mysterious geometrically. For example, the complex cobordism groups turn out to
be concentrated in even degrees: every smooth closed manifold of odd dimension
with a complex structure on its stable normal bundle is the boundary of a compact
manifold (with compatible bundle information). However, there is no geometric un-
derstanding of why this should be the case. The analogue with ‚Äúcomplex‚Äù replaced
by ‚Äúsymplectic‚Äù is false.
1. The cobordism groups of smooth closed manifolds
We consider the problem of classifying smooth closed n-manifolds M. One‚Äôs
Ô¨Årst thought is to try to classify them up to diÔ¨Äeomorphism, but that problem
is in principle unsolvable. Thom‚Äôs discovery that one can classify such manifolds
up to the weaker equivalence relation of ‚Äúcobordism‚Äù is one of the most beautiful
advances of twentieth century mathematics. We say that two smooth closed n-
manifolds M and N are cobordant if there is a smooth compact manifold W whose
boundary is the disjoint union of M and N, ‚àÇW = M ‚®øN. We write Nn for the
set of cobordism classes of smooth closed n-manifolds. It is convenient to allow the
empty set ‚àÖas an n-manifold for every n. Disjoint union gives an addition on the
set Nn. This operation is clearly associative and commutative and it has ‚àÖas a
zero element. Since
‚àÇ(M √ó I) = M ‚®øM,
M ‚®øM is cobordant to ‚àÖ. Thus M = ‚àíM and Nn is a vector space over Z2.
Cartesian product of manifolds deÔ¨Ånes a multiplication Nm √ó Nn ‚àí‚ÜíNm+n. This
operation is bilinear, associative, and commutative, and the zero dimensional man-
ifold with a single point provides an identity element. We conclude that N‚àóis a
graded Z2-algebra.
Theorem (Thom). N‚àóis a polynomial algebra over Z2 on generators ui of
dimension i for i > 1 and not of the form 2r ‚àí1.
219

220
AN INTRODUCTION TO COBORDISM
As already stated in our discussion of Stiefel-Whitney numbers, it follows from
the proof of the theorem that a manifold is a boundary if and only if its normal
Stiefel-Whitney numbers are zero. We can restate this as follows.
Theorem. Two smooth closed n-manifolds are cobordant if and only if their
normal Stiefel-Whitney numbers, or equivalently their tangential Stiefel-Whitney
numbers, are equal.
Explicit generators ui are known.
Write [M] for the cobordism class of a
manifold M. Then we can take u2i = [RP 2i]. We have seen that the Stiefel-Whitney
numbers of RP 2i‚àí1 are zero, so we need diÔ¨Äerent generators in odd dimensions. For
m < n, deÔ¨Åne Hn,m to be the hypersurface in RP n √óRP m consisting of those pairs
([x0, . . ., xn], [y0, . . ., ym]) such that x0y0 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ + xmym = 0; here (x0, . . ., xn) ‚ààSn
and [x0, . . ., xn] denotes its image in RP n. We may write an odd number i not of
the form 2r ‚àí1 in the form i = 2p(2q + 1) ‚àí1 = 2p+1q + 2p ‚àí1, where p ‚â•1 and
q ‚â•1. Then we can take ui = [H2p+1q,2p].
The strategy for the proof of Thom‚Äôs theorem is to describe Nn as a homotopy
group of a certain Thom space. The homotopy group is a stable one, and it turns
out to be computable by the methods of generalized homology theory.

3. Prespectra and the algebra H‚àó(TO; Z2)

4. The Steenrod algebra and its coaction on H‚àó(TO)
Since the Steenrod operations are stable and natural, they pass to limits to
deÔ¨Åne natural operations Sqi : Hn(T) ‚àí‚ÜíHn+i(T) for i ‚â•0 and prespectra T.
Here Sq0 = id, but it is not true that Sqi(x) = 0 for i > deg x. For example, we
have the ‚Äústable Thom class‚Äù Œ¶(1) = ¬µ ‚ààH0(TO), and it is immediate from the
deÔ¨Ånition of the Stiefel-Whitney classes that Œ¶(wi) = Sqi(¬µ). Of course, Sqi(1) = 0
for i > 0, so that Œ¶ does not commute with Steenrod operations. The homology
and cohomology of TO are built up from œÄ‚àó(TO) and Steenrod operations. We
need to make this statement algebraically precise to determine œÄ‚àó(TO), and we
need to assemble the Steenrod operations into an algebra to do this.
Definition. The mod 2 Steenrod algebra A is the quotient of the free asso-
ciative Z2-algebra generated by elements Sqi, i ‚â•1, by the ideal generated by the
Adem relations (which are stated in Chapter 22 ¬ß5).
The following lemmas should be clear.
Lemma. For spaces X, H‚àó(X) has a natural A-module structure.
Lemma. For prespectra T, H‚àó(T) has a natural A-module structure.
The elements of A are stable mod 2 cohomology operations, and our description
of the cohomology of K(Z2, q)s in Chapter 22 ¬ß5 implies that A is in fact the
algebra of all stable mod 2 cohomology operations, with multiplication given by
composition. Passage to limits over q leads to the following lemma. Alternatively,
with the more formal general deÔ¨Ånitions of the next section, it will become yet
another application of the Yoneda lemma. Recall that HZ2 denotes the Eilenberg-
Mac Lane ‚Ñ¶-prespectrum {K(Z2, q)}.
Lemma. As a vector space, A is isomorphic to H‚àó(HZ2).
We shall see how to describe the composition in A homotopically in the next
section. What is more important at the moment is that the lemma allows us to
read oÔ¨Äa basis for A.
Theorem. A has a basis consisting of the operations SqI = Sqi1 ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ Sqij, where
I runs over the sequences {i1, . . ., ij} of positive integers such that ir ‚â•2ir+1 for
1 ‚â§r < j.
What is still more important to us is that A not only has the composition
product A ‚äóA ‚àí‚ÜíA, it also has a coproduct œà : A ‚àí‚ÜíA ‚äóA. Giving A ‚äóA
its natural structure as an algebra, œà is the unique map of algebras speciÔ¨Åed on
generators by œà(Sqk) = P
i+j=k Sqi ‚äóSqj. The fact that œà is a well deÔ¨Åned map
of algebras is a formal consequence of the Cartan formula. Algebraic structures like
this, with compatible products and coproducts, are called ‚ÄúHopf algebras.‚Äù
We write A‚àófor the vector space dual of A, and we give it the dual basis to the
basis just speciÔ¨Åed on A. While A‚àóis again a Hopf algebra, we are only interested
in its algebra structure at the moment.
In contrast with A, the algebra A‚àóis
commutative, as is apparent from the form of the coproduct on the generators of
A. Recall that HZ2 is an associative and commutative ring prespectrum, so that
H‚àó(HZ2) is a commutative Z2-algebra. The deÔ¨Ånition of the product on HZ2 (in
Chapter 22 ¬ß3) and the Cartan formula directly imply the following observation.
Lemma. A‚àóis isomorphic as an algebra to H‚àó(HZ2).

6. Spectra and the computation of œÄ‚àó(TO) = œÄ‚àó(MO)
We must still prove that h : œÄ‚àó(TO) ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(TO) is a monomorphism and that
g‚ó¶h maps œÄ‚àó(TO) isomorphically onto N‚àó. Write N for the dual vector space of N‚àó.
(Of course, N is a coalgebra, but that is not important for this part of our work.)
Remember that the Steenrod algebra A is dual to A‚àóand that A ‚àº= H‚àó(HZ2).
The dual of g : H‚àó(TO) ‚àí‚ÜíA‚àó‚äóN‚àóis an isomorphism of A-modules (and of
coalgebras) g‚àó: A ‚äóN ‚àí‚ÜíH‚àó(TO). Thus, if we choose a basis {yi} for N, where
deg yi = ni say, then H‚àó(TO) is the free graded A-module on the basis {yi}.
At this point, we engage in a conceptual thought exercise. We think of prespec-
tra as ‚Äústable objects‚Äù that have associated homotopy, homology, and cohomology
groups.
Imagine that we have a good category of stable objects, analogous to
the category of based spaces, that is equipped with all of the constructions that
we have on based spaces: wedges (= coproducts), colimits, products, limits, sus-
pensions, loops, homotopies, coÔ¨Åber sequences, Ô¨Åber sequences, smash products,
function objects, and so forth. Let us call the stable objects in our imagined cat-
egory ‚Äúspectra‚Äù and call the category of such objects S . We have in mind an
analogy with the notions of presheaf and sheaf.
Whatever spectra are, there must be a way of constructing a spectrum from
a prespectrum without changing its homotopy, homology, and cohomology groups.
In turn, a based space X determines the prespectrum Œ£‚àûX = {Œ£nX}. The homol-
ogy and cohomology groups of Œ£‚àûX are the (reduced) homology and cohomology
groups of X; the homotopy groups of Œ£‚àûX are the stable homotopy groups of X.
Because homotopy groups, homology groups, and cohomology groups on based
spaces satisfy the weak equivalence axiom, the real domain of deÔ¨Ånition of these
invariants is the category ¬ØhT that is obtained from the homotopy category hT
of based spaces by adjoining inverses to the weak equivalences. This category is
equivalent to the homotopy category hC of based CW complexes. Explicitly, the
morphisms from X to Y in ¬ØhT can be deÔ¨Åned to be the based homotopy classes
of maps ŒìX ‚àí‚ÜíŒìY , where ŒìX and ŒìY are CW approximations of X and Y .
Composition is deÔ¨Åned in the evident way.

6. SPECTRA AND THE COMPUTATION OF œÄ‚àó(T O) = œÄ‚àó(MO)
231
Continuing our thought exercise, we can form the homotopy category hS of
spectra and can deÔ¨Åne homotopy groups in terms of homotopy classes of maps from
sphere spectra to spectra. ReÔ¨Çection on the periodic nature of K-theory suggests
that we should deÔ¨Åne sphere spectra of negative dimension and deÔ¨Åne homotopy
groups œÄq(X) for all integers q. We say that a map of spectra is a weak equivalence if
it induces an isomorphism on homotopy groups. We can form the ‚Äústable category‚Äù
¬ØhS from hS exactly as we formed the category ¬ØhT from hT . That is, we develop
a theory of CW spectra using sphere spectra as the domains of attaching maps.
The Whitehead and cellular approximation theorems hold, and every spectrum X
admits a CW approximation ŒìX ‚àí‚ÜíX. We deÔ¨Åne the set [X, Y ] of morphisms
X ‚àí‚ÜíY in ¬ØhS to be the set of homotopy classes of maps ŒìX ‚àí‚ÜíŒìY . This is
a stable category in the sense that the functor Œ£ : ¬ØhS ‚àí‚Üí¬ØhS is an equivalence
of categories. More explicitly, the natural maps X ‚àí‚Üí‚Ñ¶Œ£X and Œ£‚Ñ¶X ‚àí‚ÜíX are
isomorphisms in ¬ØhS .
In particular, up to isomorphism, every object in the category ¬ØhS is a sus-
pension, hence a double suspension. This implies that each [X, Y ] is an Abelian
group and composition is bilinear. Moreover, for any map f : X ‚àí‚ÜíY , the canon-
ical map Ff ‚àí‚Üí‚Ñ¶Cf and its adjoint Œ£Ff ‚àí‚ÜíCf (see Chapter 8 ¬ß7) are also
isomorphisms in ¬ØhS , so that coÔ¨Åber sequences and Ô¨Åber sequences are equivalent.
Therefore coÔ¨Åber sequences give rise to long exact sequences of homotopy groups.
The homotopy groups of wedges and products of spectra are given by
œÄ‚àó(W
i Xi) = P
i œÄ‚àó(Xi) and œÄ‚àó(Q
i Xi) = Q
i œÄ‚àó(Xi).
Therefore, if only Ô¨Ånitely many œÄq(Xi) are non-zero for each q, then the natural
map W

7. An introduction to the stable category
To give content to the argument just sketched, we should construct a good cat-
egory of spectra. In fact, no such category was available when Thom Ô¨Årst proved his
theorem in 1960. With motivation from the introduction of K-theory and cobor-
dism, a good stable category was constructed by Boardman (unpublished) around
1964 and an exposition of his category was given by Adams soon after. However,
these early constructions were far more primitive than our outline suggests. While
they gave a satisfactory stable category, the underlying category of spectra did not
have products, limits, and function objects, and its smash product was not asso-
ciative, commutative, or unital. In fact, a fully satisfactory category of spectra was
not constructed until 1995.
We give a few deÔ¨Ånitions to indicate what is involved.
Definition. A spectrum E is a prespectrum such that the adjoints ÀúœÉ : En ‚àí‚Üí
‚Ñ¶En+1 of the structure maps œÉ : Œ£En ‚àí‚ÜíEn+1 are homeomorphisms. A map
f : T ‚àí‚ÜíT ‚Ä≤ of prespectra is a sequence of maps fn : Tn ‚àí‚ÜíT ‚Ä≤
n such that
œÉ‚Ä≤
n ‚ó¶Œ£fn = fn+1 ‚ó¶œÉn for all n. A map f : E ‚àí‚ÜíE‚Ä≤ of spectra is a map between E
and E‚Ä≤ regarded as prespectra.
We have a forgetful functor from the category S of spectra to the category P
of prespectra. It has a left adjoint L : P ‚àí‚ÜíS . In P, we deÔ¨Åne wedges, colimits,
products, and limits spacewise. For example, (T ‚à®T ‚Ä≤)n = Tn ‚à®T ‚Ä≤
n, with the evident
structure maps. We deÔ¨Åne wedges and colimits of spectra by Ô¨Årst performing the
construction on the prespectrum level and then applying the functor L. If we start
with spectra and construct products or limits spacewise, then the result is again
a spectrum; that is, limits of spectra are the limits of their underlying prespectra.
Thus the category S is complete and cocomplete.
Similarly, we deÔ¨Åne the smash product T ‚àßX and function prespectrum F(X, T)
of a based space X and a prespectrum T spacewise. For a spectrum E, we de-
Ô¨Åne E ‚àßX by applying L to the prespectrum level construction; the prespectrum
F(X, E) is already a spectrum. We now have cylinders E ‚àßI+ and thus can deÔ¨Åne
homotopies between maps of spectra. Similarly we have cones CE = E ‚àßI (where
I has basepoint 1), suspensions Œ£E = E ‚àßS1, path spectra F(I, E) (where I has

7. AN INTRODUCTION TO THE STABLE CATEGORY

Ô¨Åber sequences proceeds exactly as for based spaces.
The left adjoint L can easily be described explicitly on those prespectra T
whose adjoint structure maps ÀúœÉn : Tn ‚àí‚Üí‚Ñ¶Tn+1 are inclusions: we deÔ¨Åne (LT)n

We have an evident map of prespectra Œª : T ‚àí‚ÜíLT, and a comparison of
colimits shows (by a coÔ¨Ånality argument) that Œª induces isomorphisms on homo-
topy and homology groups. The essential point is that homotopy and homology
commute with colimits. It is not true that cohomology converts colimits to limits
in general, because of lim1 error terms, and this is one reason that our deÔ¨Ånition of
the cohomology of prespectra via limits is inappropriate except under restrictions
that guarantee the vanishing of lim1 terms. Observe that there is no problem in
the case of ‚Ñ¶-prespectra, for which Œª is a spacewise weak equivalence.
For a based space X, we deÔ¨Åne the suspension spectrum Œ£‚àûX by applying L
to the suspension prespectrum Œ£‚àûX = {Œ£nX}. The inclusion condition is satisÔ¨Åed
in this case. We deÔ¨Åne QX = ‚à™‚Ñ¶qŒ£qX, and we Ô¨Ånd that the nth space of Œ£‚àûX is
QŒ£nX. It should be apparent that the homotopy groups of the space QX are the
stable homotopy groups of X.
The adjoint structure maps of the Thom prespectrum TO are also inclusions,
and our mythical object is MO = LTO.
In general, for a prespectrum T, we can apply an iterated mapping cylinder con-
struction to deÔ¨Åne a spacewise equivalent prespectrum KT whose adjoint structure
maps are inclusions. The prespectrum level homotopy, homology, and cohomology
groups of KT are isomorphic to those of T. Thus, if we have a prespectrum T whose
invariants we are interested in, such as an Eilenberg-Mac Lane ‚Ñ¶-prespectrum or
the K-theory ‚Ñ¶-prespectrum, then we can construct a spectrum LKT that has the
same invariants.
For a based space X and q ‚â•0, we construct a prespectrum Œ£‚àû
q X whose nth
space is a point for n < q and is Œ£n‚àíqX for n ‚â•q; its structure maps for n ‚â•q are
identity maps. We continue to write Œ£‚àû
q X for the spectrum obtained by applying
L to this prespectrum. We then deÔ¨Åne sphere spectra Sq for all integers q by letting
Sq = Œ£‚àûSq for q ‚â•0 and S‚àíq = Œ£‚àû
q S0 for q > 0. The deÔ¨Ånition is appropriate
since Œ£Sq ‚àº= Sq+1 for all integers q. We can now deÔ¨Åne homotopy groups in the
obvious way. For example, the homotopy groups of the K-theory spectrum are Z
for every even integer and zero for every odd integer.
From here, we can go on to deÔ¨Åne CW spectra in very much the same way
that we deÔ¨Åned CW complexes, and we can Ô¨Åll in the rest of the outline in the
previous section. The real work involves the smash product of spectra, but this
does not belong in our rapid course. While there is a good deal of foundational
work involved, there is also considerable payoÔ¨Äin explicit concrete calculations, as
the computation of œÄ‚àó(MO) well illustrates.
With the hope that this glimpse into the world of stable homotopy theory has
whetted the reader‚Äôs appetite for more, we will end at this starting point.


Suggestions for further reading
Rather than attempt a complete bibliography, I will give a number of basic
references. I will begin with historical references and textbooks. I will then give
references for speciÔ¨Åc topics, more or less in the order in which topics appear in
the text. Where material has been collected in one or another book, I have often
referred to such books rather than to original articles. However, the importance
and quality of exposition of some of the original sources often make them still to be
preferred today. The subject in its earlier days was blessed with some of the Ô¨Ånest
expositors of mathematics, for example Steenrod, Serre, Milnor, and Adams. Some
of the references are intended to give historical perspective, some are classical papers
in the subject, some are follow-ups to material in the text, and some give an idea
of the current state of the subject. In fact, many major parts of algebraic topology
are nowhere mentioned in any of the existing textbooks, although several were well
established by the mid-1970s. I will indicate particularly accessible references for
some of them; the reader can Ô¨Ånd more of the original references in the sources
given.
1. A classic book and historical references
The axioms for homology and cohomology theories were set out in the classic:
S. Eilenberg and N. Steenrod. Foundations of algebraic topology. Princeton Uni-
versity Press. 1952.
I believe the only historical monograph on the subject is:
J. Dieudonn¬¥e. A history of algebraic and diÔ¨Äerential topology, 1900‚Äì1960. Birk-
h¬®auser. 1989.
A large collection of historical essays will appear soon:
I.M. James, editor. The history of topology. Elsevier Science. To appear.
Among the contributions, I will advertise one of my own, available on the web:
J.P. May. Stable algebraic topology, 1945‚Äì1966. http://hopf.math.purdue.edu

that the most recent editions or versions are cited). I have included only those texts
that I have looked at myself, that are at least at the level of the more elementary
chapters here, and that oÔ¨Äer signiÔ¨Åcant individuality of treatment. There are many
other textbooks in algebraic topology.

P.J. Hilton and S. Wylie. Homology theory. Cambridge University Press. 1960.

18. Localization and completion; rational homotopy theory
Since the early 1970s, it has been standard practice in algebraic topology to

Ann. Sci. Ecole Norm. Sup. (4)26(1993), 309‚Äì360.

D. Sullivan. InÔ¨Ånitesimal computations in topology. Publ. Math. IHES 47(1978),

a variety of theoretical and calculational developments and applications in this area:
J.P. May. The geometry of iterated loop spaces. Lecture Notes in Mathematics
Vol. 271. Springer-Verlag. 1972.
J.M. Boardman and R.M. Vogt. Homotopy invariant algebraic structures on topo-
logical spaces. Lecture Notes in Mathematics Vol. 347. Springer-Verlag. 1973.

20. Complex cobordism and stable homotopy theory
Adams‚Äô book cited in ¬ß16 gives a spectral sequence for the computation of
stable homotopy groups in terms of generalized cohomology theories.
Starting
from complex cobordism and related theories, its use has been central to two waves
of major developments in stable homotopy theory. A good exposition for the Ô¨Årst
wave:
D.C. Ravenel. Complex cobordism and stable homotopy groups of spheres. Aca-
demic Press. 1986.
The essential original paper and a very nice survey article on the second wave:
E. Devinatz, M.J. Hopkins, and J.H. Smith. Nilpotence and stable homotopy theory.
Annals of Math. 128(1988), 207‚Äì242.
M.J. Hopkins. Global methods in homotopy theory. In Proceedings of the 1985
LMS Symposium on homotopy theory, edited by J.D.S. Jones and E. Rees. London
Mathematical Society. 1987.
The cited Proceedings contain good introductory survey articles on several other
topics in algebraic topology. A larger scale exposition of the second wave is:
D.C. Ravenel. Nilpotence and periodicity in stable homotopy theory.
Annals of
Math. Studies No. 128. Princeton University Press. 1992.
21. Follow-ups to this book
There is a leap from the level of this introductory book to that of the most
recent work in the subject. One recent book that helps Ô¨Åll the gap is:
P. Selick. Introduction to homotopy theory. Fields Institute Monographs No.
9. American Mathematical Society. 1997.
There is a recent expository book for the reader who would like to jump right in
and see the current state of algebraic topology; although it focuses on equivariant
theory, it contains introductions and discussions of many non-equivariant topics:
J.P. May et al. Equivariant homotopy and cohomology theory. NSF-CBMS Re-
gional Conference Monograph. 1996.
For the reader of the last section of this book whose appetite has been whetted
for more stable homotopy theory, there is an expository article that motivates and
explains the properties that a satisfactory category of spectra should have:
J.P. May. Stable algebraic topology and stable topological algebra. Bulletin London
Math. Soc. 30(1998), 225‚Äì234.
The following monograph gives such a category, with many applications; more
readable accounts appear in the Handbook cited in ¬ß17 and in the book just cited:
